THE RED PAGE
Recommended
As Home Affairs are demanding more
space (and this page resembles the bed of
Procrustes in one respect), it is proposed to
take it out of Foreign Affairs, Hence-
forward, novels, etc., which are approved
but do not call for extended notice, will be
mentioned in a list at the head of this page.
A formal acknowledgment to publishers of
books about which nothing can be said is of
little value to them or anyone else. They
are advised to send only literature of general
interest.
Hope —R. Cunninghame Graham (Duckworth).
Short stories and sketches in a glowing and
distinguished style.
The Exception —Oliver Onions (Methuen). Onions
is a realist with a light touch and a quizzical
view of life. Those who admired Little
Devil Doubt (the rule amongst the elect) will
like The Exception.
Uncle Polperro—A. Oourlander (Dymock’s). An
amusing frivolity.
Let the Roof Fall In— Frank Danby (Lockley
Library). A strong, attractive novel with
some fine Irish and a ripping Jew ; scenes in
London and Siam ; humor and pathos.
The Return —Walter de la Mare (Arnold). De la
Mare, a poet of sorts, handles a weird theme
exceedingly well. The principal character is
invaded by a strange spirit, and loses his
original identity. The natural and super-
natural elements are developed side by side.
It belongs to the same class of fiction as
Algernon Blackwood’s work, and is finer than
The Glimpse or The Other Side.
The Hycena of Kallu —Louise Gerard (Dymock’s).
The author of The Golden Centipede gives
another version of the tragedy of the half-
caste, with the thrilling adventures and
mysterious gloom of West African settlements
thrown in.
Wine, Women and Roses
When rosebuds into chalices unroll
For love of wine Narcissus bears the bowl.
Ah ! happy he who learns the crimson lore,
And, wine’s own Sfifi, liberates his soul.
Of that old wine some vanished Sultan grew,
Give me, that I may paint life’s scenes anew.
01), make me heedless of the heedless world
That I may sing the world’s desire to you.
Gome, love and wine beside the river’s brink ;
In every cup some shallow care we’ll sink.
Life’s span is but the rose’s, ten dear days ;
Then chain the ten with laughter’s golden link.
O lovers, you whose happy hands enlace,
For whom Time’s wheel, forgotten, flies apace.
When my time eometh hail the endless round
That other Aprils may recall my face.
* * * *
The little volumes published by John
Murray in “The Wisdom of the East”
series have already introduced to English
readers much of the literature of Asia. We
have had “ The Master Singers of Japan,”
“The Lute of Jade” from China, a good
deal of the wisdom of India and some qjf the
poetry of Persia—“ The Rose Garden of
Sa’di,” translated by Oranmer-Byng, “The
Diwan of Abu’l-ala ” done into excellent
English verse by Henry Baerlin, who has
also provided an essay on Arabian poetry,
called “The Singing Caravan.” All that one
needs of Jami has been given elsewhere by
Fitzgerald, whose version of Omar Khayyam
has become part of English literature.
But the greatest of all Persian poets,
Hafiz, has not had the luck to meet a Fitz-
gerald, and in consequence he is little more
than a name to us. Professor Cowell, who
knew him better than most moderns, was not a
vei-se-maker, and Emerson translated only a
few verses (and these from a German trans-
lation) to illustrate his essay on Persian
poetry. LeGallienne, of course, does not
count.
The austere Emerson, who shot an arrow
of prophecy into the dark with regard to
“Omar Chiam,” had a fine appreciation of
the luxuriant genius of the prince of Persian
poets, and, mystic though he was, said he
did not wish “to strew sugar on bottled
spiders or try to make mystical divinity out
of the Song of Solomon, much less out of the
erotic and bacchanalian songs of Hafiz.” It
is related that, not long after the poet’s
death, his works were condemned as un-
orthodox. The Srifi, who tried the matter,
decided (“O wise judge!”)—“that every-
one was at liberty to use his own judgment
as to the meaning to be assigned to the poems
of Hafiz.”
You may, therefore, take the wine neat or
regard it as “ the love of God,” and women
as the real thing or angels, or whatever else
may please your fancy. Hafiz, at any rate,
was violently in love with a damsel named
Branch of Candy, and when his wife left
him in a temper his passionate verses in-
duced her return. Of her he said :
If the scent of her hair were to blow across my
dust when I have been dead a hundred years, my
mouldering bones would rise and come dancing
out of the tomb.
But then the imagery of Persian poets is
always slightly exaggerated. The same
gentle Hafiz wrote of the Shah, a minor
poet who had put his father’s eyes out:
May the ball of the heavens be forever in the
crook of thy polo stick, and the whole world be a
playing ground unto thee. The fame of thy
goodness has attained to the four quarters of the
earth ; may it be for all time a guardian unto
thee.
These and other interesting matters relating
to Hafiz may be found in The Ruhaixjdt of
Hafiz (Murray), sold for Is. net. It is the
first instalment of the complete poems, trans-
lated literally by Syed Abdul Majid, and
turned into English verse by L. Cranmer-
Byng. The specimen quatrains printed be-
low and above will show that, though far
short of Fitzgerald’s “ Omar,” it is a worthy
successor to Baerlin’s “ Abu’l-ala.”
* * * *
Whence did the tangles of your hair arise?
And the dreams that haunt the shadows of your
eyes?
Since none have shaken petals on your path,
Oh, whence the attar that around you lies?
The jasmine blooms in the shadow of your hair?
Lips beyond price, since Aden’s pearls lie there,
Like you, the soul is ever wine-inspired ;
The wine’s bright soul shines through a form as
fair.
Roselit, my tears like her twin roses show
My heart’s red blood through aching eyes doth
flow.
She asked me, seeking for a fair reply :
“Why do thine eyes like lakes in twilight
glow? ”
O great of soul ! How gladly would I give
All that I am to thee by whom 1 live!
If thou wouldst know the bitterness of hell,
Pour friendship’s water through an empty sieve.
A Hew inferno?
Hafiz naturally brought up memories of
Omar, and one quatrain of the Rubaiyrit was
floating in “ the hinterland of the brain ”
(see H. G. Wells) when we read The New
Inferno of Stephen Phillips, in which,
according to the advertisement, the poet
strikes his early form again.
It is an Inferno in nine cantos of blank
verse, wherein the poet:
Dreaming in starless night, it seemed that I,
Lifted in spirit arms, was outward borne,
Beyond the steadfast boundaries of the earth
And the invisible orb of the vast sun.
Then was I ’ware that one beside me was,
Whom I felt speaking rather than heard speak :
“ One whom thou lovest and who still loves thee
Despatched me to thy side,” the spirit said.
He is advised by the spirit to get rid of the
old idea “that, after bodily death, the
trembling spirit is haled before its Maker
and its J udge and, if its deeds were evil,
sentenced straight.” He meets the Corsican
alone ujfbn a frozen Alp
Roofed by the purple vault of his own mind,
And glassed upon a glacier of the soul.
Then Josephine arrives, after wandering “in
felicity of woe ” (Tom Moore had it “luxury
of woe”), and the icy brow grew soft and
flowers sprang up from the glacier at his feet.
The other souls they met are not of his-
toric importance. One “colorless, pale soul,”
like Tomlinson’s, could not get a place even
with the “ gentlemen, three on a grid.” One
who had been a Grand Inquisitor was writh-
ing in a raging fire made of “the flame
awaked by the still living fury of the dead.”
(That is very much like the Inquisitor’s own
idea of Hell. It would have been more fun
if they had fed the old chap with never-
ending spoonsful of ice cream.) Another
person had been standing motionless for
two centuries enjoying the torture of one
who had stolen his wife ori earth—and so on.
Altogether, Phillips’ Hell is a very small
Hell. The architecture is, on the whole,
good. The report that the poet hjis been in
bad health may account for a number of weak
lines and imagery borrowed from himself
and elsewhere.
At the end the spirit recapitulates the
chief incidents of the trip and says :
This much at least thou canst discern and tell:
That hate, on earth begotten, here is hate;
That vengeance there conceived consumes us
here;
That here the soul lives as on earth it lived.
That the discarding of the body of the Earth
Sends not to sudden pain or sudden joy,
But the loosed spirit the lone journey takes
Perhaps for seons to work out its fate.
That is to say, Phillips, in 350 stanzas, tells
what Fitz-Omar put into one :
I sent my soul through the Invisible
Some letter of that After-life to spell;
And by and by my soul return’d to me
And answer’d “ I myself am Heav’n and Hell.”
Dick Harris
Monodies: A Booh of Verse, printed in a
limited edition by Whitcombe and 'Tombs
Ltd. (M.L.), is evidently the work of a
young man, for most of the contents are
grey and sad. Yet from a writer who prints
his name as “Dick Harris” one wlould
expect more spirited verse than “ Crepus-
cule,” “Lavender,” “After Grief,” “La-
ment,” etc., in which faded threads from old
tapestries are woven into new patterns—
woven dexterously enough, as will be seen
in these pieces :
Life is one long regret
For summers fled—for beauty passed away.
What tears there are in songs that once were gay !
The sad heart, dreaming yet—
Sighs the one plaintive fret. . .
Life is one long regret
For yesterday.
The air seems stirred with a calling of fairy bells,
And chanting a tender, tremulous lullaby,
The wimpling creek through a tangle of bush-
wrack wells.
See, how there in the west.
Unstirred by the ghost of a breeze,
That little band of strong and stately trees
In meditation with a hushed delight,
Stands tall and dark against the day’s last light;
An etching of woven branches over the sky—
A wistfully luminous screen
Of rose intermingled with saffron
Fading to green.
There are few faults to be found with the
construction; but a temporarily defective
ear passed this line :
The joyous harbor, seen ’tween stem and leaf.
In “Singing Youth” the writer tells us
that—
When eighteen years had found me
And left me worn with time,
And wan years wrapped around me
Grey eld of frost and rime. . . .
But now, at two and twenty;
I sing a better song;
For I have years in plenty
To love and laugh along.
So there is hope of more cheerful work from
one who certainly knows how to manipulate
the materials of minor poetry. Dick Harris,
in this little book, looks like the beginnings
of another Victor Daley, and for such as he
there is always a welcome from poetry-
lovers in Australia.
The Last of the Mirandelays
Acknowledgment; should have been made
earlier of Mirandelays from P. 8., “Tahune,”
P- A. McC., “The Blot,” “Poi-Poi,” J.M.,
A.T.R. and A.H.
“Alpenstock” sends a variation of
O’Dowd’s form, in which the first stanza
advances, the 2nd, 4th, 6th, Bth and 10th
reverse—each holding on to the first stanza
by a line, and finally the first stanza stands
on its head. As this is pretty near the
limit in whirling verse, forms it may (in a
punctured state) fittingly close the busiriess.
Directions are added for re-inflation, if
required.
A QUANDARY.
Who of all these maidens is the maid for me ?
[Here follow the five lines in italic below.]
Proud and peerless Florence of one week in May.
Swayed she in the sunlight as we rode along,
Cold and soft her voice seemed, tremulous
her hand,
Pale and glowing Florence, did you under-
stand ?
Weak, pathetic Florence, and my Florence
strong,
Did you understand it—why I rode away ?
[Reverse last six lines.]
Gwyndoline, whose breathings hypnotise the air:
Floats about her magic like an element ;
Potent are her glances, potent to compel
Love that trembles greatly else to hide or tell;
All the east its wonder unto her has lent
Maid of inspiration, magic and despair.
[Reverse last six lines.]
Cynthia, adorable her movements and her hair ;
Grace itself encaptured in a woman’s frame ;
Beauty in thesunlight playing hide and seek
In the hair that’s wafted over neck and cheek ;
Sweet her subtle graces, Sweeter than her name,
Dearer than the dearest, fairer than the fair.
[Reverse last six lines.]
Eunice of the sea beach and the holiday.
Sweet those summer evenings on the old cart
track ;
Scent of full-blown flowers on the air around.
Grasses lush and seeding on the gracious
ground.
Oft we’ve wandered nowhere, and the long way
back
Pacing in the glamor of the starry ray.
[Reverse last six lines.]
Phyllis, my beloved, beautiful is she,
Classical her name is, classical her eyes
And her voice is gentle, gentle as the dove,
She must be beholden ere you know of love,
Complex is her nature, simple, pure and wise,
She fulfils the promise of the things to be.
[Reverse last six lines.]
[Reverse lines in italic.]
[Collapse.]
The Odd Volume
The latest number of this miscellany in
prose, verse and pictures is better than its
predecessors, especially in the verse depart-
ment. This and Printer's Pie are published
in aid of English book-trade charities. Both
are sold in large numbers every year, mainly
on the strength of their pictures and amusing
trifles by well-known writers.
Why not an Australian Odd Volume to
provide the nucleus of a fund for the benefit
of writers and artists who have fallen on
the Inky Way ? It is worth considering by
the guilds of the craft.
Mendelssohn’s South African Bibliography—a
eonpiete list of books, pamphlets, periodicals,
etc, relating to South Africa, with biographical
and critical notes —is about to be published in
London by Kegan, Paul and Co. It will be of
immense value to all students of South African
life aid literature. A similar Bibliography of
printer, matter relating to Australia is badly
needed. Maoriland was fortunate in having Dr.
Hocken, a book-lover with leisure, who completed
and published a Bibliography of literature relat-
ing to the Dominion just before he died. There
is a man aere—E. A. Petherick—who could do
the same w»rk for Australia. Some years ago he
printed pari of a Catalogue Raisonni of his own
collection of Australians—which has since been
tc the Commonwealth Government.
If E. A. Petlurick undertakes a complete Aus-
tralian Bibliography, it will be well done, and his
name will he bletsed by many people.
Most working journalists know the value of
Johns’ Notable Australians, and will welcome his
projected volume, in which all dead Australians
who did anything notable will be preserved in
amber.
Another book of reference urgently wanted is
Henniker Heaton’s Australian Dictionary of Dates,
revised and brought down from 1579 to the present
time. It requires a man with nous, and wquld
mean a lot of patient labor. It is a chance of ira-
mortality for some one.
* * * *
The second or third story entitled The Heart
of the Bush, is by Edith S. Grossman (G. Robert-
son and Co.), and tells how a rough and handsome
bushnian carries off a refined and beautiful girl to
live'in the Maoriland bush—after the wedding,
of course. Some chapters explain that he
neglected hjs Adelaide for his flocks and herds and
the Frozen Meat industry. In the last chapter
we learn “ How Dennis came back to Adelaide,
and of the joy they had, and how true love is
likened to Spring.”

of its class—the third-rate detective storj

stories wherein the sentiment and fidelitj are

Shadows of Old Paris , by G. Duval, illus-
trated by J. Gavin (Sydney Book CluLf A
very readable book. The disapp/aring
landmarks of old Paris are describe 1 pleas-
antly, with much gossip about the Historic
events associated with each place* The
great and Itbfehr doings, their queep—
mistresses, Villon, Moliere, Scarr jn, the
Marquise de Brinvilliers, the giants jf the
Revolution, Theresa who was Godclss of
Liberty, her lover Tallien, and a Ifist of
minor characters flit through these pages.
As is appropriate, the illustrations arfvague
and shadowy.
THE DIPSOMANIAC AND THE
BLUE HORSE.
One two-o’clock in the morning f dipso-
maniac walking in the country of dippmania
perceived a blue horse making faceslit him
through a hole in a wall. Fasciifted by
their hideousness and the magniticenjelectric
blue color of the horse, the dijisomaniac
glued his face to the hole and Ijlird the
colorsome equine talking to itself | a voice
like a creaking axle.
“Oh, Satan ! my best beloved njfeter,” it
groaned, “ why dost thou allow idy image,
emblazoned in glory upon whisky jttles, to
be spat upon by loiterers in tfjs bottle
cemetery. When the bottles are full of
golden juice, man calls me his gffeat blue
god, and faints with the ecstasy of my
unparalleled magnificence, but ” ——
“ Vain rascal ! ” exclaimed the dipso-)
maniac, able to contain himself no longer ;
“’tis not thy beauty which causes me to
faint, ’tis the rare juice in the bottle.”
Whereupon the blue horse flashed through
the hole in the wall and cantered round and*
round the man’s head in concentric circles of
vivid electric blue, and whistled in his ear.
“Splendid!” exclaimed the man when
the blue horse had finished kaleidoscoping.
“Thou art indeed a most accomplished and
well-favored horse, and if thou wilt but
consent to be mine, there is no racecourse
in the world but will acclaim us twain ‘the
supernatural duo;’” arid, taking a halter from;
his oilskin pocket, the man thrust a bunch
of Maori land spinach through the hole ini
the wall and waited for the blue horse to
become his. It was not to be expected,,
however, that a horse of such an excellent
complexion was to be beguiled by a ruse so:
transparent and obsolete; and, galloping
madly through the man’s brain, it was back
in Satan’s stable even before his head had
crashed upon the macadam.
Thus it is that the rarest specimens and
messages from eternity are perpetually being!
frightened away by the rude and clumsy.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Closed Gates.
The Gates of Paradise (the Persians say)
That shine beyond this narrow vale of tears,
But once unfold and leave an open way
In all the cycle of a thousand years.
The Gates of Torment (crieV^. later creed),
That man may purge him duly of his crime.
And wash away the stains of evil deed,
Stand open for his entry all the time.
In troubled mood comes then the modern sage,
And, torn in twain by dubious faiths of yore,
He turns his back on creeds of either age
And shuts the Rival Gates for evermore !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
The Men Whose Cake is Dough.
VICTORIA.
Their firstfruits all are perished,
' Their shewbread is dust;
The highest hope they cherished
Betrayed them to distrust ;
Once eager for the highroad.
With lame feet and slow,
They march by any by-road,
The men whose cake is dough.
In sunshine they departed,
With hearts that beat high ;
Tiie road whereon they started
Swept grandly to the sky ;
A gallant song they chanted
Of Fate’s overthrow.
Who now march dumb and daunted,
The men whose cake is dough.
Their failure cannot 'blind them
To pathways they planned ;
They know what lies behind them,
What royal rainbows spanned
The sky that bene above them
When youth was aglow,
And Heaven seemed to love them —
The men whose pake is dough.
The songs that they were cheered by—
A heart-stirring host —
The pinnacles they steered bv ;
The guiding stars they lost;
A woman’s charm of laughter,
A spell soft and low—
These phantoms follow after
The men whose cake is dough.
The psean of their inspiring
Is silent for them ;
Of earthly music tiring
They wait their requiem ;
They journey on grey-hearted
Till God bids them go.
And Heaven addeth leaven
To the cakes that were dough.
KETTLEDRUM.
Nuit Blanche.
From burdened clouds that blur the starlight’s
gleam '
The teardrops start;
I turn to seek the chamber of my dream
With hopeless heart.
Even as lightning stabs the tortured sky.
My heart is pierced by spearS <pf memory ;
Though kindly rain may sheath the lightning’s
spears, ' <
No peace is mine, who dare not pray for tears.
But here at least is shelter from the storm,
If I but win
White nights to watch beside the fancied form
That dwells within—
My soul’s remembrance of a hope divine,
Vivid and sweet and proud, but never mine.
My love were all unmeet her heart to stir,
Whose God dreamed girlhood, woke and fashioned
her.
Here sleeps Joyeuse, my rose upon her breast;
So light she lies,
Meseems I dare not gaze upon her, lest
She ope her eyes ;
As this dead rose, if sentient of the sun
Might crave its kisses, till their warmth had Won
Glory and beauty back, that none might guess
Its journey into night and nothingness.
By her deep eyes what spell might not be laid
Of flawless youth,
To mock the march of time that makes afraid
And prove it truth
That all the threats of all the years were vain,
While love lived on to end love’s golden reign ?
The dream died with its day—and dare I rue'
Departure of the dream who dreamed it through ?
Yet the faint echo of a distant bell
Sounding at eve,
As if to bid our day serene farewell,
Still makes believe
That peace is in the night it seems to call.
Were there no night how learn that stars must
fall?
What though the dusk be full of bitterness ?
I lived my day—l would not ask for.less.
Though mute the music, not in vain the quest
Of one whose eyes
Follow the homing songster to the nest,
Where singing dies;
So sang Joyeuse a song that seemed a prayer,
And, having sung, went wistfully to share
A sweeter silence. . . . Though the night be long,
Once it was day—and once I heard her song.
Victoria.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Thk Bullktin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
bid to the Manager. 'To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bullktin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 5, 1911.
THE INEFFICIENT FIRST LINE OF
DEFENCE.
A pew days ago the Australian Government pub-
lished a return showing the imports of the Com-
monwealth for the ten months to October 31, 1910.
They amounted to £48,875,080, an increase of
£7,529,086 as compared with the corresponding
period of 1909. At this rate, it appears that the
total imports for the year will reach fully
£57,000,000. The biggest item, and also the
biggest increase, appeared under the heading of
“apparel and all descriptions of softgoods,” and,
while most of the items are a disgrace to Aus-
tralian industry, this one is especially disgrace-
ful. Australia supplies, or is able to supply, all
the raw material for the making of softgoods. It
is the world’s greatest source of wool furnish-
ment; it has splendid cotton lands; it has as
good an opportunity for the production of silk as
the next place; it has bullock-hide enough to
make boots and other leather goods for half the
world ; and as for rabbits, regarded as a material
out of which hats can be made—well, it seems to
own three-fourths of the world’s rabbit supply, or
perhaps a little more. In fact, its imports of
softgoods consist largely in buying back its own
products at vastly increased prices, this country
being the Gibeonite that does the rough and
simple work of dragging things off the earth,
while the more cultured foreigner attends
to the finer and more costly and better-
paid sections of the. business. The imports of
machinery and metal goods in general also
help to remind this backward land that it has
great iron deposits of its own, though so far they
have done it little good. In fact, the whole long
list is a dreary reflection upon our inefficient tariff
and the inefficient makers thereof. A second re-
vision—a revision which will add many new
duties to the list and increase many old ones,
some by as much as 400 percent. —is already some
years overdue.
**• * *
Just now, when Australia is taking its defence
question a little more seriously than of yore, it is
a good time to consider how much lost defence
these imports represent. It may be assumed that
£5O a year is a fair average income, on which basis
the average earnings of a married man with four
children would be £3OO. At that rate the imports
of 1910 ought to represent 1,140,000 extra people
—the 1,140,000 people that are not here—the
1.140,000 inhabitants who don’t inhabit—the
1,140,000 folk that we might have but whom
we haven’t. That is to say, they represent
that number in the first place; but when there is
added (1) the number of extra agriculturists, shop-
keepers and workers of a thousand different
varieties, including archbishops and policemen, to
whom these 1,140,000 new artisans and their fami-
lies would give employment; (2) the additional
number of artisans whom these agriculturists,
shopkeepers, archbishops and policemen would
employ ; (3) the further lot of agriculturists, shop-
keepers, archbishops and policemen that this new
lot of artisans would employ ; (4) the further lot
of artisans they would employ ; (5) and so on to
the end of the chapter—when all these are added
it looks possible enough that Australia’simporting
disease and its inefficient tariff stand for an im-
mediate loss of 2,000,000 inhabitants, with a much
larger one in the future. And 2,000,000 people
stand for a mighty lot of defence.
* * * *
In the matter of national Protection a high
tariff represents a lot more than even these figures
would indicate. Defence nowadays doesn’t simply
consist in a hairy creature tearing a branch off a
tree and going to the front with the thin end of
it in his hand and the thick end in the air. It
employs men of every trade, barring jewellers
and variety showmen and a few more. The
country that becomes involved in a great
war wants to be able to abundantly supply
itself not only with food but with rifles,
bayonets, swords, cartridges, cannon, steel rails,
locomotives, carriages, signalling apparatus, tele-
graph wire, insulators, iron bridges, ships, aero-
planes, balloons, wool, cotton, linen, clothes,
boots, hats, surgical instruments, bikes, motor-
cars, paper, machinery of all sorts to keep up the
supply of goods, and many other things—that is
to say, it wants to be able to do all this if it is an
island state which is liable to be cut off from the
outer world. A country like Germany may afford
to take risks—though Germany doesn’t—in the
matter of depending on imports in war time, for
it has seven land frontiers, and its foreign supplies
couldn’t be wholly cut off unless it was beaten at
sea and had simultaneously got into conflict with
Russia, Austria, Switzerland, France, Holland,
Belgium and Denmark. Australia hasn’t one
land frontier ; to lose the command of the sea
would mean the practical stoppage of all supplies
except such as might be smuggled in. And con-
sidering the distances, the risks and the losses, these
would be few and very costly. In days of war and
deficient sea power it wouldn’t do Australia, as-
suming that it had the importing habit badly, one
penn’orth of good even it had 70,000,000 bushels
of surplus wheat to sell, and an Alp of frozen
meat, and 10 square leagues of dead rabbit, and a
cubic mile of hide, and all the rest of the pro-
ducts of its “great natural resources.” It
wouldn’t be able to get them to any foreign
market. They wouldn’t buy it even a pound of
cement, or a yard of cloth, or a rifle from abroad,
nor would they save it from insolvency for a
moment. Australia’s position would be many
times worse than that of the non-manufacturing
Southern States in the U.S. civil war, for they
had at least the friendly frontier of Mexico as an
outlet for their cotton and an inlet for their sup-
plies. A big and thoroughly effective tariff is to
Australia an army and a navy and a military and
naval reserve, an all-round entrenchment, a shore
fortification and an inland fortification —in fact, it
is one-half the battle and probably a good deal of
the other half. And these £57,000,000 of im-
ports are just the measure of Australia’s unpre-
paredness for the battle.
* * * *
The Freetrader, who is mostly an importer, and
the Freetrade paper, which makes its biggest
profit out of the importer’s advertisements, still
stick to the old lie that Freetrade and abundance
of importation and no local manufactures spell
cheapness. They have to do that because to the
importer local manufacture is extinction. The
local maker —of whom such Sydney furniture-
building firms as Hordern, Foy, and Beard
Watson and Co. are specimens—get into the
habit of dealing direct with the private buyer, and
that cheeky, useless, exorbitant middleman who
makes money by shoving his carcase in between
the foreign manufacturer and the Australian con-
sumer finds himself compelled to go and get work.
But local industry, when it gets fairly on its feet,
does a lot more than save the importer’s profits,
and supply the country with a vast extra reserve
of men for defence purposes, and insure the
country against being beaten almost without an
effort in war time through inability to obtain
munitions. It supplies new taxpayers and cus-
tomers for the postal, railway and other services.
Roughly speaking, one-fifth of everybody’s earn-
ings goes to the public treasury in one
shape or another. Therefore, the man who pays
£5 for a locally-made article knows that
some 20s. of it goes to lighten' his own public
burdens, consequently it is 12 pence cheaper to
buy a local article at £5 than an imported one at
£4 Is. And local industry reduces the public
debt; if by making our own goods under a heavily
Protective tariff we can employ and maintain
6,000,000 people instead of 4,000,000, then the
debt that was formerly £6O per head becomes only
£4O. On top of these things, all the evidence
goes to show that, as soon as local industries are
well established, prices go down—though even if
they didn’t the case for Freetrade would be just
as hopeless as it is now. It is cheaper in immedi-
ate cash to have no army than to have an army ; it
costs less on paper to do without a navy than to
possess one ; there is a large apparent saving in
disbanding the police force; and there is economy
in suicide, because it saves the cost of clothes,
boots, hats, food, rent, taxes, drinks, etc., etc.
But most people feel that these savings are a dead
loss in the end. And it is just as dead a loss to
save money—if money can be so saved—by leaving
the country without the means of supplying itself
in war time with the thousand and one things
that are as essential to defence as the army itself.
An isolated land which doesn’t manufacture all it
urgently requires may, in one war,, have the ex-
traordinary luck to be able to keep on importing
its goods, just as a country without defences
of its own may, for once, have the amazing good
fortune to import a borrowed army and navy.
But these things don’t often happen even once,
and they hardly ever happen twice.
Therefore the great increase in imports consti-
tutes a message to Australia that its chief defence
is rotten —that its biggest gun is cracked—that its
principal Dreadnought has neither bottom nor
sides, and not even very much top. And the de-
mand for repairs is loud and very urgent.
THE RISE IN FOOD PRICES.
The statement that the price of food has gone up
has become so frequent as to be a mere common-
place. Some theorists ascribe the rise to the
enormous increase in the supply of gold during
the last6oyears or thereabouts. Australia alone has
turned out, since 1850, more gold than the world’s
entire visible stock then consisted of, despite the
fact that that old stock included all the glories of
Ophir and Pactolus, and the loot of Spanish
America, and the plunder of Solomon’s temple
and other sundries. The proposition is that gold
has become cheap through superabundance,
so that a sovereign isn’t worth so many loaves as
it used to be, though the great saving that has
been effected in the cost of carrying the loaf from
place to place has partly counteracted this
upward movement. Bi-metallists profess a com-
plicated theory to the effect that the use of two
monetary standards instead of one would reduce
prices. Freetraders have a quaint conceit that the
abolition of customs duties would have the same
effect, though in what way the abolition of cus-
toms duties would increase the world’s food supply
it is difficult to tell. One certainty of the case is
that the rise in food prices is an exceedingly good
thing for a country like Australia, which has a
surplus of wheat and beef to sell; it
grows enough food for its own use whether a
bushel of wheat fetches 2s. 6d. or 55., but at ss. it
gets a great deal more cash in return for its sur-
plus, and it is encouraged to grow a much larger
surplus. On the other hand, a rise in prices is a
dead loss to countries, like England, which import
most of their food ; they have to make more car-
pets or calico or woollen or hardware with which
to purchase their daily bread, and there is no
countervailing advantage whatsoever. Conse-
quently, England, from its point of view, wails
.about high food prices, and the local Freetrade
press, which is merely an echo of British opinion,
often forgets that our point of view is quite
different, and wails also.
* -K *
Amid the many alleged causes of high food
prices there is one which looks solid and reliable,
and which suggests that the rise is going to be
permanent and to extend further. The number
of countries with a surplus of food for export is
steadily decreasing, and the number of countries
which want to buy food from abroad is on the
rise. There are very old people still living who
were born in the days when Britain often grew
more wheat than it required for its own
use; now it only grows about enough to meet
one-sixth of its demand. Austria-Hungary has
just got off the list of exporting countries
and become an importing one. Belgium, Den-
mark, France, Germany, Greece, Italy, Japan,
Holland, Norway, Portugal, Sweden and Turkey
are all grain-importing countries, and the most
of them have also reached that stage within
the lifetime of people who are not yet dead
or even old. Russia is almost the only European
country remaining that has a surplus of food, and
its surplus is a diminishing quantity ; in an em-
pire which has added 95,000,01)0 to its population
m 73 years, such a movement is inevitable. The
United States, by reason of its vast increase of
people, shows plain and visible signs of an inten-
tion to retire from its aged job as the world’s
granary. Then every country which leaves
the food-export business and becomes an importer
takes, in a sense, another country along with it.
For instance, 16 years ago Austria-Hungary and
Servia both had surplus grain to sell cheaply to
Britain, the chief buyer; but now Austria-Hungary
has a shortage, and is capable, in consequence, of
eating the surplus of Servia; and thus, in one act,
two sellers have ceased to clamor on Bull’s door-
step asking him if he wants some low-priced food
to put on his free breakfast-table. There are con-
stantly more buyers’ doorsteps to clamor on and
fewer sellers to clamor on all these doorsteps, and
in consequence there are signs that in a very few
years a balance that has remained fairly steady
ever since the records of commerce began will shift
considerably.
* * * *
Hitherto, it has almost invariably been the rich
country that bought food and the hard-up one
that sold it. The purchasing nation was the
affluent Lazarus catching the provender that fell
from the poor man’s table. But if, with the in-
crease in the number of food-buying countries and
the decrease in the number of selling ones, prices
keep going up, the situation must needs vary.
Capital will begin to move from old, densely-
occupied countries to new, thinly-peopled ones; it
will move in payment for the exorbitant
loaf and the chop that is as refined
gold. The world’s financial centres will tend
to change from old London and Berlin and
Paris into Canada, Argentina, Siberia and Aus-
tralia. Gradually a cold day will set in for the
rich States which can’t feed themselves, for the
other States will feed themselves first, even if
they have to legislate in order to prevent the ex-
port of an undue proportion of food, and the
wildly affluent foreigu buyer will get the rest —
the diminishing rest; and the less there is of it
the more he will pay for it. This condition of
affairs will induce an extra rush of population to
the great wheat-growing countries, and they will
want it badly, for, as the price of food increases,
the ground value of the States which have still
space on which to raise a great abundance of
the article will boom tremendously, and the
desfire to steal them with a high hand will become
acute. It will be a poor specimen of a thinly-
peopled land that won’t be worth stealing in the
days that don’t seem to be so very far ahead.
How far ahead they are no one can say ; but every
time a country takes itself off the list of the
wheat-sellers and adds itself to the list of the
wheat-buyers, as Austria-Hungary, France and
Germany have recently done, the time of difficulty
has plainly come a step nearer. If the tremendous
and ever-increasing consumption of coal and iron
produces scarcity and famine prices in these
articles, and men are driven, for the sake
of fuel and building material, to start
replanting the forests which they formerly demo-
lished to make room for wheatfields, the move-
ment will be hurried up materially. In fact,
unless man stops the present multiplication of
sinful yellow journals,, he will have to resume a
great deal of his wheat-lands for forestry, in any
case. The yellow journal (New York size), printed
on wood-pulp paper, is an amazing timber eater.
All the woods and forests mentioned in all Shak-
SPEARK’s works, and all the cedars of Lebanon to
boot, would have been consumed by modern yellow
journalism in a very few years—consumed to tell
the tale of the society divorce case, the latest
nigger-burning, and the flogging in Tennessee of
a Baptist preacher’s young second wife by her two
infuriated step-daughters, named Mercy and
Charity.
* * * *
It is commonly assumed that when a nation
ceases to increase it has started on the track of
active and positive decay. Hence the worry
about the birthrate. But it is quite evident that
the increase must stop some day, means
general decay or not, unless the more virile and
valuable races do something painful, but humor-
ous and wholesale, to the less valuable
ones ip order to make room. It is hard
at times to realise how large a spurt the world
has put on within quite recent times. England
has accumulated three times as many people in
the past century as it did in all the previous years
since the Creation ; that is to say, in the period
between the first verse of Genesis and the year
1801 it raised a population of 8,892.536, and in
the period between 1801 and 1909 it added
26,864,079 to these, making a total of 35,756,615.
All these extra folk have to be fed, yet the local
available food-growing area is actually less,
partly because more of the land is required for
building purposes, and partly because some of it
has recently fallen into the sea and been drowned.
(This last is an old trouble; Earl Godwin’s
estate fell overboard as far back as the
year 1100 and made the Godwin Sands.) In
about the same period the population of the
United States has multiplied by 15; that
of Java by eight, that of Australia by
something like 720 ; and there are other cases
almost as remarkable. In face, England is almost
an example of stagnation ; compared with such
countries as Prussia and Finland, it is re-
garded these days, despite its wonderful in-
crease of population, as a slow-moving place, and
it is probably below the European average. The
nigger of various shades, who was apparently,
while under his own rule and control, rather a
diminishing circumstance, is now almost every-
where more or less under the white man’s guid-
ance, and he seems to have started to expand
wonderfully in his new conditions. (It is a craze
of the white man’s, these days, to try to save
and perpetuate and multiply even the most
useless variety of nigger.) Then both the
white man and the nigger are acquiring
new wants which fill up space that used
to Vie available to grow grain, and meat, and wool,
and timber for house building and fuel, in the
days when man wanted but little here below ; the
room required for motor garages and rubber
forests for the supply of motor and bike tyres, and
the open areas that have to be reserved for the
rising and alighting (sometimes too suddenly) of
airships are a few matters among thousands which
didn’t affect our simple ancestors of long ago.
As a side issue of the case—during the recent cen-
tury, or perhaps even the recent half-century,
man has probably done more in the way of using
up the world’s irreplaceable capital in the
shape of coal and iron than he did in all
the previous ages of history and th'e ages
before history. He has probably done more
in the last 100 years to strip the earth of
its forests than he ever did before in 2000 years.
And it is very probable—though as a great part
of the world supplied no statistics until yesterday
or thereabouts this is only guesswork —that
he has, since the year 1800, done twice as
much, taking the world as a whole, to multiply
humanity than he did in all the ages since the
monkey ancestor came down from the tree-top
and commenced, in a tentative sort of way, to keep
house. Also, the discovery of new continents has
finished, and it is necessary to be content with
silch land;as we have, unless something;can be
done with a burning glass to shift the superin-
cumbent Polar ice and snow, so that it may be
possible to grow crops on the site.
* * * *
So out of this welter of circumstances it comes
to pass quite naturally that bread has a slightly
upward tendency. It is difficult to see what else
it can Vie expected to have. *' '
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Decentralisation Burlesque.
That plodding and humorless institution, the
N.S.W. Decentralisation Commission, has accu-
mulated so much deep thought and consideration
that it now wishes to postpone its report till
February. It requires the extra time that it may
tie a towel round its head and think. The Com-
mission has been a jest from the beginning, and it
promises to maintain its character to the last. It
was,appointed to find out: (1) If there are other
harbors in N.S.W. besides Port Jackson, or if
they were put on the map by ace.dent of malice
or with intent to deceive; (2) if the water
in these harbors, supposing they exist, is as stated
on the chart or otherwise ; (3) if it is good to have
harbors, or if those of N.S.W. (apart from Port
Jackson) are so different from those of other
countries that it is better that they should be
ignored and suppressed ; (4) if 350 miles is a
greater distance than 100 miles— i.e. , supposing a
district is 350 miles from Port Jackson and only
100 miles from Twofold Bay, whether the latteris
really closer or whether it is an optical illusion ;
and (5) whether the extra cost involved in send-
ing produce for export 250 miles of a needless
journey is compensated by the enjoyment the
goods get out of the trip and the scenery. Over
these points the Commission has been labor-
ing heavily for a number of months. N.S.W.
has really only a few ports that are fit to
receive large ships; that is to say, it has
two or three which could be adapted to the busi-
ness with very little expense, and some others
which could be adapted at a great deal of cost.
The first are well enough known and quite easy
to find. Each has an important district behind it,
and plainly only wants railway communication at
reasonable rates, some wharves and other simple
appurtenances, and the laying out of a township,
and business would commence. The fact that
people want to use these harbors is evidenced by
the fact that, in the old days of road carriage,
when all places were fairly equal, sucb
dead and forgotten places as Twofold Bay and
Jervis Bay were flourishing centres of oversea
trade. They were afterwards handicapped out of
existence by the policy which gave Sidney rail-
way communication and denied it to them ; but
that is another story. The fact that it would pay
N.S.W. to open up two or three good ports needs
no weary, superfluous Commission to prove it.
And there is nothing so wildly unusual or experi-
mental or revolutionary in the idea of building a
few miles of railway with a wharf at the end (in
every case a very few miles of railway are re-
quired) that it should be approached with the
caution that one would naturally use in the case
of a gorilla. The whole elaborate inquiry is a
burlesque. And while the burlesque goes on,
Parliament is being committed in haste to so
many lines devised to perpetuate and increase the
old congestion policy that, when the Commission
ultimately toddles in with its wonderful report,
there will probably be no money left, and that
fact will serve as an excuse for sending the docu-
ment to its long home in the coal-scuttle.


The periodical disordered cry re our black brother
Binghi is in full blast these days. Reverend and
other gentlemen fill much space in the daily
papers with unprovable statements concerning
him, and with dubious legends re the deep
damnation of his partial taking off. The people
who did the evil deeds enumerated are dead
now—just as dead as the early English invaders
from Germahy who bogged in. to utterly exter-
minate the older inhabitants of England, and
came within an ace of blotting out a much better
natiop than BiNGHI ; so there is nothing much to
be gained by digging up the ancient stories; but
the digging habit seems incurable. One excited
cleric (W. J. Gunther by name)supports his case
with the statement that “ the Great All-Father
gave them ” (the Binghi people) “ this continent
for an inheritance,’’ which is a fine example of the
utter recklessness of parsondom. For all the evi-
dence to the contrary, the original owners to
whom God gave Australia for an inheritance
were killed off millions of years ago, and the
present Jacky Jacky may represent the last of a
long succession of slaughterous invaders —in other
words, his title to the land may be a lot worse
than ours, for he didn’t cultivate the land and we
do a little in that way. There is much talk, too, in
this correspondence about how the nigger was
wiped out by “ the white man’s vices,” which is
also mostly an ancient story, and goes to show
the unfitness of Binghi, who couldn’t stand against
"vices” which the white man easily survived.
And there is very little talk about how, at this,very
day, the only important section of the black people
which remains is being wiped out by the brown
and yellow men’s vices—by Chinese opium and
Japanese sin and Malay bestiality. The average
parson would just Mate to sayan unkind word
about a Chinaman, dap or Malay, so the process
of extinction goes on with no clerical’ protest
which counts for anything. Meanwhile, close
alongside us, there is a colored race which might
still be saved and which seems to have
much greater possibilities than the black Aus-
tralian, and it is going downhill with a
remarkable lack of Church inte. ference. The
native Fijian has diminished something like 60
per cent, in 60 years. He began to disappear
when that human rabbit, the Indian coolie, was
introduced in the interests of cheap labor; but
the Indian is a nigger, and the Church is loath to
speak ill of any nigger; so Brown Brother is
shoved into the deep blue sea very unobtrusively.
If he departed to make room for a white
man the curse of the tabernacle would split
the air asunder, but as he is giving way
to a greasy and objectionable, but sturdy
black, the case is different. The clerical attitude
towards Binghi is a striking example of the un-
practical character of the Church. It devotes
itself, in theory at least, to the preservation, and
still more to the multiplication, of the unfit. It
prefers the unfit—-presumably because, by reason
of their infantile intelligence, they can be
beguiled into treating the parson with more
respect than people of bigger brain. So far as all
available evidence goes, there is no ultimate hope
for a race which is incapable of developing some
kind of a civilisation of its own, and Binghi, who
left in all Australia not one single public work, is
plainly one of the incapables. When a more
advanced people come along, the incapables pass
out, either by disease, violence, or invisible
causes. The Church objects to this—in theory,
at least. If it had its way it would preserve them
all, and the earth would be full of them, and
there would be no room for the more hopeful
races. Its theory is that the white man is always
wrong and that the nigger is always right. There
is no aboriginal so small, hairy and hopeless that
it won’t weep over his peaceful exit. Of course,
all men-—even the hairiest and least progressive
nigger—must die, but if there isn’t an abundance
of hairy and unprogressive nigger born to pre-
serve the race and keep the world filled with the
unfit, the Church takes the matter greatly to
heart. And in this matter the Church is up
against a tough proposition. In fact, it is an ant
fighting against the Inevitable.
The Usual Blow-off of the Fat Landlords.
The closing days of Victoria’s session afforded a
fine exhibition of the combined disasters of a tired
Premier and an obstreperous House of Fat Land-
lords. Having put through about a quarter of his
session programme, John Weary let the other
three parts slide, including his Workers’Accidents
Compensation, Preferential Voting, Closer Settle-
ment Amendment, Land Acts Amendment,
Mines Act Amendment, Sunday Entertainments
Bills, and others of more or less importance, and
set himself to save such remnants as the Fat
Landlords condescended to allow him. The Land-
lords were in good fettle. They seized the Rail-
ways Laws Amendment Bill and struck out the
clauses providing for; a permanent Board to inquire
into railway accidents—to which Weary said
“Orright.” They cut various clauses out of the
Spirit Merchants Bill; and Weary was saying
"Orright” when Gray, who sponsored these
clauses, objected, and the Bill was lost altogether.
They threw sundry clods into the Factories Act
amending bill—and once more Weary said
"Orright.” They " reviewed ” the Metropolitan
Rivers Bill after their own fashion, and Weary
nodded; but the Laborites...talked the measure
out. They rushed through the Coroner’s Bill, a
large measure which needs thinking over—and
the Assembly decided to leave the thinking over
till next session. Finally, they laid hold of the
Public Works Loan Application Bill, which ap-
portioned £202,000 for State Cool Stores at West
Melbourne, Ringwood and Diamond Creek, and for
assisting the progressive Geelong Harbor Trust
to get to business. These items were manifestly
designed to assist the producer, especially the
exporter of mutton, lamb, etc.; but the Fat
Landlords had no use for the producer just then,
all their ears being engaged by the lobbyists,
mainly from the private butter and meat
exporting films; so they tossed it aside
altogether. Weary professed himself much
annoyed over the "disgraceful lobbying”—
" the galleries of the Council reeked of butter,”
he said. But what good does that do?
In June last he had a chance to put up a fight
against the Landlords and improve their manners ;
but, as usual, he did nothing. Now they kick
him for his pains, which wouldn’t matter if they
didn’t incidentally kick the people too. In grati-
tude Weary is expected to fight for the right of
the State Landlords to go on kicking both him
and the people, and to whoop up his State to vote
“No ”in the coming referenda. But if Weary
is fool enough to do it, it isn’t likely that the
people will be.
The Rush of the Jerry Builder.
The Tasmanian Parliament fled from the wrath
to come by considering no less than 92 Bills in the
past session. Of these, in the frantic endeavor
to show the people that it is a live, active
body, it passed no fewer than 72. Ten others
are left in a state of suspended animation ; six
were slaughtered to make room for the Christmas
holidays ; and one (Local Option) lapsed in com-
mittee. The fact that the House of Gant (late
Dodkry) only rejected three Bills is also a
record. It goes without saying that these three
were useful and democratic measures: Compul-
sory Purchase (Closer Settlement), Shearers’
Accommodation (which is badly wanted), and
Testators’ Family Maintenance (to block a man
leaving his money to a Lost Cat Institution while
his family goes hungry). The House of Dodder
considered these proposals altogether too great an
interference with the Liberty of the Suspect, or
the rights of dead men, or something. Despite
the fact that the Eighteen Dry Bones were
all the session working with one eye on the
storm-shadow of the Referenda, they grew reck-
less towards the end of their job, and went in for
the mutilation of all the industrial Bills submitted
to them. They emasculated Workmen’s Com-
pensation by deleting the “industrial diseases”
clauses, struck Wages Boards a blow by inserting
the offensive “ reputable employer ” provision,
and stuffed in a clause to make strike-talk a £5OO
luxury; and when they won in the undignified
scramble they showed their magnanimity by pass-
ing no less than five syndicate Bills in a few
hours without turning a hair ! In spite of the
tremendous effort to show the people that the
State Parliament is the real Mackay, and that no
extension of Commonwealth power is necessary,
the only measures among the 72 that Tasmania
would not be as well without are the Land and
Income Tax and the Payment of Members Bills
—and these, not for their present value, but
because they embody the democratic prinpiples
that the unearned increment of land is a fair
subject for taxation, and that to expect mem-
bers of Parliament to work for 38s. 6d. a
week and remain reasonably honest, is folly.
So all the rush will go for nothing : the people of
Tasmania won’t measure up the value of the State
Parliament by the fact that, in a stampede, it
passed 72 bills in one session, but rather by the
fact that the net result of 50 years’ State Parlia-
mentary work is a steady loss of population. The
worst thing a Go vernment can do for a country is
to drive its people away while it heaps a greater
pile of debt on those who remain ; and the Tas-
manian Government has done all that. Where-
fore the 72 Bills count for no more than the frantic
haste of the jerry-builder.
Break of Gauge.
There are times when even the bitterest State
Brighter shouts for Federal encroachment, and
one of these happened along recently when Syd-
ney Telegraph clamored that the Commonwealth
should take on the job of changing, the State rail-
ways to a uniform gauge. Its proposal—rather
vaguely expressed—was that the States should
pay half the cost and the Commonwealth the
other half. As there would be nothing gained by
the Commonwealth paying half the money out of
taxes raised at the rate of so much per head,
and the States contributing the other half
also at the rate of so much per head, it is
presumed that the Telegraph’s idea is that the
Commonwealth should charge one-half the bill
to Australia at large, and the other half be paid
by the States in which the alterations are made.
The paper, of course, demanded that the N S. W.
gauge should be adopted, so that N.S. W. wouldn’t
have to pay anything towards the second half of
the bill, which would be an interesting position
for the State whose breakage of faith with Victoria
started the original difference of gauge and laid
the foundation of all the trouble. But the whole
idea is too clumsy, likewise too thin. The fact
that Australia must have a uniform railway gauge
is obvious enough, but it doesn’t follow in any off-
hand fashion that the 4ft. of IN.S.W. is the
measure to adopt. Certainly, it would be a wild,
mad Commonwealth that proposed tospend millions
on that sort of a job without first making a
thorough test of the mono-rail in Australia. And
whatever the new system may be, the only possi-
ble method is to transfer the railways bodily to
the Commonwealth, and let it do the work and
debit the cost against the whole railway system,
which is the same as charging it to the nation at
large. There is no method by which it can be
apportioned among the States to the complete
satisfaction of anybody ; even the Telegraph's
artful idea by which its own State would pay
next to nothing, while Queensland, S.A.
and Westralia carried the gigantic end of
the bill would probably excite annoyance in many
quarters. And the idea of the Commonwealth
reconstructing the State railways, and then re-
tiring from the field and leaving the cantankerous
State Governments to start the trouble all over
again would be an absurdity. One trouble of that
sort is more than enough.

The Minister of Grace and Elegance.
The letter of “ Charles Cowbell ”
There are many good points in “ Henry llousecollar’s ’
suggestions for the consideration of the new Minister of
Grace and Elegance (B. 29/12/’10). The overhead foot-
path idea is just frabjous. It gave me purple visions of
sauntering® on summer evenings with an armful of girl
and eyes full of the view—a view, I make bold to say, far
superior to any of the landscapes (which I have never
seen) where our fish and banana merchants were raised.
It also opens the delicious prospect of being able to get
about the city at one’s ease without passing the haunts
of rapacious creditors ; likewise avoiding the smells of
Dago restaurants, low pubs and ole clo’ shops. The pro-
posal seems to me on a higher plane than that of always
moving roadways in H. G. Wells’s “ When the Sleeper
Wakes.”
All right-thinking people will agree with the abolition
of hoardings. Though some posters are pictures, they
are slapped alongside atrocities ; and the hoardings at a
little distance look like crazy-quilts made by the insane.
There are 350 reasons why all street advertising should
be abolished, besides one which I advance modestly, viz.,
that the proper meejum of advt. is the newspaper.
Apart from these matters, I wish to protest violently,
in the sacred names of Grace and Elegance, against this
city of Sydney—with its dog-leg streets, its narrow cork-
screw lanes, its hit of ruin nearby a slap-up modern
mansion of commerce, its few quaint old houses with the
broad arrow stamped on every stone, its general irregu-
larity and corregiosity which, witli the harbor, make
it the plum city of the South—this quaint old young
place in which the eighteenth century and Early Vic-
torian rub shoulders with the late Edward and Allen
Taylor styles—this city, with its poetic allurement and
its artistic surprises (includingorange peel, broken fences
and erratic pavements)—against this city (excuse me if I
seem to be getting tied up ; but this matter affects me
more keenly than anything since Uncle Joseph died) ; pro-
test, I say, against this city being levelled on top,
straightened into parallelograms, and painted a uniform
color.
The one crying need of this raw young country is
ruins. Years ago, Victor Daley talked of the establish-
ment of a Society for the Erection of Ancient Ruins in
Australia —a brilliant suggestion, which fell on stony
ground. Sydney is the only part of Australia to do any-
thing towards providing ruins which would help to
develop a romantic spirit and keep the minds of the
young from horse-race and two-up. I therefore implore
The Bulletin to use its influence against Sydney being
H iusmannised in the manner suggested by Mr. “ Horse-
collar.” Besides, if the Japs come shortly after 1915, as
The Bulletin often warns us they may, why should we
spend good money on alterations which will he smashed
to inartistic ruin by their shells?
Let us have a Ministry of Grace and Elegance by all
means, and let “ Henry Horsecollar” be given power to
try his policy on the dog—to wit, Yassberra.

Premier Vkrran, of S. Aus., who boiled over at
the usual trite and harmless reference to human
“ interdependence ” made by the Governor at the
S. Aus. Anniversary Day feed, was apparently
suffering from the consciousness that he and his
State Government had made a mess of things
during the carters’ strike. On behalf of Verran’s
police, it was publicly stated that it was no breach
of the law for strikers to take horses out of vehicles
in transit; and when that announcement was
received with astonishment, the worse excuse was
made that laws obtaining in ordinary times don’t
apply in strike time. The Inspector of Factories,
who was about to start prosecutions under the
existing law, was suspended. A Labor M.P.
sat in state at the Trades Hall issuing
permits' to citizens to go about their
ordinary business—permits to remove luggage
and obtain petrol for motors. Insurrection
and tocsin and Carmagnole complete, and the
Labor M.P. in the Hotel de Ville issuing permits !
There was no guill itine and nobody was hoisted
a la lanterne ; but what was done was enough and
too much. If the Arbitration Court can’t work
quickly enough its machinery must be altered so
that it will ; but there is no room in the one com-
munity and at the same time for the scientific
protection of the worker by law and the bludgeon
of the strike or lockout, with the Labor M.P.
sitting in his office issuing permits.

Dr. Maxwell, for some years the Queensland
Government’s sugar expert and controller of the
State’s sugar mills, is back for a while from
America and the South Sea Islands. There isn’t
a man in Australia with greater practical experi-
ence of the Yellows and Browns; and this is his
straight tip to Australia :
Australia’s danger lies in the East. This is the final
otitpost of the Pacific, the last great vacant country, and
the eyes of the East are on it. I know the Chinese
and the Japanese well, hut it is the Japanese who
are most to be feared. It is well to keep out
aliens from this country and preserve it for the
whites. If the Japanese were to get any sort
of foothold on Northern Australia, you would never
he able to drive them out, and there’s no telling what
the result might be. Every Japanese is at heart a soldier.
I spent some time in Hawaii in March last, and I learnt
something about them. There are large numbers of
Japanese in Hawaii, and they are drilling every night—
ostensibly for amusement. And they have arms, too ;
they smuggle rifles in. Australia cannot afford to take
any risks with the Japanese, for they would do the same
thing here if they once got a footing. By-and-bye, as I
say, you will get thousands of immigrants from Am rica,
and they will help you to defend this country ; hut in the
meantime Australia is an empty land, and trouble may
come sooner than one expects. Her one great need is
population.”
“ Wonga”: I have been waiting for N.S.W.
Minister for Works Griffith to correct, or add,
or do something to a recent interview published
in the S. M. Herald. In the remarks attributed
to him, discoursing anent the barbarous methods
of Johnson’s railways in dealing with inventors,
Griffith referred to an inventor who, at his
(Griffith’s) suggestion, asked the railways to try
his patent. He was told that if he brought along
a full-sized model to a certain officer it would be
tried. He did so; and, after waiting a long time,
wrote and asked the outcome. He was informed
that it had been tried, and was an absolute failure.
He went along to collect his model, and found it
exactly where he had deposited it, and was
assured (Griffith’s words) by an attendant that
it had never been touched. When the model was
examined the paint was still in the sockets, etc.,
conclusively showing it hadn’t been used. This
is Griffith’s story ; and one waits breathlessly to
see what is to happen. But nothing, apparently,
is to happen. Griffith is a member of a Govern-
ment nominally in control of the railways, yet
there is no vigorous promise that the matter will
be ruthlessly followed up and the guilty people
dealt with. Someone must have written that
letter, someone must have authorised it, some-
one must have signed it. The trail is strong.
But Griffith apparently has no intention of
following it hotfoot. Anyhow, he doesn’t say he
will, though it is not expecting too much that he
should. Someone has told a rotten lie and evaded
a responsibility, dealing unfairly with a defence-
less man, and if the Government (of which Grif-
fith is a prominent member) intends to let the
-matter rip, it is a casual sort of Government —too
casual to attract many democrats. In the same
interview Griffith told the story of a railway
man who got hold of a valuable idea, and, upon
applying to the head serang for permission to
patent it (Griffith had previously drawn atten-
tion to an extraordinary regulation forbidding
railway men to take out patents without such per-
mission, a limit in autocratic tyranny), was told
he could do so at his own expense, provided the
Commissioners were given the right to use it free
of charge. Again, Griffith, as a member of the
Government, evidently feels he has fulfilled his
public duty by drawing attention to this matter.
He certainly made some remarks about the Works
Department giving inventors a fair show, but
omitted to add anything about what the Govern-
ment was going to say to Mr. Johnson. Which
is casual again, and not likely to inspire the
ignorant voter with much respect for the Govern-
ment which knows of injustices in public depart-
ments and doesn’t, apparently, intend to shift
them.

A feeling akin to cold feet lias been created in
Europe by the resolve of the Dutch Government
to fortify its coast. Holland’s two notable possi-
bilities are invasion from Britain and invasion
from Germany. The former may be left out of
the question, for Britain has no army which could
take on such an adventure in the face of European
opposition, but the latter is a very real possi-
bility. Therefore, the fortification of the land
frontier of Holland would be more understand-
able than the present move. The interpretation
which Europe puts on the matter is that Germany
has intimated to the Dutch Government (1) that if
Holland attempts to fortify its land border it will
be promptly swallowed : (2) that unless it fortifies
its coast-line it will be promptly swallowed ; (3)
that in any case it will be swallowed—in short,
that the shore fortifications are to prevent the
British fleet interfering with the swallowing pro-
cess. Presumably the eating up of the Nether-
lands by Germany isn’t very far off now. That
country was part of the old German Empire, and
the connection was so close that the original city
of Berlin was built by Dutchmen, and at least one
Dutchman became Emperor of Germany ; so the
Germans regard the place as a bit of accidentally-
dropped property which they are fully entitled
to recover. Also, Germany’s principal river wan-
ders into Dutch territory to the great inconven-
ience of German commerce, and some day the
Germans are coming to inquire about that river.
Like the British in Burnish and Beluehistan and
elsewhere, they are in search of a “scientific
frontier.”
An interesting wail was that of the chairman of
the Scottish Australian Investment Co. which
holds vast estates in N.S.W. and Queensland.
He spoke in the best British patronising style.
His company, it seems, has always felt kindly
towards Australian Governments, and hasn’t been
too proud to nod as it passed by ; and when Aus-
tralia recovers its senses and abolishes the pro-
gressive land tax, friendly relations will resume,
and the co. will wave its hand as usual. At
present, however, this country languishes under
the co.’s displeasure. It says the land tax is “ utter
folly,” and also that it is quite unnecessary, for
the co. has always been willing to sell its land.
That sounds very beautiful, but seeing that
after being 70 years in business the concern had
£1,191,000 of land still on hand a suspicion arises
that it is either a very cheeky liar or a very bad
salesman. And as the co. is now selling land in
Kiverina it would appear that the progressive
land tax was just the thing that was required to
give this ancient incorporated land-hog a much-
needed shove. It is partly because of vast, super-
cilious land-hogs of just this sort that the fertile
Kaverina, lying between two great rivers, has a
population of six persons to five square miles, and
three out of its 11 counties are actually losing
population. So the co.’s assurance that it will
again feel friendly towards the Australian
Government when that body “pursues a proper
course ” falls flat. Australia has no use for the
Scottish Australian Co., and after enjoying its
friendship for 70 years it is tired of the goods.

“ The Pariah” on the Victorian land-hunger :
In “ Plain English ” (B. 22/12/’ 10) mention is made of the
Shaw-Sculltn argument concerning Victorian land avail-
able formen with much energy and little capital. Shaw,
a squatter, says there is plenty of good land, but that the
men won’t take it. Scullin says th/ere isn’t, and that if
there was they would wolf it.
This is the plain tale of what I saw when with a survey
party in the Western District, where the argument
occurred.
In 1909 one of the Chirnsides sold a small area
of his holding at an average price of £6O to £7O per acre.
Everard Browne had the Goroorooke Estate subdivided
and offered for sale ; reserve price, about £5O per acre.
That is Shaw’s side—plenty of good, cheap land. Now
for Sculljn’s.
Striking north from Pomborneit, we surveyed a block
fronting Lake Corangamite. It was so rocky that in 241
chains of running we never drove a peg or dug a trench—
had to build angles, etc., in with stones. This block
would have kept all Victoria in road metal for 10 years.
Leaving Corangamite, we drove towards Camperdown on
the well-kept road running through Manifold’s and other
big squatters’ kingdoms. This was first-class agricultural
land, and was mostly busy drinking in the sunshine. At
Garvoc and at Arundel we surveyed blocks of poor third-
class land densely covered with stunted or un-
sound timber, useless except for firewood. Further
south, at Curdies River, three blocks of grasstree
and heath—doubtfully described as fair oat and
potato land—had been selected. Somewhere near
here a Government experimental farm went bung with a
loud noise. Thence eastward to Laver’s Hill the land is
really good, but stands on its end, so that it affords only
fair pasture for dairy herds. It was found impossible to
grade a road from one block. From here we used
“ packs ” to get to the Calder Swamps, where we first
surveyed a block with terrific slopes, covered with heavy
but mostly unsound timber. Then the swamps came in
for attention. Most of these blocks were practically a
mesh of weeds floating’ on black ooze, and the pegs used
were 7ft. stakes. Indeed, two blocks were so unstable
that it was just about impossible to carry the jigger
round the lines, let alone put the enclosure to any prac-
tical use. There is a mighty small fall to the sea-level,
and that is made worse by an intermittent bar at the
mouth of the Aire.
I met several of the men who had selected in the dis-
tricts mentioned. With one exception, they were young
and strong, and prepared to put great heart into their
selections. Bur, cut bono } In every case it meant years
of almost profitless labor, and after that not much more
than a living.
All this shows that squatter Siiaw is right. The laz t
devils don’t want good land that they can work at a pro-
The Singing Spark.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Coherkr-leashkd, in echelon the fighting Titans
;
He drives a team of battleships with mast and
dynamo !
The singing spark yokes ship to ship, and in the
conning-tower
I see the lean Sea-Master grip his fleet of battle-
power !
To-night, upon an hundred seas, where ships and
navies ride,
The singing spark’s new symphonies are swinging
far and wide !
Orchestral voices ’midst the storm are piercing to
and fro—
Where ships of battle ride enorme, hear ye the
Message go !
From Jutland and the Dogger Bank, by England’s
salt sea ways,
The monsters clank while staggered ghosts sit on
the crests a-gaze !
They are the Empire’s ships of war, the battle-
lanterns shine;
For 801 l must keep his Open Door for meat and
corn and wine!
While England’s cops are on the deep, the great
sea-’buses come
And bear the flesh of frozen sheep unto the
Briton’s home !
Lest Bull should hunger, ship to ship speaks
gravely in the night;
The singing spark is England’s whip to drive the
team a-right!
The berged Atlantic knows their freights. ’Tis
England’s beef and pork
When cargo-shifters speak their mates ’twixt
Bristol and New York.
The narrow seas grow smaller yet, the oceans
shrunken are ;
And foodless Britain’s commerce-net goes stretch-
ing very far !
From Argentina and the Isles where Ward and
winter scream
The meat-tanks tramp across the miles—-and
wheat and butter steam !
From Galveston and New Orleans Bull’s cotton
cargoes go;
The singing spark knows what it means—the
foodless days of woe !
Coherer-leashed, in echelon Bull’s sea-policemen
ride —
Each bobby is a battleship that swings upon the
tide !
The ocean-beat, east, south and north, is paced
with silent power;
The cargo-tanks go back and forth. Bull’s in his
conning-tower !
To-night upon a hundred seas his precious car-
goes are;
The Master-Copman sniffs the breeze, his search-
lights swinging far !
And mighty voices ’midst the storm are speaking
to and fro
Where Bull’s policemen tramp enorme, hear ye
the Message Go !
