An Oft-Repeated Lie.
A great deal can be done by just sticking to the
old lie— sticking to it bard and fast, and dis-
regarding all proof to the contrary, and refusing
all explanations, especially when the lie is so silly
and so utterly insane that explanation is simply
impossible. This is the attitude of the gang which
clamors for the eastward deviation of the Port
Darwin transcontinental railway. As an example,
the Sydney Telegraph sprang off its tail — the
same old tail—in a leading article of January 3 :
Our correspondent at Port Darwin describes in a mes-
sage which we print this morning the dismal failure of
South Australia’s administration of the Northern Terri-
tory, and, in broad outline, the task before the Common-
wealth there as new proprietor. The outgoing ruler
must not be hastily blamed, for the work was at once
heavy and strange. A more energetic manager might
have succeeded no better in such an unaccustomed and
difficult sphere. What we may do,-however, is, render a
respectful tribute to the State which, after doing so
badly with the Territory, has managed to unload its
white elephant on to the Commonwealth, together with
its big accumulated debt, and on terms which assure that
railway communication shall only be through and with
the profiting State. Tiib railway conditions will have
TO BK DEPARTED FROM IK THE TERRITORY IS TO BE HELD FOR
Australia, since it is palpably impossible to keep con-
nection WITH THE NORTH OF A CONTINENT BY A SYSTEM WHICH
ALLOWS NO COMMUNICATION EXCEPT BETWEEN FAR NORTH AND
FAR SOUTH.
In other words, the Telegraph deliberately urges
Australia to break faith, and, having got the
Territory on certain conditions, to depart from
these conditions. And the reason it assigns is a
lie so utterly foolish that it shouldn’t impose on a
child, yet one which gains a certain amount
of attention through the sheer impudence with
which it is repeated at all times and seasons.
The theory of the deviationists is that the rail-
ways that strike westward from Sydney, Bris-
bane, Townsville, etc., can be carried far enough
west to join on to the transcontinental if the
transcontinental goes through Queensland and
N.S.W., but they can’t be carried far enough
west to join it if it goes through the Territory and
South Australia. Their theory is that railways
from the east coast to the west are impossible—it
must be so if railways extending half-way to con-
nect the east coast with a line in the middle of
Australia are impossible. They don’t say why this
is so, because the tale is so outrageous that it isn’t
safetogo in to detail; they just bump down the bare
statement. A railway connecting Queensland
with the Territory, they say, is perfectly feasible
provided that Queensland gets whatever profit
there is to be made by having the main line
within its borders. On the other hand, a railway
connecting Queensland with the Territory is im-
possible if the main line is in the Territory and
Queensland is only joined with it by branches.
It is quite simple and easy, it seems, fora railway
to cross the Queensland-Territory border at
Camooweal if it is the main Federal line brought
round deviously from Port Darwin. But if the
Federal railway goes due south from Port
Darwin, and the line which crosses the border at
Camooweal is only a subsidiary Townsville rail-
way extended to join the Federal undertaking-
well, it seems that even the earth will rise in
righteous anger and throw that railway off its
chest. The thing can’t be done, and the Sydney
Telegraph and the other deviation papers and
all the deviation politicians say so without show-
ing cause. Even the maddest or drunkest of
them couldn’t show cause. Wonderful calcula-
tions are made about how many thousands of
miles the trade of Cairns must travel in order
that it may reach Port Darwin via, Townsville,
Rockhampton, Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne,
Adelaide and Central Australia generally, all
through the transcontinental line not being
diverted into Queensland and N.S.W., and how
many millions of leagues the trade of every other
place on the east coast must crawl round the
same gigantic circuit; but the reason of the cir-
cuit is left to the reader’s imagination. And
there are people here and there who have been
half persuaded, through much repetition, that
there may be something in the story, despite its
obvious madness.
A brilliant observation from a recent S. M.
Herald leading article :
It is a tragic circumstance that, ever since the Com-
monwealth began, hardly a session has passed without
bringing to light some piece of legislation or some piece
of administration that has made us suspect, for the time
being at any rate, of the English-speaking world.
It is very very sad, no doubt. Still, under Com-
monwealth legislation and administration, Aus-
tralia’s manufactures have nearly doubled in
10 years; its population has started to
increase at a rate that has been un-
heard of since the great gold days; its
bank deposits and its stock of cash have grown
in an unprecedented fashion ; its commerce has
gone up as it never did before ; the birth-rate has
risen, and the death-rate has fallen ; the unem-
ployed have almost vanished ; and though Aus-
tralia still borrows a great deal too much, it now,
for the first time in its life, finds most of the
money itself. If this sort of management has
made us suspected by the English-speaking world
—which may or may not b.e the case—it is quite
possible that it is the English-speaking world
that is at fault and not ourselves. For instance,
the London Morning Leader (23/11/’10) men-
tioned casually that in the English town of Hud-
dersfield (population about 95,000) there are fully
300 underground dwellings, and that it is common
enough there for six or seven people to live in a
cellar 12ft. by 14ft. and with a roof only 7ft. high.
'lf folk like that have dark suspicions concerning
Australia’s social legislation, it is a regrettable
business, but it is difficult to see liow the thing
can be helped. Seven people in a cellar four yards
one way and less than five the other would sus-
pect almost anybody or anything.

From the “Old Soldier”: —
It. almost seems as if the Commonwealth Government
was more than half afraid of taking the business of de-
fence too seriously. One of the highest authorities,
lecturing at Albury, is reported to have cautioned the
new Instructional Staff against over-zeal
“Don’t work the boys too hard at first,” said Colonel
Legge, in addressing the area officers in the course of one
of his lectures on their duties. . . “The public like a big
show, large numbers in uniform and carrying rifles, and
plenty of bauds. Later, the public will be so educated
that they will not judge by such show only ; but at first
they will want to see something for their money ; there-
fore, we must get ready for a big parade.”
That means a continuation of the old system of sham
and pretence. No one is to be worked too hard, for fear
he should be disgusted with soldiering. That does not
make for efficiency. But efficiency is not wanted, only
show. I have come across a letter sent by a German
“ one-year-volunteer ” to his grandparents soon after
joining his regiment. Here is, his story
“ As you are aware, I have joined an artillery regiment.
From 4.30 to 6.30 we work in the stables, rubbing down
horses, cleaning stables, harness and saddles ; and we are
expected to do it with so much vigor that we are wet
through with perspiration. Then we have a lecture from
the Lieutenant till 8.30. From 8.46 to 9.30 we have riding
drill; from 9.30 to 10, rubbing down horses; 10 toll,
drill. Then we go to dinner. At 12.45 there is roll-call,
followed by stables till 2.30; then drill from 3 to 5 ;
and stables, bedding-down and feeding horses from
5.30 r.o 6.30. After that we go home and study.
Then I drop into that most beautiful possession of the
present age—bed. But it is a fine life, all the same. The
few free moments are full of fun, with all the stories
comrades tell of the things they have done or are going
to do; and when you consider how healthy this sort of
life is, I feel jolly thankful I have strong, straight limbs,
and was able to become a soldier.”
There it is ; and it must be remembered that the one-
year-volunteer in Germany must do all this hard work at
his own expense. He must provide his own uniform,
board and lodging, and must pass a very stiff examination
to qualify him for the privilege of training for 12
months instead of two years. That is what makes the
German army the terribly efficient fighting machine it is.
Here in Australia we seem to be anxious to ask from our
soldiers the irreducible minimum of efficiency. According
to our Administrative and Instructional Staff, as we ball
it, the only object they have in view is to make the public
believe it is getting the worth of its money. Only boys—
senior cadets—are to be enrolled this year, and they are
not to be worked hard, the dears !

St. Kilda Esplanade .. The Follies.
The writer took a mental ’bus this week
and drove furiously through the back files of The
Bulletin till an imaginary policeman stopped the
vehicle on a charge of cruelty to the supposed
horses. He was trying to find out when “Our
Miss Gibbs” started; but his dream on four
wheels didn’t get far enough back. The begin-
ning of the show that still rages at Sydney Royal
is so remote that it has become geological,-so to
speak. As for the end, it can’t be foreseen, for
during the glad New Year season the Religious
Editor drank the office telescope—that is to say,
he pawned it and surrounded what lie bought
with the proceeds.
** * *
“The Whip” still goes its way at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) with a fair measure of success.
The fine staging carries it through, for thp play
itself is just the usual melodramatic kind of
picture drawn with blobs of black and blobs of
white. The hero is white, of course—a sort of
dazzling ass, to be considered through tinted
spectacles out of regard for the eyesight. If he
slipped on a mountain side it would be difficult
to tell whether he was a mule or an avalanche.
His folly suggests the former, and the nobleness
of his intentions remind one of the color of the
latter. And the villain is a coal-mine in human
shape—an ink-blot doomed for a certain time to
walk the earth. Between them is the figure of
the pure woman—but no words can do justice to
her. Her place is Heaven, not earth, and it is
only by carrying bricks around in her corset for
ballast that she contrives to remain on this mun-
dane sphere.
** * *
“The Arcadians” makes a first-class holi-
day bill; and, on the. strength of the crowded
business at the Criterion, partner Rupert Clarke
will be able to tack an extra week or two on to
his honeymoon tour of Palestine and other sacred
parts. There are no dull seconds in “The
Arcadians” —and the principals are brighter than
the seconds. No affluent cousin from the country
has done justice to either himself or his holiday
until lie has heard Mr. Doody’s motter and seen
how he lives down to it.
“The Girl from Rector’s ” has been having
a high old time at the Palace for nearly two
weeks, and so far she has not been arrested by
the police, warned by the Watchman, censured
by the Earnest Christian Workers’ Smile-Not
Association, or shunned, banned or sniffed at by
the church of the Woeful Wowsers. Also it
has to be recorded that she has not yet
been made an honorary member of the Com-
mercial Travellers’ Smoke-Room Union, nor
handed the emblem of the blue garter by
the Associated Band of High-Steppers. None
of these things has happened to the lady for the
excellent reason that she hasn’t earned one of
them; but she has earned the good will and
spontaneous hip-hurrah of audiences that have
filled the theatre nightly. Strangely enough,
with all the big business, Hugh J. Ward looks
gloomier as the season progresses. “ Every
night,” he says, “I am mentally saying good-
bye to those people down there. I know
them all well; and I keep wondering if I’ll
ever meet them again, and get the old thrill
of their friendship.” Here sympathetic silence
is expressed on all sides .of the actor-manager’s
dressing-room —he does this between acts. Then :
“Life is a mean sort of business, boys. Man
meets his fellow man, and passes” . Then
somebody hands along the bottle, and by the
time that has been attended to the callboy has
put his oar in, and the soliloquy has to stand oyer
for another night. But Ward swears he’ll finish
it if he has to keep the season running six months.
For all the information to the contrary, “The
Girl from Rector’s” may intend to keep going
that long herself.
** * *
Alfred Hill’s testimonial benefit at the
Standard Theatre, Sydney, on Monday night was
a crowded affair, as the house should have
been on all the eight nights of “A Moor-
ish Maid.” Lovers of bright and good
music—and there are many in Sydney—
may well hope that the “ Maid ” will shortly re-
appear in better setting and environment. “A
Moorish Maid ” ought to be at least half as
attractive as “ A Merry Widow,” even allowing
that the Maid labors under the disadvantages of
maidenhood and music of a higher order.
The Six American Dancers continue to
step gaily in the forefront of Sydney Tivoli bill;
and the Blessings, who have flowed from foreign
parts by mailboat, demonstrate every evening
that they are something to be extra-thankful for.
Frank Harwood, Fred Bluett, Fanny Powers,
and the Quealys are the other artists who are
entitled to the heartfelt gratitude of first-part
dodgers. The Meyrnotts’ season is billed to close
to-morrow (Friday) night, and their departure
will leave a fair-sized cavity in the programme;
but the final exit of the Virginian Judge, Walter
Kelly, who vanishes the same evening, will leave
a cave so large that unless it is immediately filled
by a landslide of talent it will certainly be taken
up by a syndicate of Middle Harbor hermits.
The Religious Editor is deeply pained to think
that a man with so much humorous capital is
about to leave the country.
** * *
At Sydney Glaciarium West’s Pictures
have struck a Bullfinch in the Beauty Competi-
tion. The local, family and patriotic interest is
fully aroused, and the audience rolls up to
judge which of the Sydney girls who travel
across the tremulous film is the most beautiful.
Up to date about 25,000 people have voted,
and the interest shows no signs of falling off. In
addition to the Beauty Show there is the usual
programme, with its regular intermixture of
comics, its occasional minor notes of tragedy, and
its pleasantly educational section, in which we sit
back and learn things about the world on which
we play the part of fleas.
’*# * m
At Sydney Lyceum Spencer’s Theatre-
scope has an impressive film of historical interest
showing the taking of Saragossa. Saragossa, it
will be remembered, was the Spanish town that
held out so desperately against the great Napoleon,
and which the French army found, after the regu-
lar fortifications had been smashed, “ to consist
of a great number of houses every one of
which was a fortress.” It is a fine, thrillsome
subject, and makes a most excellent film. The
dramatic film of the week bears the terrible name
of "Life for Life.” Mere words cannot paint its
thrillsomeness. “The Broken Doll” is an In-
dian story ; “ Wood Carving ” is the educational
item, and "At Sea Under Naval Orders” gives
an idea of life on the rolling wave under naval
conditions.
** * *
Maggie Papakura’s Maori Village at Clon-
tarf (N-S. W.) will probably get a lift after this week
from the opening of the Spit-to-Manly tramway.
Hitherto the big population which nowadays lives
under red tiles and straw hats on the northern side
of the harbor has had to take two water trips to
reach Manly ; after this week it will be able to
go the whole way by electric tram, except that a
punt must still make for it the perilous pas-
sage across Middle Harbor at the Spit. The
State Treasury probably won’t burst with the
ptofipff&m the line in competition with the four-
penny ferry ; but while Maggie Papakura and
the Arawa maidens are making soulful eyes to
them from Clontarf, at the Spit, a few thousand
holiday makers will no doubt feel called upon to
go the round trip—to Manly by water and back
by land.

“ Billy B.’s ” place of amusement :
The only place in Sydney where a man can get any
excitement these non-electioneering times is at the
Stadium, a large open-air amphitheatre where profes-
sionals stoush to make a hoodlums’ holiday. I never
miss going there when a fight is threatened, and I never
regret what it costs me. When I say that, I don’t mean
that the fight, as a fight, is worth the money ;
mostlv it is worth nothing. The abolition of
the Marquis of Queensbury rules by American pugs
and slugs has removed all sporting and spectacular interest
from the game, and the proprietor of the average modern
bash-emporiutn is a plain, hard-faced citizen who sops up all
he can while the boom lasts, and treats the public like
the fool it is. But the ring-side compensations are many.
Last week there were two fight nights, and I don’t know
how many fights were crowded into them. On
Boxing riight, daring an unusually fierce round
between the professional sluggers, a couple of
spectators suddenly rose and battered at each other s
•noses, and made weird yelps, and kicked chairs
over, and rolled on tiie ground and stuffed sand and
gravel into one another’s mouths, while the crowd for-
sook the advertised encounter for the impromptu battle
in the dirt. A few minutes later one of the ring con-
testants struck the other below the belt and dropped
him like an axe-stricken sheep. Billions of in-
furiated men immediately sprang to their feet and
yelled and shrieked and cursed in every key in the
language. Behind me a woman stood upon a seat,
and raved: “ He’s killed the man ! He’s killed
the man ! ” until a big whisky-voiced person turned
and snarled: “D you; get home to yer kids.
But of all the thrills to date I best enjoyed
that of last Wednesday (28/12/TO), when a flash-Harry
individual from the land of the great hog-export trade,
who was acting as a second to a brother expert, waltzed
into the ring and insulted the referee (a gentlemanly per-
son who always reminds me of a young deacon). To the
utter amazement and great pain of the flash-Harry
person—who is a star-and-tripe pug of some reputation—
the referee promptly knocked the insult as far back as it
would go For the next five minutes Chaos beat time for
a whirling mass of pugs and their lovely following—
policemen, blokes, spielers, pressmen, hysterical youths
and others—all shouting:, swearing, hooting, pushing
and fighting towards the centre of the disturb-
ance. After the row I found myself alongside a
red-faced man with one end of his collar
torn away. Glaring at me malevolently, he said : “ Did
the referee job him.” I replied that the referee certainly
had “ jobbed ” him ? “Good enough for the cow,” he ex-
claimed. “I wish he’d broken his neck. Don’t
you?” I admitted I was a bit disappointed. Still, it was
a great night, and probably somebody will be killed next
time. I’ll be there on the off chance, anyhow.
“ THE GAY GORDONS." AT MEL-
BOURNE ROYAL.

Edmund Siddons. Frank Danby as Nervy Nat.
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the Beanstalk
which lived in the House that Jack built con-
tinues to provide good fodder for the Cow with
the Crumpled Horn. Also, an Australian gun-
boat ploughs the village where the Fairy Queen
sows a Vegetable Ballet, and the curtain rises at
7 40 p.m. sharp on account of the magnitude of
the production. If the Williamson panto, seemed
a doubtful success on the first night, the doubt
must have passed away, for the treasury receipts
are enormous. Eight times a week until further
notice the Christmas Annual will'be repeated
amidst shrieks of approval from the children. 1 his
is a more osteiitatiously “comic” panto, than
some that have happened since the harlequinade
was finally abolished. A cow, armed with all the
qualifications for a cab rank, is a leading feature
of the show. The principal boy, who is a girl,
and the principal boy’s mother, who is a male
Scotch comedian with thistles in his accent, talk
gravely and sympathetically about the,cow > apd
when that obstreperous animal joins silently in
the conversation, a roar of general merriment is
quite permissible. In a well-regulated panto,
there are moments when the kiddies don t have
all the joke to themselves.
* * * #■
As regards “ The Old Woman Who Lives
in a Shoe,” at the King’s (its appearances are
confined to matinees), the present historian finds
it a matter of deep personal interest that he
can see nothing funny in a panto, played by chil-
dren Liddiard’s Lilliputians go through their
work without a hitch, and are plainly well pleased
with their performance ; but their happiness is
not contagious. They are neither amazing nor
amusing. Perhaps it is the libretto of the children s
panto, that gets on one’s nerves. There is abso-
lutely nothing in it which could bring a blush to a
curate at a Sunday-school picnic. What is called
“patter” in an ordinary music-hall song is re-
duced to “piffle ” in the ditties that the youngsters
struggle with ; and when six youngsters in suc-
cession have sung a series of long, soft songs con-
cerning spoons, moons and coons, a captious critic
grows discontented with his lot.
** * *
The mixture of frivolous melodrama and
flippant music, which serves the purpose of comic
opera at Melb. Royal, proves to be no better or
worse than it appeared on the first night. The
same remark applies to the new Clarke-Meynell
people, who are doing their best for “ The Gay
Gordons.” Nothing in the show is strikingly
bright or remarkably humorous, although a good
deal of it has an air of novelty. The lawyer
(Fred Winn), who discovers Angus Graeme’s
mysterious parentage and suddenly makes an
earl of him, is a matter-of-fact sort of
person until near the end of the piece,
whan he sings about Mr. Jolliboy and dances
his own accompaniment. He comes as a sur-
prise among the incidental “ turns,’ and is
acceptable on that account, whereas some of the
other “turns” are dragged in by people who
have been singing previously, and are not in great
demand for further contributions. For instances,
the crazy jingle of “Mr. McKie ” and the
“Limericks’” in which five members of the eo.
take part, are items that merely weaken the en-
tertainment by prolonging it. The tail of “The
Gay Gordons” is much too long for its body, and
should be shortened by 15 minutes at least.

The large public relish for “ The Bad Girl
of the Family ” suggest that ’couta ean be caviare
to certain sections of the people. And yet the
present show at Melbourne Princess’s is one of
the least deserving of indulgent patronage. It is
full of irritations, from its dreadful humor to the
vulgarities of its comic Jew. Moreover, it seems
to ha,ve been produced on a scale of lavish inex-
pensiveness. Nothing, for instance, looks so
cheap as the picture of a room in Erskine Hall,
the alleged country seat of a noble lord. Here
the carpets and" ornaments shriek of the
second-rate and dingy genteel. Also, the furni-
ture, instead of being (say) after the mode of
Louis the Fifteenth belongs to the unexalted
style of Smith the Manyth.

Barnold’s dogs and monkeys, particularly
the dog Daniel, are the main support of the
Christmas bill at the Opera House. Later arri-
vals are Howard and Foy, and Wood and Fraser,
whilst Prince continues to expound the possi-
bilities of the concertina, and the pictures of
Greater London are staged as before. A few re-
appearances of the picturesque Marbas were to
end early in this week, and their vacant place will
be filled to-morrow.
** * *
West’s processions of pictures are still
floating past the eyes of Melbourne congregations.
On Saturday the best item on the bill was “The
Sergeant,” which detailed the adventures of two
affinities amidst Yosemite scenery. The customs
of the stork were a pleasant variation from the
manners of a person by the name of Foolshead ;
while the tale of “Bertie’s Elopement” was not
so popular as the story of “ A Lucky Toothache.”
This last had an odd simplicity, and it was so
naturally pretty that if strangled criticism with a
rope of roses, so to speak.

Amusement shows are being multiplied in
Adelaide, and almost every week the earnest
pleasure-seeker has to reckon with some new
“Arena” or “Casino.” (“Coliseum,” which
would be a fine name, has already been collared
for a drapery store.) These new buildings are
mostly devoted to picture-films or boxing. About
the latter the City Council has changed its mind,
and it quite enthusiastically granted a license for
an extra holiday battle to celebrate the Season of
Peace. At the Tivoli a shapely lady named
Yetta. just from Paris, walks a wire in a dashing
style that takes the mind back to stars of the
past. But they didn’t cakewalk on the
wire; in fact, they wouldn’t have known
hovit to. At the Tivoli “The Prince Chap” has
run its sugary course with Roberts and the
three heroines, and Maggie Moore now gets her
chance in “ The Chinese Question.” West’s Pic-
tures have the dramatic American war film, “ The
Road to Richmond,” and some round the world
scenes. The Empire has the same drama (why do
things get duplicated in this way ?), and is also
wisely specialising with local scenes—-Glenelg
beach on the 28th, and'so on.

“ Puketapu ” seeks information :
Can any old hand tell me what hidden insult lav in
calling a man “Joe ” back in the gold rush days of'Vic-
toria and Maoriland ? My father, Hemi, sometimes speaks
of the early days in Dunedin (M.L.), when sin and grog
and flying cash were the primitive town’s chief attributes,
and again and again he has recalled how riots and general
battery broke out through the hurling of the solitary
word “Joe.” The contemptuous remark was first
introduced bv diggers who had migrated from Aus-
tralia, and was particularly used as a gibe at the
police. In those days there was only one lockup
in Dunedin, and the whole town lay betw’een it and the
court-house, so every morning a long procession of
drunks, thieves, spielers, and Flossies with feathers that
would have made a brigadier-general’s hat blush for its
nakedness, was marched through the ill-formed and
(generally) mud-covered streets to be introduced to the
magistrates. Wives who had lost their husbands over-
night, friends who had been parted from friends in
a fight or debauch, riff-raff (and a nice collec-
tion of it there was then) and the general public
lined up regularly to see the march past; and to
yell “Joe” at the escort. ’Arrests always followed
the cry—it seemed to gall the police to fury/ Resistance
led to a free fight, arid batons, oaths, mud, handcuffs and
concentrated stoush filled the neighborhood with unholy
joy. My father never knew what “Joe” meant—since
the man-eating days he had lived at peace with his
fellows, and never inquired—but I would like to know ;
and if anybody can tell me I’ll be eternally obliged.
A Royal Commission might very well be
appointed to inquire into the case of the Dunedin
(M.L.) girl who went to the altar in full bridal
array, with relatives and friends in attendance, and
then burst up the ceremony by saying “No” to
the parson’s fateful question. A bridegroom-elect
may always be regarded as a possible non-starter
up to the very last moment. In most cases he
wants the girl rather than the marriage tie,
whereas she is dead set on achieving the contract
to which the man is the submissive party. On
the other hand, outside of bellowdrama, the lady,
before she proceeds to the scene of action, has in-
variably decided to see the wedding through. She
may intend to revolt against the man the moment
she has got him, but she means to get him first.
She doesn’t put on her war paint for
nothing. Her mind is made up, anyhow.
The wedding is her show, and she helps to make
it a complete success, otherwise she wouldn’t have
dressed herself for the star part. Consequently,
a bride who upsets an old-established order of
things by monkeying with the nuptial ceremony
is a matter for earnest consideration. She looks
very like a startling result of woman suffrage;
she’s a new development in the relations between
the sexes. Probably she’s an awful outcome of
wicked Socialism, and is directly due to the
Labor Party. Needless to say that she mustn’t
be allowed to establish a precedent. If bachelors
in general are confronted with the risk of becom-
ing rejected bridegrooms, they will treat the old
idea of a marriagb contract with lofty contempt,
and the necessity for divorce courts will soon cease
to exist.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.": The Johnson-Jeffries fight film, edited by
Jeffries, is even more deceitful than the film which dealt
with Johnson’s defeat of Tahmm.v Burns, and which issued
from the white man’s camp. The picture story of Jef-
fries v. the Niggah appears to have been arranged in the
interests of Jeff from the start. The ultimate hero never
gets a look into the picture until he appears in
the ring with a smile of confidence wreathed
around his ivories. All the preliminary scenes
are dedicated to Jeff and his white predecessors, and
they make one very weary. The predecessors—Jonel
Sullivan, Corbett, Fitzsimmons, etc.—are rather less un-
impressive than the man whom Massa Johnson was going
toknock out after they had all mustered todohim homage.
Jeff is a disappointing person. His appearance is somewhat
against him, and he is eternally chewing gum. Hisjawsare
at work in every scene. With a quid of the pestilent
Yankee notion in his mouth, and supported by a couple
of other ugly pugs, he is depicted as receiving a levee.
He just leers and chews and gives his hand to the visiting
pilgrims, who seemingly come along to tell him that he
carries all their hopes and dollars. And then
the fight ! Barring the final knocking out, which
comes as an inexplicable finale in this severely
edited picture, nothing has been left in which
could go to show how completely Jeff was over-
matched. The tenth or eleventh round—in the picture
—concludes with a rather furious onslaught by Jeff, the
artistic retaliations by the nigger having been cut out. In
the twelfth round Johnson actually downed Jeff, but the
picture gives no hint of that occurrence. In fact, so many
evidences of Johnson’s superiority have been eliminated
from the pictorial narrative that the climax seems ab-
surdly sudden. A cynical showman who knows the
tricks of the cinematograph trade remarked that the
showing of the negative was a mistake. They ought to
have shown the positive, which would have reversed the
order of color and represented a white man as triumph-
ing over a black.
“Ho”: A touching faith in the sanctity of the mar.
riage tie is displayed by certain of the characters in
“The Bad Girl of the Family,” who is misbehaving her-
self nightly at Melbourne Princess’s. The villain has
long pursued the heroine with unwelcome protestations,
and eventually he succeeds in having her young bloke
charged with murder, and sentenced to cessation of life.
The heroine and the villain (both of whom have a habit
of wandering around the prison in a promiscuous way)
visit him, and the latter announces that when he (the
hero) is rotting in lime he (the villain) will compel the
heroine to commit unmitigated matrimony. After much
noisy emotion, the hero is struck with a brilliant idea.
Calling the prison chaplain (who also happens to be wan-
dering around), he announces that he desires to wed the
worried lady. The ceremony is performed on the quick-
lunch principle. The chaplain tells the girl: “You are
now the wife of convict 56478A3X, who to-morrow will be
hanged,” and the heroine turns upon the baffled malefac-
tor and informs him, in deep-chested tones, that, as she
is a respectable married woman, he cannot harm her
now. Whereupon the evildoer retires “ down stage ”in
an unfastened condition to chew his moustache.
“Stargazer”: “The Gay Gordons’’asserts with the
emphasis of a 100-ton gun the grave need of an up-to-date
horticultural expert to supervise the culling of the
chestnuts for stage-plays. There are comedians here who
scatter gags as familiar in Melbourne as King O’Malley’s
hat, or the feet of little Willie Judkins. Japes that
were chafed threadbare in last year's pantomimes are
doped and put into circulation again ; wheezes as old as
Noah, and better known than his ark, are impudently
offered as good goods fresh from the vine. “The
Arcadians” got as far as billing an expert in corns. What
I’m earnestly looking for now is the time when every
management will have its expert in nuts.
“Bleriot”: Went the other day to Sydney’s first
aviation “carnival” and enjoyed myself terribly. There
were all of 50 people present when I arrived. They were
watching: several men under the direction of a lictle chap
mauling- a dirty-looking balloon about the ground. At
the end of half an hour the balloon was hoisted on a
clothes line between two posts, and 60 minutes later the
little chap roared through a megaphone that the hot-air
bag would ascend at 4.30 p.m., whereupon the balloon was
dragged down, and subsequently—-several hours subse-
quently—was replaced by an even dirtier one. By-and-b.ve
a start was made to inflate it; and still more by-and-bye
the little chap announced that, to allow those who had
arrived at 1 p.m. to see the show, the balloon would not
ascend until 5.30 p.m. Such is the inconsider-
ateness of human nature, this actually annoyed
them. After another long wait, the little chap
roared “ Let ’er go ! ” With the majesty of a one-winged
duck, the balloon rose fully 20ft., toppled over, righted
itself and waddled through the air straight for the Moore
Park rubbish tip. The little chap had floated off early.
The next item was announced after a pause of about 16
weeks. It took the form of two canvas wings; but as six
men and a boy staggered under its weight, it was clear
that it was no flier. By this time nearly everybody had
died from old age; still, a few remained to see the
“great monoplane trial.” This monopole turned out to
be a biplane, with an engine that refused to be aw'akened
out of its sleep : so, as the only thing at the “ carnival ”
that appeared likely to fly was time, I set out for home.
When I arrived I found that the old house had been
burned down the previous year.
“Snell”: Hughip Ward, on the occasion of his final
appearance as an acting-manager at Adelaide, told the
people he was joining the firm that had done more than
any other for the artistic side of the stage in Australia.
“Mr. Williamson’s life has been beautiful,”he said. “He
has assisted stage art and artists by every means in his
power.” I remember that, in a recent heated discussion
on the quality of the Australian stage and the duties of
Australian managers, it was Mr. Williamson’s representa-
tive who scoffed with Pagan ribaldry at artistic preten-
sions in theatrical managers, brandished the obvious
truism that running theatres is a business, and de-
clared that the first and fundamental principle of
a business is to make money, and the next principle is to
go on making money. But. your gifted management
always has two voices—the one which declares its own
virtues and its claim upon public gratitude as an artist
devoted to art for art’s sake, and the other (used in press
disputations only) which agserts the entrepreneur’s
sterling common-sense and Sound business aptitude in
giving the public what it wants, regardless of the clamor
of sore-heads who demand literary merit and dramatic
art and all that sort of visionary humbug.
“Spotlight”: “Theos,” in his par. (B. 29/12/TO) on
“ The Whip,” shows that his long suit isn’t a sense of
fairness, and that he is a little reckless with the facts.
That the Sydney papers are doing their best to boost the
production, and that it needs it, is an opinion not deserv-
ing of any great respect. The daily criticisms were not as
glowing as the success. Never before has drama at the Wil-
liamson tariff taken so strong a hold of the public- Sales
of seats have been shut down by manager Goodman
before curtain rise at every Sydney performance.
“ Theos ’’ mentions that the dailies chronicled “ cheer-
ing” on the first night, whereas, to his hearing, it was
“jeering.” He ought to consult both aurist and oculist.
I have searched the notices and can find nothing warmer
than “applause.” And there certainly was plenty of
that. Furthermore, it was a wonderfully smooth per-
formance, the scene-shifters handling their hundred tons
or so of Coleman with amazing alacrity. Judging from
what “ Theos ” saw and heard that didn’t occur, he ought
to change the brand.
“Stargazer”: Wonderful is the wit of the “local
allusion!” There are many bright sallies in Melb.’s
Yuletide shows —brilliant references to Doctor Billy
Maloney’s hat (which, by the way, is quite an ordinary
hat, and seems to have got mixed up with the cady of
Kink O’Malley), caustic and biting interpolations of the
name of one Judkins, scintillant epigrams about Swan-
ston-street dust, and poignant jests about the slowness
of Melbourne’s trams. These have for many years been
the themes of the mad wags who sling Australian cham-
pagne and southern sunniness into pantomimes imported
for Antipodean consumption. The method is simplicity
itself. Your low-comedian merely “ ups and sez ”
“Judkins,” or “Melbourne dust,” or “slow as a St.
Kilda tram,” and the thing’s done ; the groundlings roar
and the galleries howl. I explain this with the theory
that laughing at certain matters becomes a habit.
Theatregoers laugh at the things mentioned simply be-
cause it is habitual. The mention is not funny in any
way, nobody is really amused, but the crowd cachinnates
over the word “ Judkins ” just as it would sneeze over
pepper or cry over an onion.
IWA, THE MAORI CONTRALTO.
Iwa, the 18-year-old girl with the voice, who is with
Maggie Papabura’s Maori village combination at Sydney,
en route to London and further, is going to make a con-
cert reputation if she is looked after. She was “dis-
covered” by Richard Seddon at the Bluff, and was a very
small child in those days. Since then she has been en-
couraged by various musical celebrities who have visited
Maoriland, and has had considerable polish put upon her
vocal property by Miss Macintosh, a well-known Dunedin
teacher. What is needed now is additional polish and
cultivation, but the chances of obtaining it in a travelling
Maori pa are as remote as the chances of finding a
brewery in Gehenna.
Max Maxwell has commenced his tour round Austral-
asia with dramatic goods very successfully at His
Majesty’s, Brisbane. Manager George Portus writes that
since Boxing Night he has personally offered apologies to
2153 persons who were unable to gain admittance to the
show, and spent all his salary to cool down individuals
. who threatened to have his life for not providing seats
after 7 p.m.

“Minko”: Touching on stage villans. Why are these
unfortunate and unpopular adjuncts invariably compelled
to massacre numerous unnecessary victims before they
nearly .-•ccomplish their base designs? If a horse is to he
nobbled, the virtuous lady’s marriage lines purloined, or
the family jewels appropriated, several minor cha-
racters must be done to death, violently and
gruesomelv, although the pampered hero has
no difficulty in escaping every time. I assert, from
experience, that there is no earthly reason to
indulge in promiscuous slaughter before you effect a rob-
bery or forge a will. I’ve robbed scores of banks, faked
several missing wills, passed myself off successfully as the
heir to a title and large estates, shot the moon from a
few large hotels, and burnt terraces of mansions with the
people in them, disembowelled lOpolicemen, and scuttled
a ship for the insurance ; yet I never had to slay an irrele-
vant funny man or a heavy but well-meaning parent
while rushing headlong on the path of infamy. I just
killed the people I wanted to, and let the others slide.
No, the stage isn’t true to nature—not by a tall heap.
The Christchurch (M.L.) City Council is making
another attempt to stop the early-door scheme for drag-
ging an extra bob out of the theatrical customer. Last
.year a bylaw was passed providing that all seats must be
numbered off and exhibited on a plan at each entrance
door ; also, that when required to do so by the Council,
the manager must arrange for the sale of tickets
at some approved place at least eight hours before
the start of ihe show. And even if the purchaser of a
ticket didn’t roll up to collect his seat before 9 p.m., or
even 9.46 J p.m., his seat had to be supplied on demand.
The idea has worked all right in several big theatres in
London, N’York and elsewhere ; but it didn’t work in
Christchurch, mostly because managers or lessees didn’t
want it to work, and the bylaw had no punch behind it.
Now, however, by special arrangement with the Muni-
cipal Corporations Act, 1908, a default fine up to £2O has
been tagged on, and a dose of that physic is expected to
cure even the worst case of stubbornness.
“, Kodak ” : Somebody gave me a five-shilling ticket for
a concert in the Old Cannibals’ Association Hall ; and
having nothing better to do, I went. I found the place
without the assistance of a tramguard who didn’t know
it, or of a policeman who had never heard of it. A lonely
doorkeeper took my ticket from me and I was shown to a
seat immediately behind a stout lady, who was partly in
her evening dress. There were about 60 people scattered
round, trying not to look lonely amongst the polished
jungle of empty chairs. About, 8 o’clock, a girl dived
from behind the scenes and scuttled to the keyboard of
the triangular piano. An angrv-looking man with a
fiddle in one hand and a bow in the other followed her on
to the platform, and, facing the handful of abashed
people, sneered at them. I felt inclined to rise and
apologise for not being more numerous ; but, instead,
cowered behind the stout lady and waited. The violinist
laid the head of the fiddle on his neck, and, holding- its
one wooden leg with his left hand, started to make it
scream by sawing its chest. I am fond of melody, but I do
not care for difficult exercises; so I let the angry man
escape without applauding him. Then another young
woman with feathers in her hair appeared, and, sitting
at the piano, chattered prettily, in best high school style,
through five cream-puff recitations. Two of them were
ancient friends, and one was in German or Choctaw.
Everyone tried to look as if they understood
every word of it. After that a young man sang
an unusually long song, and a tall young woman
sang an unusually sad one. Another cream-puff
recitation by the lady with feathers in her hair followed.
Then there was an interval without refreshments. The
second part of the programme bore a strong resemblance
to the first, and the whole show expired somewhere in
the neighborhood of 10 o’clock, when the audience—in-
cluding the sheltering stout lady—faded down the stairs
and helped to fill a passing tram. I know of no more un-
comfortable or futile thing than the high-priced evening
entertainment of garden-party standard in an obscure
hall which can only be found by the determined ex-
plorer. The artists are always infuriated at the smallness
of the audience, and express their sentiments by making
faces at the piano-lady. The audience is horrified at the
waste of empty chairs, and panic-stricken at the grimaces,
and tries to atone by nervously applauding anything and
everything. This merely makes the artists make more
faces and talk at the piano-lady out of the corners of
their mouths. At various times I have made heroic
efforts to overhear these asides ; but my hearing is not as
good as it was, and I am reduced to making wild guesses.
I do wish, though, that these clever entertainers who ap-
pear at the Old Cannibals’ Association Hall would look
more kindly on the faithful few who neglect to be absent
from their shows. I have been sneered at so often for
not being a whole row or tribe that I am getting dis-
couraged.

SEMIRAMIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The story of Semiramis has been bioscoped.— Trade
announcemen t.
What liberties these picture-fellows take !
They range the world, through Arctic zone and Tropic ;
And even ancient history they fake —
They follow Moses round and round, and make
Semiramis a story bioscopic.
Some say she was descended from the gods,
And others that her origin was shady ;
So scandal goes about with winks and nods :
But, light of love or goddess, what’s the odds?
At any rate she was a Perfect Lady.
She was a queen Assyrian—passing fair,
Concerning whom more kings than one had quarrels ;
And please remember (should she raise your hair)
The habits of 1200 B.c. were
Quite different from twentieth-century morals.
But whether goddess, or—the other thing,
Semiramis grew up extremely pretty ;
And she could dance the Babylonian fling,
And chansonettes Assyrian she could sing—
Songs highly-spiced and most uncommon witty.
Now when we find a woman just like that,
To any way she chooses she’ll incline us ;
And so, where’er she made her habitat,
Semiramis had monarchs in her hat,
And by-and-bye she nobbled old King Ninus.
A lusty old campaigner was King Nine ;
He could have made a meal of all your wowsers 1
And what an oath he muttered o’er his wine
When this fair woman, whom they called divine,
Came out to give him battle—wearing trousers!
King Ninus won the tournament, of course,
And took her home, and kissed her oft and clammily ;
But soon would thank his stars for a divorce,
Because the grey mare proved the better horse.
The missus wore the trousers in that family !
Beside his couch some evenings she would sing,
And make old Ninny cut such funny capers,
Till, in a doting mood, the ancient king,
Gave her his sceptre and his signet-ring,
And quite a bundle of official papers.
Then came a night when Ninus couldn’t sleep,
Lulled by’ the usual minstrelsy 7, or hear it;
His head was on no cushion soft and deep,
But lay inglorious on the rubbish-heap,
And —funny thing—his body wasn’t near it.
Up rose Semiramis, and donned the crown,
And looked Too Sweet in filigree and Tyrian,
And sent her heralds running through the town
To say, “Oh yes ! Oh yes ! Bow down ! Bow down,
Ye Babylonians! I'm the Queen Assyrian ! ”
And how she sent plain miscreants to death,
But gave to handsome criminals their pardons,
How men at sight of her drew quicker breath,
And how (for here each story differeth)
She built, or didn’t build, the Hanging Gardens ;
And how she conquered Egypt and the Nile,
And led stuffed elephants against the Injuns,
And how her son displayed a traitor's guile—
These things would take up space ; and all the while
The Editor is swearing red-hot vengeance !
Semiramis is dead : her pride lies low ;
Thus perish all diurnal things ; so be it !
But meditating on that long-ago,
Unless they’ve bowdletised the picture-show
I rather think I’d like to go and see it.
VICTORIA.

A STARRING FORTNIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ I haven’t seen your name, dear boj’,
On any recent play-bill—
You who could always find employ
At pantomime-time. Say, Bill,
Why is it managers are coy ?
Don’t say you’re ‘ out,’ I pray, Bill ! ”
Bill, after he had quaffed, replied :
“ A special fortnight, laddie,
I’ve just put in ” —he swelled with pride—
“ Of ‘ shops ’ it was the daddy.
I as a Star quite satisfied
My boss— and he is faddy !
“A cast of one the drama had,
And I was IT at last, sir.
The * run ’ was short—it makes me sad,
To think the glory’s past, sir.
But ” —here he quaffed some more—“ egad,
It was a splendid cast, sir ! ”
“What show?” I asked. “ ’Twas some new biand,”
Bill said. “Lor’, can’t you tumble?
The shop was Bloggs, the draper’s, and
Show me the pro. who’d grumble
In his front windows there to stand,
A Santa Claus with whiskers grand—
That part just suits your ’umble ! ”

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
Melbourne’s long-promised concert hall
has reached the stage of definite announcement.
.T. and N. Tait are the prospective lessees. The
hall is to be the ground floor and two storeys of
an eight-storev building on the site of the old
Presbyterian Assembly Hall in Collins-street,
nearly opposite the Athenaeum ; so the two halls
in the Athenaeum building will be considerably
assisted as show places by the attraction of crowds
to the tony establishment on the other side of the
road. When the up-to-Tait concert hall comes
into service, the ill-conditioned Town Hall will
be abandoned by music purveyors unless the
drowsy City Fathers do something towards making
it cosy. If the drafts were shut out by an inner
enclosure, the Town Hall might still retain its
place in the show market; but as Melbourne’s
principal concert hall it will cease to be when the
new building is completed. The Taits’ hall is to
have a foyer, 10yds. square, on the ground floor,
leading to the balcony, and the auditorium will
give a full view of the platform to 2000 seat
holders. The present idea is to call the place the
Auditorium, though that doesn’t bite the ear very
hard.
LOVE AND ITS LODGINGS.
SHE: “ Ah! Love in a cottage"
HE: “is a castle in the air"
Very few men die standing up, and the
perpendicular corpse is exceedingly rare. A Vic-
torian constable found one last week by the side
of the switchback White Horse-road, which alter-
nately climbs and coasts eastward from Melbourne.
The deceased was leaning thoughtfully on a bicycle
as if he was having a good rest after climbing a
stiff hill. It was evidently a case of overworked
heart. When he plays the bushranger, Death
generally intercepts the traveller at the bottom of
aslope; but on this occasion he quietly tracked
his victim along the undulating road with the
ominous name till the time came to gently end
• the lonely ride.
“ Yigihms ” : Though
the C. of E. Bishops and
Archbishops of Australia
have begun a crusade
against the maltreatment
of Black Brother, I
don’t believe they
know the size of the
job they have under-
taken, or how des-
perately it calls for
attention. In the
Northern Territory,
away from Palmers-
ton, almost every-
body who travels has
an attendant black
sister. The attrac-
tion of the white for
the black lady is re-
markable. One East
Kimberley station
manager had 17
“attendants,” and
most station hands
have two or three.
As regards swapping
the girls: On the
stations the manager
names the wages to
an applicant, and a
gin is thrown in. A
man who travels
without an aide-de-
camp is looked upon
with suspicion as
either a wowser or a
person who has
lately committed
murder. At one time

“ Hermes” : Apropos the N.S.W. Govern-
ment’s intention to build railways where and
when possible by
day labor, and the
S. ’M. Herald’s
pained surprise
in consequence.
Writer was once
in a train on
which the driver
had got drunk on
boiled Benedict-
ine and gone tem-
porarily mad. Be-
lieving that the
fiend was after
the manager, the
driver attempted
to save him by
putting the train
at the top speed
he could get out
of the engine,
which was about
54 miles an hour.
The chief en-
gineer was on board. Immediately the situa-
tion transpired he inquired : “How was this
line built —by our own men or a contractor?”
“By a contractor, sir.” “Then we’re done!”
said the engineer; and there and then, re-
gardless of appearances, he slipped down on his
knees and prayed—prayed openly and hard.
I’ve never believed in contract railway build-
ing since then. We got through ; but that
was, no doubt, owing to the fact that the line had
been reconstructed by the railway men on day
wages.

ITS EFFECT ON WILLIAM.
THE WOWSEBESS: “ The curses of drink are
terrible! ”
THE EVERYDAY FEMALE: “ Yes, some of ’em do ;
but my Bill always gets speechless"
Port Adelaide had a mysterious Christmas
tragedy. William Battye was a man of 32, in
business with his brother, happily married, and
seemingly without a care in the world. At 10.30
p.m. he was chatting to his brother on his own
doorstep. At 1 a.m. his house was on fire. The
brigade found nothing much burnt except the bed,
and on that were Battye’s wife and child, with their
heads battered in—apparently with a lemonade
bottle. In the morning, the house-father himself
was found in the docks near by, badly burnt, nearly
nude, and dead by drowning. There the matter
rests. That any outsider killed the three of them
is unlikely. To say that Battye went mad and
did the deed—just because he had once had
deiigue fever in Queensland, and with no evidence
—would be (said the coroner) to “ introduce chaos
into all criminal proceedings.” So it was recorded
officially that all three are dead.
A very remarkable affair in the way of
letter-writing was the epistle of Tommy Hoy,
who hanged himself in the lockup at Albury
(N.S.W.) last week. The document commenced
“Dear Gaol.”
* * *
What are the wowsers doing ? At a wood-
chopping contest at Horsham (Vic.) one of the
backers of the winning axeman rushed to pat the
hero on the back when he was delivering his final
chop. The interloper’s right arm got into the
line of stroke and was nearly chopped off just
below the shoulder. Yet not a wowser has been
heard agitating for the suppression of wood-
chopping matches. Has Judkins lost his punch,
or what ?
* « * *
“Rooinek”: The South African Govern-
ment did for Fisher and his party what
the Australian Government could not do
for any party of distinguished visitors. That
is, it provided them with a couple of
sumptuous corridor saloons, in which they
were able to enjoy all the comforts of a good
hotel and travel at will over every province of the
Union. For one thing, the Australian Govern-
ment doesn’t own the railways ; and for another
thing, the breaks of gauge would necessitate a
number of trains instead of one, with continual
turning-out and re-arranging of luggage, etc.
Fisher’s outfit included dining, sleeping and
observation compartments, and a cook and steward
were told off specially to accompany them. With
Fowlds, of M.L., and Ambrose Pratt and M‘Cay,
Australian journalists, the Prime Minister lived
on that train for a fortnight, and seemed reluctant
to leave it. I heard Fisher at a banquet inviting
S’Africans to come to Australia, and assuring
them of a warm Australian welcome. But, as an
Australian, I almost blushed to think of the rail-
ways we should show them, with their multi-
tudinous gauges and their antiquated equipment.
# *
An unexpected development the other
day was the beginning of an agitation among the
people of Queanbeyan (N.S.W.) to have the
Federal capital territory enlarged so as to include
their town. If adjacent N.S.W. towns begin to
struggle desperately for inclusion in the Federal
area, that area may become a very large place one
of these days. The movement is at least under-
standable. The area inside the Federal capital
boundary will be subsidised by the money of
Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria, Tasmania, S.’A.
and Westralia. It will be the one glad spot in
Australia which will have six States to help
make its roads and bridges, and to fix up its
drains and water supply, and to pay for beautifi-
cation and adornment. If King O’Malley builds
the City Beautiful about which he speaks so
eloquently’ through his nose, and sometimes
through his hat, there will presumably be a great
cleaning up and repairing of any adjacent towns
that are included in the territory, to make them
correspond with the metropolis, and that is
always a pleasant circumstance. Being inside
the territory will mean having tramway communi-
cation with the capital, while being outside will
mean getting there with the aid of Dobbin in the
old spring cart. And there are other possible
advantages which Queanbeyan doubtless per-
ceives, so that settlement is resolved to get in
early.
HEAVILY HANDICAPPED.
POPPER: “Hurry up, Amelia! If we run we
might catch it.”
Melbourne City Council is an intelligent
body—one might almost call it intellectual. It
took barely two years to draw up a set of by-laws
dealing with taxi-cabs, and then it provided that
the hire of the vehicles should beat the rate of Is.
a mile, or 6s. per hour. This was done to suit the
vested interests of the owners of horse-drawn
cabs ; but as the whole essence and advantage of
a taxi-cab is that you pay by the mile, the taxi-
owners simply decline to put their carriages on
the ranks at all. Which was probably just what
the antediluvian councillors wanted.
A THANKSGIVING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
It is over, thank God, it is over—
From the Ham they have sliced the last shred.
And the bone has been given to Rover,
Who sleeps with it under my bed.
They have done with the paste and the puffing 1 ,
With the pudding and jelly and muck ;
They have thrown out the last of the stuffing 1 ,
And buried the end of the duck.
The Christmas Bush, dried up and broken,
’Neath the gasolier mournfully waves.
It reminds me of some loving 1 token
One sees on the summit of graves ;
And the flies wander drearily o’er it,
And murmur a dirge as they go.
Vile emblem-of Feed—l abhor it;
For I know —miserere !—I know.
Little Willie’s been “ salted ” and “ castoreci ” ;
The bottle-oh man came to-day,
And the cargo he manfully mastered,
Though he staggered a hit by the way ;
And the heart of me leaps in its gladness,
And the soul of me chortles in glee,
For they’ve done with the Season of Madness,
And —I’m having grilled steak for my tea !
N.S.W.
HORACE HALLORAN.
A HURRIED FAREWELL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I lost all acute consciousness of self and surroundings.
The Stone Age savage rose and demanded blood —Papke’s
blood. Whenever Smith hit him in the face—which was
fairlv often —I gasped with pleasure, and the harder the
blow the greater the joy it caused. A good heavy thump
on that hard, white body also provoked pleasurable sen-
sations in me, but the smashing face blows were the best,
... We had been back in the Age of the Flint Axe, under
the naked, everlasting stars, in touch with the root of
human nature, if only for three-quarters of an hour, and
we emerged refreshed to face conventions, civilisation
and electric trams with a new courage.— Dulcie Learner,
writing of the Smith-Papke fight, in Sydney Sunday Sun.
“ I am unworthy 1 ” cried the youth,
And gathered up his roses.
“ Dear, hear me tell the shameful truth 1
In no graveyard reposes
A victim to my six-ounce glove !
So there can be no bridal 1
Farewell ! My tendencies, dear love,
Were never homicidal !
I’ve never thumped my fellow-man,
Or shed the vital fluid !
Forgive me, darling, if you can !
Had I but been a Druid,
Or something of the Age of Mud
(Of which Jack London proses),
I could have brought you foeman’s blood
Instead of sweets and roses !
N.S.W.

After the crushing defeat Mr. Deakin received at the
last election, very little has been heard from his oily
tongue, but the Macrocephalous instinct has agiin given
birth to a monstremata in the shape of a manifesto.
And from The Bulletin’s imagination (5/l/’ll): —
Lumper : Wot the'ell's th’meanin’ov that, Bill?
Coalie : Dun no. Serves him right, though.
* * * *
How hollow is this Empire talk—this
prattle about dear old England being ready to
help us out of any war-trouble that may hit us.
from motives quite unconnected with the British
moneylender! The officers of the Gibraltar,
which convoyed the two Australian destroyers
from the Cold Country, had turned down the
“ Colonial ” officers of the Yarra and Parramatta
long before Australia was sighted, and at Broome
the position was acute. The Gibraltar’s men and
the Australians fraternised gladly enough, but
the British officers had no time for the Aus-
tralians. Yet any cheap politician who is pre-
pared to yap about brotherhood and the beauty of
the love borne us by the dear old Mother Country
can have his prattle reported to the length of a
column or more.
* * * *
The promise of a cheap fish supply for
Melbourne is hazy at present. A syndicate, it is
said, has bought a vessel which will trail its
trawling gear “ in waters east of Tasmania,” and
have a depdt ashore for the supply of fish direct
to the public. But the syndicate didn’t need to
make a commercial start by catching its own fish.
It might have got tons of fish from men already
in the business. What Melbourne wants isn’t so
much more fishermen, or even more fish, as a
simple system of distribution from depots; and
the purchase of a trawler before a new system of
supply and demand has been established seems
like tackling the problem from the wrong end.

The concert-hall which is to go up for the
Tints in Collins-street, Melbourne, will replace
the old Presbyterian Assembly Hall. That will
be the second “ degradation ” the place has had.
It was formerly St. Enoch’s Church, and has the
pretence of a bluestone front and a white spire,
though the hidden back is only mean brick. The
front and spire were stuck on in rivalry of the
Scots Church opposite. Over half a century ago
Melbourne had three rival sets of fiercely-battling
Presbyterians. There was Hetherington’s Scots
Church ; and Ramsay’s St. Enoch’s, which stood
for the “United ” church party ; and Chalmers’s,
at East Melbourne, where Cairns held up the
“Free ” Church. Cairns was a particularly bitter
enemy of “Rome”; but the time came when
“Rome” bought his church, pulled it down and
built the stones into a convent hard by—which
was a sweet revenge ! The three Presbyterian
sects in the end combined, and St. Enoch’s was
turned into a mere Assembly Hall.
The Melbourne Tramway Octopus is glar-
ing at the lately-born Association of Tramway
Men, and saying things under its breath. It has,
for example, issued a circular, intimating'
in velvety language that the company
may discontinue its habit of paying half
the members’ subscriptions—a whole
per week—to the employees’ Benefit
(Society if members of the new organisa-
tion are allowed to remain inthe older
society. From which it will be gathered
that the company is waiting to aim a
clod at the new combination as well as a
brick at the principle of unionism. Still,
the men may even resist a bribe of
per week when they remember that the
Arbitration Court will probably argue
that, if the poor co. could afford to put
all that money into the men’s left-hand
pocket, it can also afford to put it into
their right-hand pocket. No Arbitration
body is at all likely to allow men to be
penalised for submitting themselves to its
jurisdiction; wherefore the men need
worry much less about the brick than the
co. itself very reasonably might. Some-
how, nearly every brick aimed by the co.
nowadays turns itself into a boomerang
before it has finished its job, and bashes
the co. dreadfully.
* * * *
The Council of Churches in Mel-
bourne has started a crusade against the
practice of conducting “frivolous conversa-
tions ”on the Sabbath. It is not an example
of charity beginning at home, for the agita-
tion is not aimed at incompetent parsons at
all ; its object is merely to minimise the
Sunday use of telephones. The Council of
Churches asked Act ng-Premier Hughes for
“a silent Sunday”—which is precisely what a
large majority of Melbourne residents are
pleading for at the hands of the pastors and
spiritual masters composing the Council of
Churches. When the church bells have been
rung off in the interests of a silent Sunday, the
parsons may with some reason ask Hughes to
silence the telephone bell.
* * * *
Departed from Port Jackson on Christ-
mas Eve, H.M.S. Gibraltar with a shipment of
time-expired ratings who have had their allow-
ance of “ colonial experience.” There was much
lamentation afloat and ashore that the departing
Jacks were not allowed a few more hours in port
so that they might spend Christmas with the
girls they’ve left behind them. But prayers and
entreaties were alike unavailing. The movements
of the British navy are made to suit the British
navy, and all the festivals of Christendom will not
serve as an anchor for even one gunboat when the
sailing orders pipe to capstan. So at midday on
Christmas Eve the Gibraltar dropped down the
bay with the homeward-bound pennant stream-
ing on the breeze, and the tears of Emily and Lou
streaming on the quay.
“ Silas Snell ” : I went out into the wild-
woods on Boxing Day with a large party, ham-
pered with loads and loaded with hampers, seek-
ing bush experience, snake stories, cow adven-
tures, and other “ Aboriginalities.” We got into
the thick of the nearest thing to pristine wilder-
ness that a os. Bd. railway journey from Melbourne
can supply, and discovered a previous picnic
party, much mote hampered than ourselves, and
fraternised. The other push had a swagger accent
and English ideas. I noticed something like a
hag of spuds leaning against a tree, and investi-
gated. Before heaven, it was a bag of coal! In-
quiries elicited this delicious heart throb : “Oh,
yes, we were determined to be real Australian
bushman, you know. That is some coal for boil-
ing the billy ! ”
“ 10-2-4 ” on the Silence of the Wowser :
The wowser has a strange faculty for missing the right
moment to sound the tocsin of protest.
The other month there was some talk of showing the
Jeffries-Johnson fight pictures in Sydney. The world’s
verdict, as expressed by such eminently righteous papers
as London Times and New York that
there was nothing debasing about the flicker view of the
Reno disturbance. The dim presentment of a stout,
elderly-looking Caucasian wrestling with a large, affable
nigger, and then subsiding noiselessly on the ground,
as a preliminary to fading right off the sheet,
was not reckoned likely to stir any baser passions.
Yet, at the prospect of that innocuous spectacle, Wow-
serdom arose as one snuffler and protested so hard that
the Reno picture was held over till public interest in it
was moribund. After the Burns-Johnson fight—a fairly-
conducted and by no means ultra-gory fray—the person
with the monkey-shave yelped so deafeningly that the
Wade Government hastened to make regulations which,
according to Chief Secretary Wood, were destined to
make fights as bloodless and edifying as golf matches.
During the past couple of months the Sydney Stadium
has been exploiting a team of Yankee pugilists. With
one doubtful exception the imports can’t box for sour
fruit. Their strong suits are an animal-like capacity for
taking punishment, a wild swing that is liable to finish up
anywhere from the surrounding atmosphere to the pit of
the referee’s stomach, and a talent for “pointing” that
amounts to genius. Papke, the alleged middle-weight, is
a heavy-weight. “ Cyclone ” Thompson, who got a
match with Hughie Mehegan under the pretence that he
was a light-weight, and who smashed plucky little
Unholz into unconsciousness in what the Stadium humor-
ists described as “a light-weight contest,” turned out
when publicly weighed at the ringside to he a heavy-
weight. Papke fouled Dave Smith. Another of the
tribe, disapproving of “Snowy ” Baker’s refereeing, used
language which would have earned a mere wharf lumper
a term in gaol. And so on.
As a result of all ttiese things, the Stadium has de-
veloped into a raging inferno of blanky wild words and
ready stoush. Sydney Telegraph, which so virtuously
denounced the Burns-Johnson upheaval, describes how
“ for 19 rounds, Land and Thompson fought like tiger-
cats in a rage.” And it dwells almost lovingly on the
hoots, the yells and the promiscuous disturbances. Yet,
up to the time of writing, the astounding wowser has nor.
said a word.

floated by—
Ileav’n help the little Buttercup, she Kissed the Butter-

Who nOw exhales k perfume rare to bless huh as he goes ;

THE FALL OF MAN: REVISED VERSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Church, having taken to food reform and teetot.al-
ism, grows very discontented with the orthodox account
of the fall of Adam. One Melb. anti-meat-eating minister
declares his belief that it was not an apple Eve gave
Adam, but a mutton chop. Another, whose particular
fad is teetotalism, says he is sure it was not an apple in
its simple, natural state that brought abodt the fall of
man. Our own belief is than it. was not a serpent, but a
worm, that led to the original sin.
The moving tale of Adam’s fate
And Eve’s profane excursion
Is not entirely up-to-date ;
We need a modern version.
Eve, Adam and the Devil, they
Get their deserts ; but here to-day
In every church and chapel,
The texts undoubtedly convey
Injustice to the apple.
The apple is a guileless thing
When ripe and round and mellow;
We can’t believe that it would bring
A fall to any fellow.
Eve’s name w'as Hebe, and her plan
Was much more deep and sinful than
The one the Scriptures lead in.
In fact, the wily Devil ran
A private still in Eden.
Eve took the apples from the tree,
And to old Nick she hied her ;
They brewed in secret two or three
Fat little kegs of cider.
Eve gave to Adam (so we think)
A pipkin straight, and he did drink.
So apples, worm and madam
In fatal combination link
To fix the fall of Adam !

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Chloe,” to “Celia Lloyd” (B. 24/11/TO) :
Re the “ dirty, dangerous hit of Nor’-West Australia,”
let me say a word to “Celia” about the pearler, of
whom she knows so little, and deny absolutely and
emphatically that the pearler is wanting in grit, and that
he lets the Japs run the industry. The .Japashore does
run the business houses of Broome ; afloat, both Japs
and Malays are pretty quickly taught their places when
teaching is required.
The Jap or Malay diver gets his billet because he knows
all there is to know about sailing, and shell gathering,
and the best grounds for conducting operations. The
white lugger-master, in many cases, has had no previ-
ous experience of the sea, but is, and has to be,'master of
that boat, and pretty quickly lets his diver and tender
know that their business is to pick grounds and dive ;
and in nine cases out of ten the diver is as anxious as the
lugger-master to have discipline. Trouble, no doubt,
begins if the divers’ sailing orders are unduly interfered
with; and such interference is most unbusiness-like.
But let either diver or tender exceed his authority, and I
guess there isn’t a white man at the game who doesn’t
know how to give the offenders a set-back.
Now that the pearling industry is getting by degrees
away from the Jewish and Jap firms which hoped to run
the business (and did for a while), it is only the gritty men
who keep at it. And while there are men at the game
like the Chamberlain Bros., not forgetting good old Cap-
tain Lee, Gregory Bros., Francis, Owen and others, don’t
talk of grit being wanted, because it isn’t fair to men
who are too busy to say much for themselves. I also
have lived in a pearling community, and though I allow
none of those I have named are reaching out for the
halos of saints, they are the men who will be wanted if it
ever comes to a struggle with the Japs. And, what is
more, I believe the Japs would have been in long ago if
it hadn’t been for the effect Wrought on the Jap mind by
the steadiness and cool couratre of the pearlers. So here’s
niy best of good wishes to them, and a lot of sympathy in
the losses and sorrows which they take like men, and
good, gritty Australian men at that.

FAIR AMERICAN: "Nay, reckon you beat us on rabbits.”
“Puketapu”: I was moving down the
west coast of the South Island of M,L. when I
met the Ca.sual Gravedigger. It was a warm day,
and as I leaned over the fence of the old, dilapi-
dated burying-place I observed a whirl of smoke
rise from behind a near-by mound. Thinking
that the Devil’s kitchen chimney must be on fire,
I walked round to offer my assistance, only
to find myself confronted with a wizened
old party who was splitting a wooden
head-slab with a spade and feeding a
fire upon which rested a blackened billy.
“G’day,” he remarked casually; “yer jest in
time for a swig ev tea. Y’ can’t beat tea when
y’ve bin sinkin’ boles like that one” (pointing to a
gaping grave) “in wehther like this.” He stirred
the fire with his foot as he spoke, and I moved
back from a shower of sparks. “ Yes,” he said in
answer to my movement, “that’s the worst ev
that d d totara ; it sparks like blazes. Still,
y’ can’t git nothin’ better fer coffins. That there
bit ev timber we’re a burnin’ was put round ole
Bill Jackson Gord knows’ow many years ergo, and
it’s as sound now as the day it wuz lammed home.
For why did I split it up? Well, it wuz handy,
and it ain’t no manner o’ good to Bill. He don’t
want no firin’ where ’e’s gone ; not if I know ’im.”
After having a pannikin of tea—and it didn’t
taste any the worse for the way it was brewed—l
asked the casual sexton how long totara would
last underground. “ Now yer aistin’ something,”
was his answer. “It’s ’aid to say, but I reckon
a good coffin ev that there wood in. a
well-dug ’ole oughter larst a bloke till
tli’ Day ev Judgment. Sometimes I think
that there’ll be a nice few corpses will miss tli’ ’bus
owin’to not being able to git outer their cases.”
Just then I saw a dismal little procession winding
slowly down the dusty incline. The Casual Grave •
digger saw it at the same time, and, rising from
the mound on which he was reclining, he stamped
the fire out and scattered the ashes. Then he
swilled the tea-dregs round the bottom of the
billy and threw them into the newly-carved grave.
“ Here comes th’ larst of pore ole ’Arry Clarke,”
he said ; “ Vs got a fine day for it, ain’t ’e?”

“Agricola”: How about this for bush
surgery? Picked up a defunct magpiejl who
had been experimenting with an overdose of
phosphorus. On examination I found that
his off-side leg had been broken, as by a
rabbit trap. The break had been patched up and
skilfully splinted* or rather spliced, by means
of feathers tightly twisted round the leg, and
glued on by the clotting of the blood. The
feather bandages bad been provided by the bird
itself, and had been plucked, from the longer
breast feathers, which were pliant enough to be
twisted round the leg easily. The wounded limb
was twice as big as the sound one, on account of
the quantity of wrapping. The bone was knitting
well, and in a,few weeks it would have been as
sound as ever. Probably the difficulty of obtain-
ing food had led the invalid to absorb the phos-
phorus bait, and I move a motion of want of con-
fidence in the hard luck that struck that feathered
surgeon.

forming ass isn’t wanted in a Zoo. It isn’t a

“Gip”: We had everything hut bees,
and we decided that the place would not be truly
rural till we had them too. So we got them, and
all was well—till the arrival of the ants to collect
the honey. I didn’t think there were so many
ants in the world. They came in millions,
blackening the hive with their massed bodies and
r.usliing the door in squads, heaps and battalions.
The bees threw down their tools, and the hum of
war died only when darkness called “ half-
time.” We tied kerosene-soaked rags round
the legs of the bees’ residence; but as soon
as they dried a bit up went the determined
black millions again. Then a man told
us that ants had never been known to cross tar ;
so we plastered it on regardless of cost. . By-and-
bye it dried also, and the ant commandant ordered
another assault. We tried rabbit fur. No good.
Finally, in desperation, we placed the legs of the
stand in four soup plates full of water, and at
present the ants are sitting round thinking. I’ll
bet they’ll get over somehow, even if a billion
or so have to be sacrificed to form a bridge for
the others. Somebody advised me to make a
paste of sugar and borax and place it above
the ants’ nest, and I’nr going ' to do it.
I’d make a paste of fried whiskers if
I thought it would do any good. Meanwhile can
any nice aboriginal man tell me something—a dead
sure-as-sure something—to keep the bees safe.
What with aphis, mildew, red spider, ants, straw-
berry beetle, pumpkin beetle, caterpillars, grubs,
grasshoppers and plain, spotted and variegated
bugs one can’t help agreeing with the other chap
who said that “life was just one thing after
another.”
“ Celiat Lloyd ” : long do you think
it will take for the Northern! River (N.S.W;)
cow-cocky to develop into a horned creature, wh6
will stand behind fences and nioo at you as you
pass? I reckon he is evoluting in that direction
at a fairly fast rate. Already he presents a bovine
appearance and a limited power of speech ; almost
the only words he can use freeh r are “cow,”
“bail-up,” “cream” and “butter,” though he
also says “How much?” very plainly. The
question with which I started C’tuses me many
sleepless nights, as some of the best friends I have
are working in butter, and I’m quite certain that,
when the cocky evolves horns, my friends—the
tester, at any rate—will be gored to death.
Taking the creamery job all round, it just means
313 underpaid days in the year, the 52 Sundays
being the only days off for the factory employee.
The working manager’s life is not worth mention-
ing; and for a straight-dinkum devil of a game
you may pass the tray to the tester. The cockies
roar and howl about losses and robbery, until the
much-worried tester, or whatever he is classed,
goes over the mark in trying to keep the peace.
The manager at the end of the rrionth then finds
that he has been paying for more butter than he
has been making, and he roars. Then the tester
brings boss-cocky to. his true level ; and, as the
boss-cocky is most likely a shareholder, and in
such case the manager’s boss, they roar at each
other, after which they both roar at the tester.

“ V. J’s. ” champion mean cuss :
He was an inveterate gambler ; but poverty and hard-
ship were his only winnings. His wife, like many
another plucky Australian woman, buckled to, and
managed to keep the family from the wolf. By the
strictest economy and self-sacrifice she had scraped to-
gether £4, and put it by to meet that emergency which
crowds upon us all just when we’re looking the other
way. But the mean, gambler knew of the little
hoarding, and his fingers itched t,o spin t lie money
on the wheel of CiVahce. As the annual race
day approached, he sorted out a few “dead
birds” and decided that by some means or other the
family nest egg must be placed under them to hatch.
He knew it was no good going to his wife to try and raise
the precious savings on the moribund-poultry proposal,
and he was almost giving up all hope of success when a
mean, dirty and brilliant, idea struck him. Having got
hold of a blank summons form, he filled it in with an
accusation against himself of smashing a shop window
while intoxicated, concluding with a claim for £4
damages. Forging a few bogus names, he showed the
document to his wife in consternation. “1 don’t know
what I’m to do,” he said. “ I haven’t a bean to settle it,
and it, means I will have to take it, our. in gaol. It’ll be a
disgrace for all of us. and I’ll lose my job.” Much dis-
tressed, and, like most women, horrified at the blue
paper, the wife handed over the few “hones” she had
salted away. Needless to add, the “dead birds” made a
meal of them.
