“ Pick ” : My entry for the friendly bush-
bird prize is the soldier, a little slate-colored,
yellow-gilled fellow, common in certain parts of
Monaro and the South Coast (N.S.W.) districts,
and owing bis name to bis pugnacity. He will
fight anything from a cockroach to a Tantanoola
tiger; and, unlike the thrush, fancied by recent
Abo. writers, be is not only interesting for bis
familiarity, but is useful to boot. The soldier
takes to a house, tent or gunyah as soon as one
appears on the landscape, and immediately
enters upon the duties of early alarm clock, and
sentinel to give warning when any snake,
gohanna, sundowner or other seventh day
creation is creeping upon the hen house. He
cleans up the table and floor after even' meal,
and. incidentally, will clean up the sugar basin ns
well, unless shooed outside with a broom. Hav-
ing an especial antipathy for any Cold Country
importation, he is regarded as a feathered blessing
by the Monaro farmers for the enthusiasm with
which he drives off English sparrows and star-
lings from grain and fruit crops. For these
reasons, the farmers and cockies quite cheerfully
put up with his troublesome and persistent
attempts to nest in their beards.

THE ARAB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The arching neck and fiery eye
l’roclaiiued the Arab in his veins,
He saw ih dreams a burnished sky
O’er palm-set wa tes of Asian plains, 1
Black Syrian tents with ragged fringe,
The sun-parched desert rolling free,
And far blue mountains that impinge
On lands of eldest mystery.
The languorous wind breathed low and ce sed,
It dropped its burden borne from far,
He snuffed the fragrance of the E ist
And whinnied to the western star.
Bush shadows hovered warm and brown
Filled with all voices of the night—
Some hand had left the sliprails down
No eye was near to mark his flight.
With quivering flank, and flying mane,
And freedom pricking like a spur,
II .■ galloped till the trackless plain
With dawn’s full pulse began to stir.
Behind him rose the fiery sun,
The grey lands glimmered like a sea—
No pause until the race be won
By the black tents of A rally.
The rocks of Hafiz, burnt and bain,,
Cast a lung shadow soft and grey ;
The kind-eye,tl black Bedawi marc
Whinnies to long-legged colts at play.
The red sun sank in clouds pf fire,
He stumbled on with rocking stride,
The deserts of his heart’s desire
Seemed spread before him waste and wide
Beneath the palms the waters gleam,
The airs are warm within the shade,
Where Arab steeds still love to dream
Of gallops in the border ('aid.
But when the desert stftrs a-bloom
Make all the skies of Hafiz gay,
With faint-heard whinny through the gloom
The.grey ghost-horses come to play.

“Itan” : Part of the expedition df bird
and beetle-hunters, which put in a few days on an
islet off Mackay (Q.), takes its second-best oath
that De Rougement’s yarn of a few years ago had
more gospel in it than most people supposed.
While the expedition ran riot on the beach, it
took to riding the turtles which lay tamely on the
sands. Directly the mount takes his seat he
must, it seems, be ready for a time of hilarity.
The turtle at once makes for the surf, and if his
head is not kept up he will go to the bottom.
Here is a chance for some enterprising individual
to get a few turtle for Manly and St. Kilda. A
man with some time on his hands could easily
bring down a team and save freight, riding one
and driving the rest tandem. But the object of
this par is to tell of a waybacker who rode a
Murray cod the other day at Narandera. No one
saw him do it ; but as he is a fisherman every-
body will take liis word for it. He had hooked a
GO-pounder which fought with great vigor for its
life. After tiring it out a bit, as he thought, the
fisherman got down in the water to land the prize.
With his arm thrust through the gills he tried to
climb the steep bank, but the fish upset calcula-
tions and sprang backwards. The pair went into
the deepwater with a swish. The fisherman’s
only chance was to stay in the saddle and keep
the fish’s head turned shorevvards. He did it, of
course, though he swears he will never ride an-
other without a snaffle.
“ The Pariah ” : Mighty dreary work is
that of the marram grass planters near the mouth
of the Aire, Cape Otway. The work has to be
done in the winter, and in that district each
winter week consists of eighr, wet days. The job
necessitates constant stooping, and the never-
resting wind swirls the sand into the eves, nose,
lips ana ears. Add to that occasional bursts of
sunshine on the.white sand, dazzling the already
tormented eyes. It has been decreed that the
marram shall be planted from the shore inland,
so that the drift may be arrested at its source.
Coming down to the Cape from Laver’s Hill
one could believe that the. landscape near
the Aire mouth was an immense vineyard.
The grass is planted in long parallel lines,
but it is a moot question whether it will arrest the
sand-drift, which is well up to an average height
of 60ft. I saw one instance of how the drift
advances. A section about a chain long puffed
outwards, and in a hissing cloud made a forward
move of about two chains. Eleven days after-
wards I passed the place. The wind had driven
so much sand into the gap that it was a scarcely
discernible undulation. But nothing so despair-
ingly marks the strength of the drift as the sight
of the upper branches of mighty blackbutts beat-
ing in the wind above the surface like ragged
haired giants fighting for breath.

“L.H.” : The green turtle, though he is
so often in the soup, is not by any means such a
fool as he looks. Was camped on a Barrier island
awhile back, and on my arrival found that Mr.
and Mrs. Shellback came in to the beach to
canoodle and sun-hatcli in great numbers. One
or two were killed for food, the steak being very
excellent—something like a good fillet crossed
with a veal cutlet. Whether it was that we left
the offal about too freely in the water, and the
turtles smelt a rat, or whether they had a secret
service which reported our nefarious goings on, I
don’t know. What Ido know is that after a few
days they would not land on our beach at all,
though I counted 30 or 40 at a time inside the
lagoon at high water. Same thing killed the
turtle canning industry on North-West Island.
A Rockhampton meat-preserving firm erected a
plant there, and made turtle-soup—partly of
turtle. Things hummed for a while, but finally
the turtle refused any longer to come and be
canned, and the plant was shut down. Where-
upon the turtles came back in all their old
numerousness.

“Horatio B.” : I notice that Victorian
Governor Carmichael, whilst in the country re-
cently, showed a number of children how to feed
a mantis—the curious insect that resembles a
wowser without his umbrella. I saw a mantis
feeding himself the other morning; and if Car-
michael can do it any better, then he ought to be
slip and wicket-keeper in the next Test match.
When I first sighted him, the long, solemn,
armored thing was waiting with his front claws
devoutly folded. A venturesome flying insect
aeroplaned out of the hedge close by, alighted
three or four inches in front of the religious beast,
and started to clamber laboriously across a
blossom. The head of the mantis turned slightly ;
the guileless insect continued on its way ; then
suddenly, as I watched, it disappeared. 1 looked
at the mantis—which, I was prepared to swear,
had not moved or dropped one Amen. But there
was evidence to prove that he had. In the crook
of his right praying claw the dazed flying insect
was kicking his last; the mantis had already
bitten off his hind-legs and coat-tails. Now I
don’t see how it is possible for a mere Governor
to give a brute like that points on how to eat a
quick lunch. Seems to me that Carmichael might
do worse than sit back and allow the mantis to
show,him how to open bazaars like a lightning
flash. What a lot of time would be saved if a
mantis could be engaged to open Parliament 1

“ The Scapegoat ” : As my nomination of
the thrush as the champion of feathered friends
has passed unchallenged, I make hold to nomi-
nate the wagtail as champion for bravery, In
my hayshed roof, right up under the rafters, a
pair of wagtails built their home, and during the
recent hay-making William’s'Uinnoyance at being
disturbed was frank and to the point. As each
load of hay brought the stack nearer his sitting
wife, his rage increased; He swooped at each
forkful as it was tossed up, and eventually
attacked horses and men indiscriminately. A
space was carefully left for William and Wil-
hehnina’s entrance and exit, and they now have a
thriving family'; butby the way Billiam swaggers
round, it is clear that he reckons he saved all
their lives by hunting away a drove of giants.

“ Itan The saltbush, so long neglected
by squatterdom, is threatening to turn dog on
Australia. Although the season has been unpre-
cedentedly good out in the tract behind Broken
Hill, the saltbush is withering off as if struck by
a drought. There may be something in the yarrt
that the seasons have been so prolific that the
vegetable wants a spell, and is dying off to get it.
In other words, it walnts a drought— which it will
probably get if there is anything in the law of
averages. After the weather got tired of being
dry, seven or eight years ago, the salient resident
resuscitated itself just as if ithad riotously enjoyed
its state of suspended animation. The bluebush,
however, is Australia’s best drought-resisting
shrub. Mulga has been known to give up the
ghost during a thirsty spell, while bluebush hung
on to the raft. Even the gnawing of bunny at the
bark is defied. Myall and gidya are hardier than
mulga, but'they'haven’t the same feeding value.
“Agricola.”:. The youngest son of the
pub came up from the billabong the other even-
ing with a fine string of blackfish. YVe consumed
them at breakfast and rejoiced, and were exceed-
ing glad. For several evenings the hopeful pro-
duced a decent string, and bribes were showered
upon hini to induce him to discover his fishing-
hole. We went with him several times after
tea, and the total catch amounted to nil. Sus-
picion being aroused, he was tracked down one
afternoon to an entirely different spot, where, sus-
pended from a ti-tree branch a few feet above the
water, was a rabbit so totally defunct that it
advertised the fact for a considerable distance,
Herds of blowflies were camped on the advertise-
ment, and their immature offspring, dropped off
at frequent intervals, to the great joy and delecta-
tion of the fish. The boy baited with nothing.
The flop of the hook was enough to cause a rush,
and as the biggest and strongest of the fish were
in the orchestral stalls, he always secured, a good
sample. Blackfish were off next breakfast.

“fG-omerander ” : To “Mimosa” (B.
8/12/TO). I fiave a rosella that certainly knows—
I don’t say that he counts— up to four. It is my
practice to divide a small biscuit into four parts,
which I give him in succession. But sometimes,
for experiment, I withhold the last instalment,
and the result leaves no room for doubt that
Josephus considers himself a deeply-injured fowl.
He prances madly to and fro along his perch,
alternating frantic denunciations ,of breach-of-
contract with gentler lamentations over abuse of
confidence. Then he settles down and whistles
plaintively and appropriately “There’s Nae Good,
Luck.” But presently, when he is handed the
missing quarter, he returns thanks with “Larry
O’Qaff,” performed allegro con fuoco, which, I
understand, is Graeco-Roman for a devil of a row.

“ YVhaffor ” : Re turtles on the north coast
of Australia. There are two ’ species—the
green turtle and the liawksbill. The former is
celebrated as the author of the far-famed
soup. The green turtle is specially distinguished
by its short beak, which is . devoid of a hook at
the tip, and by the shields being in contact at
their edges all through life. In color, the shell of
the adult is yellow or brown, with yellowish spots
or marblings ; while in the young it is uniform dark
brown or olive on top, and yellow underneath. The
green turtle’s food consistsof seaweeds. The hawks-
bill is not edible. It has beautifully-mottled,
horny shields, which are the sole source of the
tortoiseshell of commerce—such df it as isn’t imi-
tation. Some of these amphibians are of enor-
mous size. I saw one that weighed 2501 b
immense ungainly creature of great age. Shells
have brought as high as £4 each in Europe. Yet
there is no turtle industrjo One sea-turtle lays
from 140 to 150 eggs, which are hatched by natural
incubation in the sand. When fresh they are
better eating than hen eggs. The whole coast is
teeming with turtle, and Buchanan and Quail
Islands are where they hold their mass meetings.

“ Vigilans’s ” northern friend :
The most extraordinary reptile in the Commonwealth
is undoubtedly the frilled lizard of North Australia—the
solitary representative of its genus. It,attains a length of
35in., about 13 of which is tail. The species is at once
recognised by the curious frill surrounding the throat and
extending upwards to the sides of the nape. This tnem-
b ane has a serrated edge, is covered with scales of
larger size than those•'on the back; and recalls the Eliza-
bethan frills of our forgotten grandmothers. - In form the
body of the frilled lizard is slightly compressed, the
general color of the upper parts being pale brown, which
may be either uniform or mottled; The reptile favors sandy
districts, where it walks with a swinging gait on its hind
legs. When frightened it sits down on its hindquarters,
raises its forequarters and head as high as. possible,
strikes its body with its, tail and show's its teeth at the
intruder. The frill when extended forms a shield, con-
cealing the body, limbs and tail. It is one of > the gam eat
of the lizards,, and a great fighter. The blacks call it

IN THE STONE AGE.
“ Ha! Ha! Out for a day’s sport, were they?"
“ Curnbangaree ” : I would trouble you
to present my compliments to “Leo Lear” (B.
1/12/10), along with my observation that lie
doesn’t know much about termites’ nests and the
appropriation of same as lodgings for married
couples of birds. He would have us believe that
the white ants, the old man and Mrs. Jackass and
the kids occupy the one flat, with use of kitchen
common to both families. Rats! Every member
of the Muscicapidse (fly-catcher) and Halcyonidse
(kingfisher) families regards white ants as the
juiciest morsel of the menu. No sweet, milky,
little bit like them can be caught on the wing ;
and for this reason the elevated home of the ants
receives constant and vigorous attention, until the
whole colony is wiped out. ; Then, and only then,
does the bird rent the flat. The birds which 1
have most frequently found taking up residence in
the depopulated village'are the grey and the
speckled woodpecker; though, besides birds,
green snakes, ’possums, bats, squirrels and frogs
have made many a “shake down” in the white
ants’ gunyah.
KING BILLY’S LAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Already the time seems nigh when the last full-blooded,
Victorian-born black-Australian native may be expected.
—News item.
The black man stood upon a hill
Where smoked a city far and wide,
And flowed ’twixt house and raging mill
A never-ending human tide.
He saw the trains come streaming in,
The clash of iron beams he heard,
The strokes of piston and of gin,
The murmur of the alien word.
Here once the grateful forest spread,
The tribes took rest beneath the hill,
And Nature offered simple bread,
Sweet water and the beast to kill.
“ The tribes are gone. Their dust the rude
North wind is driving to the sea 1
llow true it is the gods send food ;
The Dev.l, Oooks—how true ! -’ said he.

THE FINANCE OF PHIDSTON.
Premier Kids ton told the Queensland Assembly that he fully recognised how perilous a time was impending for the State when the payment of
£12,000,000 of old loans became due in 1915. Therefore, he said the wisest thing to do was to get a fresh ,£10,000,000 loan on the marketas early
as possible.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
John G. Cramond has got his name into
the Melbourne death-notices at the age of 79.
He reached Victoria in the ’so’s, but stuck to tape
and sundries instead of pursuing the uncertain
nugget; and he continued at the prosaic business
all his days—first in Lonsdale-street, then in
Warrnambool, and in later years as a director of
the heavily-gilded Melbourne firm of Ball and
Welch.

W. H. Donald, one time of Sydney D.T.
staff, and now special correspondent in China for
the New York Herald, is alleged to be making
much money these times apart from what his inky
remarks bring in. He is managing director of
the new Tonkin (China) pulp and paper mills,
and recently rushed over to America to purchase
large quantities of machinery for the business

The man who happens to he mayor of
Glenelg sits in a chair made out of timber taken
from the old Buffalo. The Buffalo was the ship
that brought the Province’s first Governor,
and Glenelg is the village that has been built on
the shores of Holdfast Bay, where that first
Governor landed and read the Proclamation.
Archdale Parkhill, secretary of the Liberal
and Deform Association of N.S.W., was given an
umbrella last week by the members of the cellar
band. As it is altogether too late for the
Deformers to come in out of the deluge, a brolly
just about fits the case. They might have added
a pair of goloshes, though.

Robert Nall, news editor of the Sydney
Daily Telegraph, returns from a trip round the
world with better health and more experi-
ence, which is a first-class return for the
money invested. Like the average press-
man, who must write what he sees,
Nall turned his holiday into copy.
He tells one specially interesting
yarn about the liquor problem in
the United States. Very soon, he
thinks, most of the States will be
“dry.” Nevertheless, statistics
show that the Americans hold
their own as a drinking com-
munity, despite prohibition;
and he quotes Rear-Admiral
Evans, who “denounced pro-
hibition in the State of Maine,
because . . . his sailors when
they went into Portland were
poisoned by bad whisky, which
they had no difficulty in get-
ting, for oceans of intoxi-
cating liquors are obtainable
in Maine coast ports.” Alto-
gether, Nall decided that there
are worse places than N. S.
Wales, and he advises Aus-
tralian journalists to go and
see them. “Make a tour of
as much of the world as you
can cover,” he has been telling
his young men. “Go some-
time—the first chance for
choice.” So far, no announce-
ment has been made from the
manager’s department that a
year’s holiday on full pay may
be had on application.
J. W. Hackett, of the
Perth West Australian, had a
christening party the other day, and the event
was described in his own paper in the tones of a
man who is trying to sell the Bull-frog West Ex-
tended Consolidated East :
The guests passed into the ballroom, which was arranged
like a large music-room, and in an alcove at the end stood
a perfect bower of waterlilies and roses, intermingled with
fern, arranged by |here followed the florist’s advt.], the
fairest flower of all being the little one who lay peacefully
sleeping, unconscious of all the admiration he was receiv-
ing. For certain it is that the baby is always the central
figure on such occasions, and little John Winthrop
Hackett was no exception to the rule.
Poor little John Winthrop Hackett, who takes so
early in life a lesson in the arts of advertising and
of canvassing for ads !

Dankel, Labor M.P., who is a colleague of
Treasurer Vaughan and Education Minister
Coney beer in the representation of Torrens (S. A.)
was christened George Casper Adolph Maria
Prosper by his loving parents. He has, however,
by official process ridded himself of all but one of
the prefixes, and friends may now simply call him
George without hurting his feelings.
The Australian climate gets an unsolicited
testimonial from Colonel Madley, till lately Ade-
laide’s Commissioner of Police. He went back to
his native Wales only a few months ago, intend-
ing there to end his days. But, having sampled
the Cold Country weather, and compared it with
what he had left behind him, he pulled up stakes
once more, and New Year’s Day saw him back in
the streets of Adelaide—for good this time, he
says.
Antonio de Travers, which sounds like
either a bag of bananas or a penn’orth of ice-
cream, is really the legal moniker of an Austrian
Count, who subsists humbly at Tallangatta (Vic.)
by carting firewood. The Count, who is the last
male sprout of his patrician tree, is locally an ob-
ject of all the more curiosity from the fact that he
is reported to be related to the House of Haps-
burg. The relationship, however, is admittedly
distant, and can only be perceived by the eye of
faith looking through a powerful glass.
The news that Manoel, late of Portugal, is
shortly going to wander round this way and pick
up points about the antique art of kingcraft, is ■
rather mystifying. Theatrical business has cer-
tainly been booming of late ; but Paris must still
have a big lead in chorus girls and dancers, judg-
ing by the quantity of Grand Dukes and such
who are round there on business. Justice will not
be done to this matter of greenroom government
until some tired comp, makes a Court Journal
solemnly remark that “ His Majesty left yester-
day for foreign tarts.”
There are still surviving four members of
the Chittleborough family who all came to S.A.
with the first Governor (Hindinarsh) in H.M.S.
Buffalo on Dec. 28, 1836. One of them played in
inter-State cricket for S.A., and he was for many
long years secretary of the Licensed Victuallers’
Association. He is a splendid advt. for the
healthiness of good liquor judiciously swallowed.
H. T. Morris, a nephew of the Governor, and a
fellow-passenger, also survives. Hindinarsh
fought at the battle of the Nile, and was thanked
by Nelson on the quarter-deck for saving the flag-
ship from being burnt, when the French L’Orient
caught fire. Morris was a well-grown youth
when he came to Adelaide with this interesting
uncle 74 years ago.
Douglas Mawson figures that the Aus-
tralian Antarctic Expedition which he is organ-
ising, to start perhaps at the end of 1911, will
cost £40,000; and £40,000, he allows, will take
some getting. The original idea was that
Shackleton should lead the party, with Mawson
as scientific director ; but as Shackleton has other
fish to grill, he has passed the whole thing on to
the Australian, and is giving him help at the
other end. Mawson’s present idea is to go almost
due south from Australia. There may be less
chance of reaching the South Pole that way ; but
he reckons that there is more scope thereabout
for scientific exploration.
“Brigadier” Bruntnell, who formerly
Hallelooyered for Booth and now Hallelooyersfor
Wade, has gone into business as a suburban
auctioneer and land agent. The firm evidently
intends to run things on - Hallelooyer principles ;
in its announcement it hopes that it will (d.v.)
be spared to knock things down for many years
to come.
Frank A. Nanldvell is holding a winter
exhibition of pictures in New York, and, accord-
ing to Boston Transcript and the talk of
American art journals, is making good even
better than before. Two or three tine portraits
are shown, but the big end of the exhibition is land-
scape and forest scenery. The Australian artist
does more picture work than Puck illustration
these days, and feels that he has' done enough
American subjects now to shortly entitle him to
an exhibition in Australia, and time to paint some
of the typical life of his own country.
The first letter that fell into The Bulle-
tin office this year came from Pine Creek, the
little township that hangs on to the end of the
railway line which started to hurry across Aus-
tralia from Port Darwin, and then stopped short
till a bigger man arrived to build it. “The
Northern Territory is Australia's now, and those
of us who are Australians want to give the Ter-
ritory all the lift we can. So we’re thinking of
starting a little paper. It won’t be much bigger
than a postage-stamp; but it can grow.” The
man behind the scheme is Dr. Abbott, Govern-
ment medical officer in those parts. He used to
be in Denial Bay, out in the West Coast of S.
Australia. That coast, which was marked down
as a desert, has become a big wheat-producer these
last few years ; and Abbott did a lot to bring that
change about. Yet all he had to do it with was a
small type-written sheet. The novelty of the
thing got the uaper quoted widely ; so, in the
end, he had a circulation of tens of thousands for
his whoops. This time he wants to go one better,
and get the thing printed ; but, if he can’t piint
it, the chances are he will write it—and The Bul-
letin gives him three cheers to go on with.
Frank Brown, a vigorous young Australian,
having some time on his hands and some few
pounds in his pockets, overlanded from
Bourke to Port Darwin last year with a
mate. On that trip they found something
that looked like a mine at Maroomba.
Brown frankly said he didn’t know a mine for
certain when he saw it; but he took in samples,
and they assayed w'ell enough. As it turned out
the mining man whom the Melbourne syndicate
sent up said it wasn’t a mine. But Brown wasn’t
finished with the Territory. He went back, and
roamed all over the north of it for six
months, and comes south again surer than
ever that it is going to do great things
when the markets are available. Some day,
he says, it ought to grow more pigs, for instance,
than ever Chicago packed. In some parts, where
a pair or two have somehow broken away and set
up housekeeping, there are now thousands—not
weeds, but fat, marketable pork. Judge Herbert,
who used to be Government Resident up that
way, has the same hopeful ideas on the pig ques-
tion ; which no doubt explains why, when he
transferred himself to Papua, he left two young
Herberts behind him on a stock-raising squattagf,
the stock being mainly the sort with a grunt
before and a curl behind.
ITS PROPER USE.
SODDEN SOUL: “ Tha’s riyh', my boy, don’t
spare it.”
One of the old-time Admirals of J. Bull’s
navy is Kennedy, K.C.8., at present on a trip
through Maoriland. This old salt butted into the
Crimea row as a midshipinite aboard the Rodney,
90-gun line-o’-battle ship, and saw the first and
last shot fired at Sebastopol. Subsequently he
was on service on the China station during the
argument which broke out in 1856, and smelt
much powder and saw much Pong. In thr*e days
the sailor-man was the most casual of Britain’s
assets. Reporting an incident on the Canton
River, where he shot through a door at a dis-
appearing Chinaman who had attempted to kill
him, Kennedy said
When the time came for us to retire, I went ashore,
and out of curiosity looked behind the door, when, 10, my
friend (Pong) was lying there dead. We then returned
to H.M.S, Barracouta to report proceedings and have
dinner.
A few years ago Admiral Kennedy retired from
the position of Commander-in-Chief at the Nore,
and at 65 years of age started out to enjoy life.
So he has taken a run down to Maoriland, some-
body having mentioned in his hearing that good-
sized trout are to be had there for the catching.
If the trout are not up to expectation, he proposes
to pack up for Sweden, where he knows his fish
to an ounce.
“LAWSON.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A correspondent in Melbourne Hrrald proposes that
the Federal capital should be called Lawson, in honor of
the Australian bard. The proposal is suggestive.
I took the train to Lawson,
Our Federal Capital,
Where shearers fill the shanties
And beer is brown and tall.
And on the road to Lawson
Some queer towns by the way
I saw, from Grover Alley
To porticoed McCrae.
Across the Hervey mulga
The car shot merrily,
And sighted Banjo Station,
And Outback Ogilvie.
It rattled on to Bedford,
A roaring mining camp ;
And Emerson, ’mid gum-trees
And pastures green and damp.
Romantic domes of Bayldon
Glowed in the sunset sky ;
Down new straight streets of Adams
I hear the train cars fly.
We passed Gay Brereton junction,
A classic, cultured town ;
We took in coal at Brennan—
French coal, symbolic, brown.
And coming close to Lawson
I paused for drinks within
That rolling seaport Brady,
Then sped to moonlit Quinn.
M‘Kay looked weird and starry,
The engine grunted loud ;
It shied at D. O’Reilly,
And broke down at O’Dowd.
And though we walked to Lawson,
The Federal capital,
Drovers and swaggies hailed us,
And beer was brown and tall.

COLUMN
E. Rich and Co., Ltd., merchants, station-
supply people, etc. (Sydney):—
Available De-

1909 .. 8,009 5 ~ ~ 9902 4577
1910.. 10,196 5 „ „ 9902 294 4871
This co. is in 193,037 shares of £1 each, having been
brought up to that figure through the taking over of the
Queensland Mercantile Co.’s business a few years ago.
The original Rich was a N.S.IV. back-country trader,
best known in the upper-Darling district. He sold
to an Australian co. in 1889, and some shockingly
bad years followed for the far-back country, and,
no doubt, also for this co. After nine years Dalgety’s
took a hand, and the head office was shifted to
Dalgety's offices in London. A period of careful nursing
followed; and that and the alrnost-magical recovery of
the N.S.NV. back-country apparently did such great
things for E. Rich and Co., Ltd., that a couple of years
ago the recuperated concern was led to Dalgety’s front
door and discharged, presumably cured. The head office
was then shifted back to Australia. Reserves are cer-
tainly still very small. But doubtless for a good
many years there were not only no reserves at all,
but really a deficit greater even than that disclosed
by the accounts. In 1904 there were book debts
amounting to £70,746 ; and if they had been severely
valued at that time, they might have had a good many
thousands lopped off them. The chances are that,
though the lopping was never done visibly, many a good
sovereign of profits made during the past few years has had
to go towards wiping out some of those old book debts.
Gradually the co. has relied less and less on the far-out
nrulga and saltbush, and more on a general trading
business. That has meant increasing stocks ; and as these
now represent £121,208, and as properties and plant run
to £36,696 and book debts (including bills) to £101,244,
it is obvious that the co. has to do some financing. The
total liabilities are £54,866. Compared with the share
capital that isn’t, however, very tremendous. From the
outside one can’t get much real idea as to the progress
the co. is making. One can only be sure that it ought to
have done very well indeed, and that its position
ought to be far sounder than it was a few years
ago; and the unhobblmg, so to speak, of the
co. supports the idea that it did what it ought
to have done. There is risk in the business,
of course : there is risk in every concern whose customers
are mainly engaged in the uncertain business of wheat
and wool harvesting. Those people mostly want credit;
when a wool-harvester wants to buy an earth scoop or
half a ton of fish-hooks, he can’t go and clip the surplus
wool from 100 or 1000 sheep that were shorn three or
four months before. If he ltasn’t got the cash, somebody
has to finance him. And at the end of the 12 months the
sheep may be dead. Generally, however, he is prepared to
pay pretty well for the accommodation ; so the co. that
obliges him can put something up its sleeve to meet the
cases in which the sheep have been disobliging enough
to die, and still show a good profit. Much depends on
how much a co. in this business has up its sleeve ; and
that is why one can’t always tell from the published
accounts what the real position of a co. like E. Rich and
Co. is.

May, 1910.. 16,452 20 ~ ~ 12,500 2856 50,752
Nov., 1910.. 22,148 20 ~ „ 12,500 2824 57,576
The future of this co. lies in the lap of the gods, the gods
in this case being some old gentlemen, reputed to have
more experience than teeth, who sit now and then as the
Privy Council. The trouble is over the old question of
goodwill. The co. works under an Act which gives the
Perth City Council the power to acquire the undertaking.
When the time arrived for the City Council to speak, the
two parties found themselves at loggerheads. The City
Council said that it had only to pay for the works ; the
co. said it had to pay for the business as well —that
is to say, the people would have to pay for giving
themselves their own custom. Lawyers were consulted,
and of course one lot said one thing, and the other lot
said the other thing. Then came arbitration, and after
that the matter drifted on to the old gentlemen
in London. The amount offered by the coun-
cil was £158,866, or about 255. per share, whereas
two years ago, just before the clawing started, the
market valued the concern at over 50s. per share. That
presumably was for works and goodwill; and if that was
a fair valuation for the property, including goodwill, it
looks as though the co. expected to get about as much
for the thing that it couldn’t smell, or taste, or bruise its
nose against, as for those things that are visible and
tangible. So far as the trouble has gone, the City
Council has won ; the arbitrators said that the
valuation should be on the basis of the plant
in situ. The share quotation eased on that, but
there are buyers even now at about 39s.—which means
either strong faith that the Privy Council will jump the
other way, or that the works, etc., are much under-
valued in the co.’s books. The whole of the assets, less
liabilities, are equal to £1 11s. 3d. per share according to
the published accounts.
The co. supplies electric current as well as gas ;
in fact, the income from the former is more than double
that from the latter, and the net gas profit for the half-
year was £5769, while the other department showed
£13,555. Dividing the general assets among the two
departments in proportion to the capital specially
debited to each of them, the electric business shows a
net profit of about 22 per cent., while the gas gives about
16 per cent. Still, it isn’t likely that these things will be
of personal interest to the shareholders much longer.
Those who got in early have had an amazingly good time;
as the table shows, there have been terms when the
holders of contributing shares drew at the rate of 50 per
cent; per annum, though they certainly had to pay a
part of it back to the co. to wipe out their share
liability. But it is pretty safe to say that, on the lowest
possible valuation, they will fret every penny of it back.
The only people who possibly may fall into the soup are
those Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide men who didn’t
take the trouble to find out what the co.’s Act was
loaded with, and who bought from the guileless 'Groper
at 50s. and thereabouts.

This energetic co. has the mayor of Sydney for one
managing director and an ex-town clerk of Sydney for
another, and, to quote the articles, both “ shall remain
in office until they resign or die or are removed by special
resolution of the co.” As to shareholders’ voting power :
“ One vote for every 50 shares ; no member shall have
more than 100 votes.” As there are 100,000 shares
in the co. it should not be impossible to remove
the managing directors if such a thing should
ever become necessary or plainly advisable. Not
that there is the least suggestion of such a thing
now ; but where there are managing directors appointed
on these terms, the investor ought to satisfy himself that
the power of removal is real. No doubt there are ways
by which the limitation of votes to 10(3 can be got over.
But if a managing director holds so many shares that he
has to call in dummies to help him exercise his full vot-
ing strength, it is pretty certain that his interest
in the co. is large enough to make him at least
try to advance the co.’s interest. No doubt it
is possible for managing directors —or even directors who
are not managing directors—to fatten themselves while
starving the shareholders. Assuming that they have a
controlling vote, they can do it, for instance, by sucking
up the profits in salaries. In this case the salary is fixed
by the articles of the co.—a very proper thing in a case
of this kind. As far as can be seer., then, the interests
of the outside shareholder are satisfactorily protected.
The list at the top of this brief anniversary
address says that the profit for the half-year was £6605.
The published balance-sheet says it was £5635. The
difference is made up by additions to the insurance fund,
which is invested apart from the business —another
excellent feature of the co.’s financial methods. That
fund now amounts to £11,269. Including all assets
except book debts and similar non-combustible goods,
the total book value of the property is £51,298. The
insurance fund is therefore already very substantial,
especially when the comparative youth of the co. is
taken into account. Certainly, part of the rapid
growth is due to share premiums ; the business hasn’t
done it all. But it has apparently done very well in the
matter of providing for possible future knocks, though
exactly how well it has done can’t be told in the absence
of any hint as to the amount written off against depre-
ciation and wear and tear. The directors’ report says
that the profit is shown after “ full allowance for every-
thing- in the nature of bad and doubtful debts and the
usual appropriation for depreciation.” But what “ the
usual” is isn’t even whispered. This comparison only
gives a hint of it in one line :

It will be noticed that wharf properties four years ago
were valued at £15,900, and now they stand at only
£7OOO. Some certainly may have been sold, though there
is no mention of it; so it is more likely that the book-
value has been written down out of revenue. If
that practice has been followed to the same ex-
tent all through, then the co. must have been having
an amazingly good time, and its position must now be
extraordinarily solid. The one item at which the eye-
brows go up an inch or two is “Book debts, etc.,
£53,977”—0f which £36,532 is ordinary trade debts, as
against £17,841 twelve months ago. The turnover may,
no doubt, have increased vastly ; but that item needs
watching, for Sydney’s building boom may conceivably
come to a very sudden and very unpleasant end.

This isn’t plain sailing ; and the figures are not as abso-
lutely correct as they ought to be. The trouble is the
old one of lumped items. Thus “Depreciation of plant
and buildings” is lumped with “income-tax,” which is
unpardonable ; and “Amount written off book debts and
transferred to contingency reserve” is another mixed
item which shouldn’t be poured into one pot in that
fashion. In the table, the total which has disappeared
by way of these two items is shown as written off, though
of course bad debts and income tax ought to be deducted
from the profits and not shown first as profits at all.
Companies law is bad enough in most States in Aus-
tralia ; but in Maoriland it is far worse, with the result
that any old thing can be put forward there and called a
balance-sheet. The best that can be said for the present
accounts is that they are no worse than dozens of others
that come from across the Tasman Sea.
Eighteen months ago this co.’s position didn’t
strike The Bulletin very favorably, and it hasn’t im-
proved much in the meantime. The capital is £23,680,
including calls paid in advance, and the reserves are
£4116. The liabilities are £66,778. It is quite true that
some of the most solvent banks in the world have bor-
rowed six times the total amount of their own funds, and
this co.’s liabilities are not three times greater than the
funds. But the bank that didn’t keep nearly one-third of
all its deposit and other liabilities in cash or liquid assets, so
as to meet any sudden demand for repayment, would
come in for some strenuous language. This co., on the
other hand, has practically no cash or immediately-liquid
assets—not 1 per cent, of its liabilities. If it wants
money quickly to pay depositors, etc., it must either
depend upon calling it in from clients or rely upon the
bank for more overdraft. Of the total assets of £95,296,
advances represent £74,410. They may be perfectly
good. But perfectly good assets won’t always fetch 20s.
in the £ if they have to be realised whether the
market is favorable or unfavorable. The position is,
therefore, not as strong as it should be. There ought to
be more capital in the business; and even then there
possibly shouldn’t be as much trading, if it meant keep-
ing the safe as bare of cash and liquid assets. Getting
back for a few seconds to the table, it should perhaps be
explained that the accounts are for 18 months. A div.,
apparently at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum, was
paid for the second six months and 6 per cent, per annum
is to be paid for the .past six months.

The accounts don’t anywhere show the transaction re-
lating to the transfer of reserves to capital ; but as
£15,000 has fallen out of reserves and £15,000 has fallen
into capital, there is no doubt as to what has hap-
pened. The co. is partly a business and partly a
gambling concern. In 1909 it bought, and then
the price of wheat jumped, and flour, of course,
jumped too, and so a profit of nearly 80 per cent, on
the then capital of £15,000 was disclosed. This past
year the co. bought again ; but this time the wheat
market slumped, and then there was trouble. A country
mill, out towards the dead end of things, isn’t quite like
a mill at a seaport, or one a good way along the track
towards a seaport. The way-out country mill has to buy
early. If it doesn’t, the wheat from that district is
shipped away, and then the mill has to buy in other
parts, and drug the wheat out, and then drag the
flour back ; and all that dragging is bad business.
So the far-out country mill has to get practically all its
requirements early in the season, while the city or sea-
port mill, or the mill in towards the city or seaport, can
wait if the prices are hig-h at the start, and catch the
stuff as it comes through later on. The wheat-buying
business is a good deal of a gamble always ; for a co. like
this it is more of a gamble than usual. But though it
has had a comparatively bad year, the Young Co. had
got into such a wonderfully good position that it
reckons it can afford a 5 per cent, dividend,
equal to 10 per cent, on the actually-paid
capital ; and as it was fairly moderate in the extra good
years this paper will stretch a point and return a verdict
of “ Not guilty and don't do it again.” It softens its
heart in that way in consideration, among other things,
of the fact that the co.’s total liabilities are only £439,
while its liquid assets, mostly cash, amount to over
£24,000.

It is only a very moderately-profitable concern, consider-
ing the sort of concern it is. Certainly people who
invest in Consols don’t get 8 per cent. ; but there are not
quite the same risks about Consols. Also, this co.’s 8 per
cent, isn’t really 8 per cent., after all; for, after striking
a piece of bad luck or bad management a few years ago,
the co. had to write off more than half its capi-
tal. The piece of bad luck or bad manage-
ment in that case was embezzlement; and it
wasn’t the only time the co. has suffered that way.
It may be that the co has too many branches. There are
eight of them, besides the head office ; and £24,652—the
total of capital, reserves, deposits and overdraft—isn’t
very much to finance nine loan establishments. This
probably means poor salaries ; and poor salaries are apt
to be dangerous things in a business of this sort, where
the supervision can’t be very effective.
ft * •* *
“ L.IL”: Grind to see that The Bulletin is not
allowing Wade’s Railway Superannuation business to
slumber undisturbed. When the scheme was first
announced, none of the men who really know anything
about pension schemes—and they are practically con-
fined to the actuarial departments of the life offices —
dreamed for a moment that it would be suffered to go
through. The thing was such a ludicrous financial and
actuarial impossibility that it was not taken seriously.
When it was finally made clear that the farce was to be
played right through, the actuaries of the insurance
offices found themselves in a hole. They had no
time to obtain expert opinion from outside. The
job was rushed through too quickly, for that. And they
realised that their own opinions, given in all good
faith and based on sound actuarial calculations, would
not have the same weight, inasmuch as all the offices
catered for the Railway Employees’ Assurance scheme,
and all stood to lose if this were replaced by a pension
scheme. Being staunch Liberals to a man, they knew
what Charles Gregory and his party were capable of in
the matter of fair criticism, so they shut up. But it
would have been better if they had braved the irate
accusations of self-interest which Wade would have
hurled. A little mud more or less would not have mat-
tered, especially if it had saved the Community from such
a patent political job. Before the thing goes another
step further the Government should appoint a Commis-
sion of actuarial experts to report on the wild scheme.
* * * *
Hill Fisher and Son’s list shows that there are
very few Maoriland stocks that didn’t add something to
their market value last year. About the biggest rise
was in New Zealand Shipping Co.’s shares, which went
from £7 10s. to £ll 2s. 6d. This is an Anglo-Maoriland
concern, with two head-offices, two lots-of directors, and
the annual report and accounts are presented first in
London and then in Christchurch. The co. has a
big say in the Australian-Canadian line. Union
Steamship Co., whose £lO shares were worth £ll
15s. in 1900, has made further progress; the £1 shares
are now £1 16s. 6d. (= £lB ss. per £lO share),
and there has also been a 33J per cent, watering. The
share that was worth £ll 15s. in 1900 is thus worth
£24 6s. Bd. to-day. Fire insurance shares have moved
along slowly, but steadily. Gas have shown little change
for some years ; what movement there has been has been
upwards. The same applies to building and investment
shares. Meat preserving cos.’shares fluctuate ; last year
they moved up quickly. The Christchurch Co.’s stock
fattened fastest—£ll 2s. 6d. to £l5. Industrials held
their own, with a bit to the good in most cases.
* * * *
The views of a mining engineer whom The
Bulletin is sometimes glad to consult: “The Bullfinch
Prop, is in an angle formed by the junction of the
granite and the hard greenstone. Its own country rock
is a schist. Near the granite, and running parallel, is a
hard bar ; and the Bullfinch’s rich ore is up against this.
There are five shafts. One, south of the others, found
gold. It was, therefore, supposed that it was on a par-
allel lode. Prospecting work from the shaft did not sup-
port that view. It now looks most likely that the
Bullfinch deposit is a boomerang-shaped lens, or a rounded-
off angle, conforming to the obtuse angle formed by the
junction of the granite and the greenstone. There may
be other lenses. At the back of the camp there is ore, as
mentioned last week ; but the outcrop is not of the same
character as that capping the main lens. The value of
the capping of this second body is variously stated at
from 6dwt. to loz. As a matter of fact, it has probably
never been thoroughly sampled.
“No other claim on the field has the same con-
ditions as the Bullfinch, because no other can be in the
same angle. There may, for all that, be other lenses;
and it is possible that they do not show outcrops.
Attention has so far been mainly directed to the out-
crops that are actually showing. These are mostly very
hard and not promising. If there ever were others, the
best of them have probably eroded. That, no doubt,
occurred at the Bullfinch, where the erosion left the gold
in the surrounding loam. This adds to the difficulty of
prospecting. On the other band, there is no very strong
reason to believe that such eroded outcrops are
numerous.
“A careful inspection discloses no values on the
Chaffinch. Yet there is no doubt that gold was got,
though opinion is divided as to how it came there. Look-
ing at the ground now, one sees no reason why the gold
should have been there. After the smash, a detective
was sent up from Perth. He seems to have dismissed
the salting theory, and turned his attention to the
question whether there was a ‘ bear conspiracy.’ There
is no doubt whatever that many wires were sent
from the field discrediting the rich find. Men
who had gone over the ground could see no
reason to expect values, rich or otherwise, and
when a very rich patch was found they offered the
easiest explanation—salt. The question perhaps never
will be thoroughly cleared up. All that can be said now
is that, while it is practically certain that nothing what-
ever will be got in the claims in the granite to the east of
the Bullfinch, it is less likely that mines will be found in
the hard greenstone than in the comparatively
soft schist. In this matter, theory is to some
extent supported by experience. Nothing, at any
rate, has yet been found in the greenstone on
the Bullfinch field, while the Bullfinch itself is in the
schist. The Chaffinch is in the greenstone. At the
same time, if gold is found in the greenstone, the
Chaffinch is very well situated ; for there is gold in the
Bullfinch—very rich gold—and the Chaffinch is next door.
“The Bull Ant is in the schist. A little gold
has been found in this claim, hut in a narrow vein so far,
though the surrounding schist also carries gold. The
body bears no resemblance to that of the Bullfinch Prop.,
and there is no reason to suspect that it is of the same
character. Of the rest it can be said in general that
they have so far got nothing. If the Bullfinch lens
goes out of the Doolette ranch, it has yet to be
found. The chances are that it doesn’t go out. And if
there are similai lenses they have yet to be found. It is
no use sinking shafts to find them. A very little work
will show whether the outcrops are worth anything or
not. For the rest, trenching at comparatively little ex-
pense will show whether there are eroded outcrops.
When that is done, and if nothing is found, there are two
courses open—either liquidation or the testing of some
of the-low-grade mines which live in the Southern Cross
district. Shareholders should see that the money isn’t
wasted.”

The last fortnight’s crushing from the Bullfinch
—exemption began on 17th December—was 265 tons for
33900 z., worth £14,400. That made a total of 965 tons for
10,3750 z., worth £43,563.
The Bullfinch Miner’s view of the matter :
“While it vindicates Mr. Greenway, it is obvious that
Mr. Grut’s report suggests that the Chaffinch was salted.
The metallurgical expert does not say so in as many
words, nor do we. But it looks that way, and for the
fair fame and good name of the Bullfinch field generally,
for the sake of Yilgarn and the mining industry of the
whole State, all engaged in mining must welcome the
official announcement that Warden Finnerty is to hold
an inquiry into the Chaffinch boom.”
* * *
Detectives are also making inquiries about the
Young Bullfinch. A man on the Young Bull comes to
the manager of the Bullfinch, who had befriended him, and
says he : “ You did me a good turn once, and I’ll do you
one now. We’ve struck it rich in the Young Bull—here’s
the drillings.” Now, 999 men out of 1000 would take that
talk as being made in genuine gratitude for past favorc,
and the sampling of the drillings went 4oz. The manager
bought and told his friends. The manager of the Young
Bull was ill in the hospital, and when given the news he
sent out a man to sample, and the sampling blew the rich
strike into the Indian Ocean. The messenger was ap-
parently only a blunt tool, and the detective office hopes
to have the principals in a nice new dock soon.
* * * *
Some of the syndicates floated for prospecting
Yilgarn may be glad to know that many of the “ pros-
pectors’’are panning good prospects of beer in bars at
Southern Cross and elsewhere. Two representatives of
capital who were drifting very far to leeward had a pub-
lic talk on the position the other day, and the least lee-
wardly of the two said to the other: “ Why didn’t you
get off the shypoo for a day and peg out a few leases in
your spare time?” And the man who had pegged out
more liquor than the questioner replied : “ I ain’t had no
spare time. I have been drunk for tvvo weeks.” Some
of the money thus disappearing is good wowser money
from Adelaide, and loud shrieks may be expected as soon
as the Tabernacle syndicates know that their good cash
has been blown away in froth.

“Where’s the (say) Golden Mountain Bullfrog?”
a mining man asked another mining man in the Bullfinch
country the other da3 r . “ Dunno,” said the mining man.
“ But i thought you represented Bilking on the field, and
that’s one of Bilking’s floats isn’t it?” “Yes,” said
Bilking’s manager ; “ but I can’t find the darned thing.
I’ve been out there twice, and I can’t strike the pegs—if
there are any pegs. And I’ve had instructions to do a
bit of work on it too.” All the same, at least a couple of
“manager’s reports” have been published in eastern
papers about the “ Operations in progress at the mine.”
Of course, Bilking’s manager may be a liar. On the other
hand, Bilking may be a thief. There have been more
than one of both sorts engaged in this Bullfinch business.
■* * * *
Fraser’s, Southern Cross (W. A.), which has pro-
duced a million pounds’ worth of gold, hasn’t had a
chance in recent times. Not a foot has been sunk for 12
years, and mostly tributors have done the work that has
been done. There is talk of a new float, and if it comes
off Fraser’s will have another chance. It is almost bound
to answer to developments once it has been relieved of
the inertia which is inexplainable except by the curse of
Deeming.
* * # *
Trouble is threatening again in connection with
the firewood supply for the Kalgoorlie mines. The men
want more pay. They say they now earn 9s. per day—
frequently of more than eight hours —and they want to
be able to earn 10s. in eight hours. Laborers not on contract
also want a rise. Living is dear in the wood camps, the
work is hard, and the conditions scarcely civilised ; so, if
it goes to arbitration, the men will probably score. If
the wood cos. passed the extra cost on to the mining cos.,
two or three of the latter would probably seriously con-
sider shutting down. They would be very glad of some-
thing to divert the attention of shareholders ; and in that
case, of course, the shareholders would be strenuously
encouraged to curse the wood-cutters and workers in
general instead of the directors and managers, whom they
will be likely to curse if the diversion isn’t provided.
* * * *
The two years’ agreement between the men and
most of the cos. at Broken Hill ran out on December 31 ;
but another has been arranged. There was never much
danger of a strike or lockout this time. The cos. were
ready to give a solid increase; the only thing they in-
sisted on was that the matter should be fixed for a fairly
long term. Four years was suggested. The Broken Hill
business people didn’t want the agreement to run
out at the end of a year—the uncertainty
spoils their Christmas trade. The cos. were ready
to oblige them to that extent; so a 4J years agree-
ment was asked for The smaller unions accepted
the rise—about 10 per cent. —offered ; and, on a vote, the
miners have followed suit. This doesn’t, however, apply
to the Broken Hill Prop. That co. professed two years
ago that it couldn’t pay the rates fixed by Justice
Higgins, of the Commonwealth Arbitration Court, and
refused to resume work in the mine ; and none has been
done there since. Work at the mill (where the co. treats
accumulated tailings for zinc) and at Port Pirie (where
custom smelting, etc., is done) has proceeded under
Justice Higgins’s award ; and this will continue until the

The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends;
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair than
Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is of the-
well-known Blake type, but has some additional improve-
ments which have justified in practice all the claims-
advanced by the patentee. The work of crushing is done-
on the down-stroke of the pitman instead of the up-
stroke ; there is no strain on the pitman cap ; two large-
side rods resist the thrust of the toggles, and enable the-
jaws to be regulated within a large range; the back
toggle is so made as to resist all legitimate strains of'
crushing, but will shear under exceptional conditions-
and save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings-
are self-lubricating ; the jaws are convex and concave,,
and with the cheek plates are made of steel and are-
reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering Company, of
South Melbourne, have installed a great number of these-
machines, and hold the sole manufacturing rights for the
Commonwealth.* [3

Nobel’s Dynamite Factory, Hamburg, at the
recent Brussels World Exhibition received the highest
awards, viz., Three Grand Prix for explosives of all grades
for both mining and military purposes. This against all
comers. Mine managers in New South Wales, who are
experiencing trouble, write the agents, Wm. Adams & Co.
Ltd., 175 Clarence Street, Sydney. Large stocks on hand.*

The Bolinder Oil Engine, which is known as the
simplest and most easily managed of all oil engines, is now
represented throughout Australia by Babeock and Wilcox
Ltd., Haymarket, Sydney. The advantages of the
Bolinder are small consumption of oil, ease of starting, no
complicated arrangement of valves, and automatic lubrica-
tion, and these are testified to by Australian users. The
engine is made by a firm who have manufactured oil
engines for 25 years, and comes from a factory with an
output of 1500 engines per annum.* [1
One of the oldest and best-known engineering
concerns in the Commonwealth has been converted into
a proprietary company with a capital of £150,000, and will
now trade as Mephan Ferguson Proprietary Limited, the
governing directors being Mephan Ferguson and Robert
Shand. The head office and works are at Gordon Street,
Footscray, Victoria.*

two parties have had a chance to talk the over.
The conference will probably happen early this month.
After being threatened for 12 or 15 years, the
Broken Hill Water Supply Co.’s monopoly has got its
knock-out at last. The McGowen Government passed
the Uniberumberka Bill; and the works, which were
actually started in O’Sullivan’s day, will be pushed on.
A couple of \ r ears at the outside oug-ht to see them
finished. How long- the dam will be before it has the job
of holding* back a flood nobody knows ; for Barrier creeks
don’t run as often as suburban trains. The co. has about
eight years more to go ; then it will have to hand over its
works to the Government, too. The Umberumberka
scheme will run to about £200,000 ; and though when it
is finished Broken Hill and the mines ought to be pretty
safe from water famine, interest, sinking fund and pump-
ing costs will be so heavy that there won t be any
cheap water—unless thev can work the Kalgoorlie swin-
dle over again, and make the general taxpayer hump some
of the burden.
* * * *
Broken Hill Junction has for the present given
it best, and the directors have adj*ourned operations sine
die .
* * * *
“Jack” Scantlebury has been talking and
writing forover 20 years about the re-opening of Hawkins
Hill (N.S.W.), the famous mountain of gold and dirt
which yielded the most amazingly rich crushings ever
made in Australia. One 400-ton lot from Beyer and Hol-
termann’s gave 15,0000 z. —which makes even the Bull-
finch look mean. The hill was a nest of small 33ft.
claims; and, though small areas were all right while it
was a matter simply of chiselling out gold, it killed ex-
ploration at depth. Scantlebury has, for a quarter of a
century or so, had the idea of amalgamating the leases,
and getting to work that way ; but the job was a big one,
which called for much time and patience. However, it
has been done at last; and the next thing will be, no
doubt, an application to the public to come in and take
a risk in the business. The deepest workings are at
700 ft. ; but there is a good deal of quite shallow ground
which hasn’t yet been probed.
Very few of the old workings can now be
entered, and the accounts given to explain why work was
stopped run in ail sorts of ways—excessive costs, more
heavily mineralised stone, and so on. It isn’t always
good to believe the Ancient Inhabitant’s yarn about old
mining fields, and the tale that “she’s as good as ever she
was” is mostly a base reflection on the miner of the last
generation or the generation before last. As a rule, it
wasn’t his way to leave gold in a mine when he could
make a bit more than tucker by taking it out.
Still, two or three special things have to be noted
in this case: (1) The smallness of the claims.
If you only have half an acre of ground, it would need to
be amazingly rich stuff to pay to belt a shaft down to get
it. (2) If you’ve been in the habit of picking up sove-
reigns, a change to thrippenny-bits seems too dreadful to
make life worth living. On the other hand, if you’ve
only been in the habit of picking up pennies, a change to
thrippennies seems good enough to call for three cheers
and a spell for drinks. (3) We know a good deal more
about getting gold from mineralised stone than grand-
father did. (4) Rock-drills and better hauling appliances,
etc., have greatly cheapened work, especially in deep
mines. So Hawkins Hill will have another chance, and
deserves it. The richest stuff was in leaders, forming a
stockvyork, and it’s worth spending a good many thou-
sands to find out where those leaders came from.
* * # *
The Cobar district promises some pretty good
developments in a small way after the holidays. The
New Eldorado gold mine will send a parcel of a few tons
away, and probably Mount Drysdale, which is driving at
the 560 ft. level, will have something cheerful to say
about prospects. The Canbelego copper mine will make
shipments to Cobar smelting works. Reserves in this
mine were some time since reckoned as worth between
£30,000 and £40,000; but that, of course, represents
gross value. At Mount Boppy a good development is
reported at the north end of the 600 ft. level. The Cobar-
Gladstone is showing a few inches of steel-grey ore of
high grade from the top to the bottom of the drive at
the south end of the 260 ft. level; five tons of ore sent
away assayed 32-39 per cent, copper, and 13 tons of
seconds assayed 7 - 15 per cent, copper. The C.S. A. mine
also reports a good development, and sooner or later this
mine is likely to pass to the Great Cobar.
* * * *
John Fox, one of the best-known mining men
in N.S.W., died a day or two back. He crossed the
Murray from Bendigo, and fo, years managed the Cobar
Gold Mines. Then he got a big interest in the Queen
Bee, and struggled with that show for a long time, first
as chairman of directors and afterwards as manager.
* * * *
Bookham, near Yass (N.S.W.), has had a rich
patch in the 75ft. south drive, and a 4-ton parcel has
assayed Boz. lldwt. per ton. Four-ton parcels, even of
Boz. ore, don’t make a mine, though ; and there are only
vague reports as to the size and value of the bulk of the
stuff. Thus it is said that there is 4ft. of “ milling ore"
in the face of the south drive, which probably means that
it is worth a few dwt. per ton. Driving was to have been
started at the 100 ft. level, but the enrichment near the
75ft. level proved too tempting. The Bookham is ob-
viously a patchy show and is likely to prove a gamble.
* * * *
Tolwong (N.S.W.) publishes the results of x.
sample assay—ll'6 per cent, copper, 7’4 per cent, tin,
18 per cent, iron, and 2 per cent. zinc. Add the sulphur
(19 per cent.) and the arsenic (16 per cent.), and there is
a total of 74 per cent, of mineral. This was a special
test sample from two tunnels in No. 6 section. Some
time ago a mine report stated that this part of the lode
was 12ft. wide. But for the present the size of the re-
serves is less important than the treatment of this weird
mixture of metals as a commercial proposition. The
scannite show recently worked in Tasmania has practi-
cally closed down, as shipments of copper matte —which
seems to have been the chief product—did not give a pay-
able return ; and that doesn’t look hopeful, though the
Tolwong people themselves are professedly as confident
as ever.
* * * *
Vegetable Creek, the most hopeful deep-lead
tin show in N. S. Wales, has been handicapped by an im-
possible agreement with the freeholder, who was to be
paid off at greater speed than the mine could legitimately
stand. The Adelaide co. was in 130,000 shares, a big swag
of which were prefs., so that calls brought in compara-
tively little. Reconstruction or abandonment at length
became inevitable. To help a reconstruction the freeholder
is willing to make better terms, and the shareholders are
therefore to be invited to put up another shilling a share.
Nominally a new co. will be formed ; and in this concern
all shares will rank alike. On payment of the shilling
they will be issued as paid up to something under a
pound—a few shillings liability will be left to meet con-
tingencies. With things fixed up properly, development
on right lines will he possible. Hitherto the co. has been
mainly interested in chasing patches of rich wash. No-
doubt a new shaft will have to be considered ; the one
now in use is poorly situated for effective exploration.
* * * *
Ballarat, for all practical purposes, has ceased
to be a mining field. The Plateau knoweth no producer,
and Ballarat East clings on mostly to the skirts of the
tributor. He, poor devil, has not much chance of doing
more than earn a crust, though his lot is a little better
since the Mines Department came to his rescue and in-
sisted upon a decent agreement. What is wanted at
Ballarat is a big broom and a vigorous arm to sweep out
the present crop of directors and machinery men, to give
mines their own mills, and to put in charge modern
managers who would see if it was not possible to work the
big Ballarat East formations on a decent scale, and, with
proper appliances, make them pay the divs. that they
ought to yield. Ballarat doesn’t hustle out an annual
mining balance-sheet; when it does issue any figures it
mostly runs in some that relate to mines miles away. All
that is known is that, like Bendigo, it will show a poorer
return than it did for 1909, even with the outside-district
figures thrown in.
*■* * *
Bendigo plumes don’t flutter in the breeze with
the old flaunt. For 1910 the record of dividends is one
of the worst the field has ever shown. Not only was the
gold yield low, but calls were high and dividends were
disappointing. How disappointing can be judged from
the following table :
Gold, Calls. Dividends,
oz. £ £
1905.. 218.683 175,281 230,009
1906.. 223,733 231,262 246,254
1907.. 196,944 208,779 122,017
1908.. 193,253 135,923 126,268
1909.. 213,681 110,762 159,685
1910.. 185,394 119,057 96,289
Here is a drop of £63,000 for the year, while if 1906 be
taken as a basis for comparison, the decline is £150,000.
No wonder that there is comparative stagnation in the
old field. It is not hard to give a reason. The field was
for years a slave to the idea that the only thing was to
chase the gold down to over 4000 ft., to the absolute
neglect of. the side lines where shallow saddles could be
picked up. The experience of Bendigo itself has been
that below 3000 ft. the shoots of gold shorten, and they
become poorer. It is the same the world over, and no
reason exists why nature should cut Bendigo out for
something exceptional. The find at the Central Blue
came just in time to save the field. This co. built up the
divvy list £16,000 in a short six months, topping old
stagers like Catherine Beef and Virginia, which were the
only other cos. to run into five figures for the year. A good
sign is that tributors netted good profits. Even reckon-
ing them in, Bendigo had in 1910 its worst time for a
couple of decades at least. But its record to date—
-19,189,9180z., valued at £76,760,000 —takes a lot of beating.
* * « *
Minister Mcßride would serve the cause of the
Vic. State coal-mine better if he would slop hifalutin and
come down to bedrock. When he is challenged with
having made a loss at the mine during the first few
months, why does he not say: “Yes, that’s true,” and
ask those who are baiting him to show a mine at which
they did any better in the first six months ? Then he also
needs to get the business management seen to. The
civil servant isn’t always a careful soul in handling other
people’s money, and there has been a good deal of waste
here. If he will have proper working costs kept he will
take a big step towards success.
* * * *
White Star, Gippsland (Vic.), might as well be
rechristened White Elephant. Most of the shareholders
have got tired of dropping gold into the mine, and it is
said that less than one-third of the 60,000 shares are now
alive. A good deal of development has been done ; but
last year’s little lot of stone gave under 3dwt., and as
that isn’t enough to make a meal of, the directors fill up
the weary shareholders’ plate with hope—which isn’t
fattening, especially when it is of the worn and wilted
variety.
* * * *
For November, Great Fitzroy’s production of
metals (185'6 tons copper, 9920 z. gold, and 39240 z. silver)
was officially given as £15,696. But the revenue expected
by Great Fitzroy was only £11,806 ; so £3890, or £2l per
ton of copper, comes off the gross value to cover freight,
reduction of matte, and refining and realisation. Under
the circumstances, no wonder profits have been small
and only obtainable by picking the best stopes. The
management now sends along the comforting assurance
that it will save fully £6 per ton of copper by doing its
own converting, which it is just about ready to do.
* * * *
Gympie (Q.) made a fair effort for Christmas
with 6611 tons for 60330 z. gold, and £12,129 as divi-
dends—figures exclusive of those of the Bull-owned
Scottish Gympie, which pays £4125 quarterly. The
Scottish, by the way, has put itself into a rattling good
position, and has been paying for a great deal of develop-
ment out of profits. The harvest should be reaped pre-
sently. Gympie Homeward Bound has been resurrected
as Two-Mile Consols ; apparently a co. has been floated
in Melbourne. Apparently, also, some of the same people
are concerned in the matter as were in the Home Buie
and Hilton float, which got so much unpleasant notice a
few months back.
* * * *
Gympie surprised itself by paying four Christmas
divs. Even S. Glanmire and Monkland was in the list.
* * *■ *
Pretty well everybody at Charters Towers has a
flutter in the share market; and the bigger the promise
in the mines the more energetic the flutter on the pave-
ment. Measuring things by that rule, the Towers situ-
ation can’t be considered cheerful. Here is the total of
the shares sold in the past four years:—l9o7, 461,991 ;
1908, 1,823,644 ; 1909, 862,055 ; 1910, 466,168. And the
close of the past year was the dullest part of it on
'Change.
* * * *
Charters Towers market closed the year in a
dull mood. The failure of the Brilliant St. George to
locate the Day Dawn at 1860 ft., following the poor results
obtained in Clark’s shaft, has cast a doubt on far eastern
Day Dawn prospects. Nevertheless, things may still
turn out all right; and the Towers remembers hopefully
the hard times the field passed through before the Day-
Dawn was struck in the New Brilliant Freehold and the
East Mexican. Then, just as Mills’ United began to feel
the strain of a continuous output of rich stone, the Free-
hold came along and gave £60,000 in dividends. The
Freehold is now likely to reduce its monthly crushings,
and it remains to be seen whether the East Mexican will
take up the running and provide a few dividends. The
shaft will soon be finished, and the winding engine in-
stalled at the 760 ft. level. All that is known for certain
is that there is a rich shoot of stone skirting the New
Freehold boundary, but how many levels it will rise to
in the Mexican lease can only be determined by develop-
ment. The extension of No. 5 is the most important
work in hand.
* * # *
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, crushed 270
tons for £BB5. The previous month’s return was 255
tons for an average of £3 13s. 6d. per ton. The most im-
portant development work in hand is the sinking of a
winze below No. 3 N.E. level. This winze is between 70
and 80ft. deep, and has occasionally shown a strong reef.
The co. holds 100 acres adjacent to some important
mines. The Brilliant Extended western levels are a
source of hope; but they have not yet gone far
enough on payable stone to reach the Deeps boundary.
Generally speaking, the reef worked in the Deeps lease
has been more erratic than the main Brilliant formation.
* * * *
Blucher’s Victory, near Charters Towers, felt a
trifle sadder just before the holidays, because No. 4 had
failed topiekup the reef. A promising formation will
be driven on, and it is still possible that stone will be
met with as good as that found in No. 3. The financial
position is pretty strong, so the directors risked a little
thrippenny div.
* * * *
Golden Horn (Mount Leyshon) is 18 miles from
Charters Towers. In the old days the big quartzite and
porphyry formation, interlaced with numerous ironstone
leaders, kept a big mill at work. It is now hoped that,
with up-to-date methods, including a suction-gas plant
and cyanide vats, the big low-grade body can be made to
pay. It is figured that material from the open cut can
be handled for Bs. per ton. Looking at the fact that
Mount Bischoff can deal with its soft porphyry' and allu-
vial dirt for very little over 45., there is hope for low-
grade propositions that can command cheap power and
water. If the Golden Horn is a winner, other low-grade
propositions in the neighborhood of Charters Towers are
safe for a trial.
* * * *
Kulamadau, Woodlark Island, will have a
credit of £4OOO after paying its sixpenny (£3125). Most
of it will be wanted ; for a good outlay is in view. The
mine is to be equipped with a third Huntingdon mill,
and shaft-sinking is to be attended to. The stopes carry
wide faces of payable ore, but values are erratic, and the
market is inclined to be a bit nervous.
* * * *
Mount Molloy (N. Queensland), in which John
Moffat is heavily interested, hopes that a connecting line
with Port Douglas will help to revive its fortunes, partly
by giving- its tramway something to do. The distance is
only 22 miles, but there is the coast range, which would
make the job expensive. Mount Molloy paid several
dividends, but, as so often happens with copper shows,
development at depth was unsatisfactory and divs.
stopped with a jerk. The timber concessions also have
not turned out so profitable as was expected. Looking
at the expense of entering on a short smelting cam-
paign, the directors lately decided to sell all the ore
stacked up to the Ohillagoe Co. The heap brought from
the Anniversary mine at Herberton, purchased by Mount
Molloy, has, therefore, been sent to Ohillagoe with the
rest.
* * * *
Mungana (N. Queensland) has determined to
sink deeper, so it is improving its pumping equipment.
Both shafts are to go down, one to explore the Lady Jane
lode below 320 ft. and the other the Girofla down to
710 ft. Shipments of ore are being made to Ohillagoe,
which, from their high silver and copper contents, appear
to be of payable grade. All through its career, however,
Mungana has never been able to do much more than feed
the smelting co. And it hasn’t even had the satisfaction
of seeing the smelting co. get fat.
* * * *
Mfc. Lyell has consigned 1614 tons blister copper
since the last half-year closed on Sept. 30. For 12 weeks
the output was 1790 tons copper, 152,8680 z. silver, and
22930 z. gold from 83,749 tons of ore. That is close on
1000 tons per day, and if the weather had been finer
the average would have been higher. The strength of
Mt. Lyell lies in the fact that, by extracting metal from
over 90 per cent, of the material fed to the furnace, it can
show exceptionally low costs ; and its smelting charge is
also almost perfect chemically, so that it can make blister
in two operations. If only Mt. Morgan could do the
same, there would be some firework dividends. But,
though Morgan’s practice will improve, it never will be
able to do what Lyell is doing with apparent ease.
* * * *
With its tin selling at over £174 per ton, Mount
Bischoff (Tas.) was able to double its distribution for De-
cember, and shells out 10s. per share (£6000). The pro-
duction of black tin concentrates is being maintained at
90 tons per month. On this output a rise of £ll per ton
in the value of concentrates gives the co. an extra 10s.
per share profit every six months. The average value of
the dirt treated is under J per cent, tin oxide ; in other
words, it takes over 200 tons of dirt to produce a ton of
concentrates. The Queen lode is, however, a fissure, and
of better quality than the big benches of decomposed
porphyry worked near the surface. The striking feature
about Mount Bischoff is the economical manner in which
the poor material is handled ; costs have sometimes been
as low as 4s. per ton.

* * * *
Seeing how much the little mining speculator
depends on the dailies for his information, it is as well
Australian markets should know that Adelaide gets
its press information of the Bullfinch from one source.
The stuff is sent to the Kalgoorlie Miner, which sends it
to the West Australian, which sends to both the old
Adelaide papers, and these send the worn and frayed
news to Sydney and Melbourne. And it’s all for cheap-
ness, and almost anything does so long as nobody who
can kick is libelled.
