More figures arrive concerning the Tanganyika
concessions, in mid-Africa, the huge proposition tow'hich
Queenslander Alan Gibb was taken as metallurgist. The
mines were, at latest advices, just starting production,
after a capital expenditure of over £1,500,000. They
reckon to work up to a production of 60,000 tons of metal
within three years—which isn’t any' too good news for
other copper-producers, for the world is pretty well sup-
plied already.
* * # *
“L. Lear”: The Bulletin propels a needed
brick against the Queensland State Bank. The institu-
tion is nominally there to help the farmer ; but the red
rope to cut, before the required aid can be got, bars hun-
dreds of good State customers. The clause re “ unspeci-
fied purposes” is like a 6ft. high jump to the “flat”
athlete. Take a case in point. Two young Australians
with small capital in three y'ears put improvements on
their block to the value of £6OO. Besides, they had
ploughs, other implements, horses and a small herd of
cattle. Crops failed through the dispensation of the con-
troller of water w'orks, and the men on the land were
unable to pay their rents (£9O) and rates (£2O). They
could not borrow from the State Bank because it will not
advance unless rents are fully paid up. Also, they would
have to state that the money was wanted for lib. tea,
three gallons flour, a pint of pins, and a kerosene tin, and
they weren’t sure that they would want the pins.
By the terms on which ‘ the land was selected
they could not mortgage to Private Enterprise
until five years’ residence had been fulfilled. Not-
withstanding the ample security, 20 acres of grow-
ing crop and their own personal integrity, they
were notified that their land was forfeited. The only
trouble was that they had run into a dead-end for ready
cash. Another cocky was advanced money to buy cows.
But he also purchased a horse to cart away his cream,
and for that his draft was dishonored. He couldn’t buy a
horse with cow money. When the Kidston has relieved
himself of the tons of State Frights that are sitting on his
chest, he might do worse than rake away the red-tape
entanglements, put a No. 12 boot to the door of the State
Bank, and allow some capital to flow out through the
cavity.
* * * *
“P.X.”: The idea begins to circulate that
almost any old thing can get a quotation on the Ade-
laide Stock Exchange; so that nowadays, when a pro-
position is turned down by the committee of (say) the
Sydney Exchange, the desperate promoter, anxious to
make a market for his shares, thinks at once of Adelaide
It is largely the fault of the Adelaide system. The Sydney
Exchange, for instance, has a committee which goes
through the articles and rules, etc., of a co. when it
applies to be put upon the list; and the fees paid by the
co. when it is listed go to the Exchange. In Adelaide the
similar committee, it seems, gathers in the fees for itself.
If a co. is unsound, or if it has a rule which gives (sav)
the vendors or other parties an unfair advantage over the
rest of the public, it is supposed to be passed out. The
Sydney committee is no worse off for passing a co. out;
while every refusal to put a co. on the Adelaide list
means something out of the committeemen’s pocket.
That may make not one atom of difference to the decision
of the committee ; but if Judges and jurors only got
their salaries and their fees when the prisoner was found
not guilty, some possibly uncharitable people would be
almost bound sooner or later to disparage the adminis-
tration of justice in that neighborhood.
* * * *
Most of the advts. calling on objectors to Bull-
finch mining-leases to speak up promptly at the Warden’s
office, or for ever hold their pence, are signed with as
short a name as there is in the Directory—Du. Not du
anything; just J. T. Du.
* * * *
Bullfinch railway should be completed by mid-
February, and the W.A. Government will then probably
decide to go on with a line from Southern Cross south
through Marvel Loch. Yilgarn is a great prospecting
field, but the big low-grade shows south of* the Cross
cannot do much without a line.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, January 2, 1911.
My Dkar Arint, —
The stars in their courses fought for the Sydney
theatres and concerts on New Year’s Eve. The
Chinese method of celebrating a festival—with
noises—being forbidden, and the shops being shut,
there was little for the people to do but go to
places of entertainment. The press says more
prople than usual got vine leaves in their hair.
Possibly. They had next day, Sunday, with the
New Year imposed upon it, in which to comb
them out and make good resolutions. On the
Saturday evening every house of mirth was full.
“The Whip” was sold out at 7.30, and “The
Girl from Rector’s ” about the same half-hour.
Grace Palotta’s clothes in this rectory frisk are too
sweet for words written in black ink. They should
be in rose pink extract of sweet-pea. A pink
cloth with big black buttons and collar is one
creation. There’s a toque of green silk that hugs
Grace’s head quite closely, and nobody quite likes
Grace in small toques. There’s a gown in
smoked ninon and murky green and dull pink;
and there’s a grey chiffon evening frock, and an
assortment of evening coats from chiffon to white
lace.
The Lucas-Tooth baronet has arrived on one of
his rare, brief visits to this great land whose
passion for beer has made him what he is. He
has a place at Kameruka, which he likes to see
occasionally ; also, he insists on peering into the
family beer-vat from time to time to assure him-
self that it is keeping up its old reputation.
There are a few Teeth still in N.S.W., but they
lack the affluence of Lucas. Many stories are
told in London about the family’s great wealth.
It is said that the first word the youthful Tooth
is taught is “money”; the second “beer.”
Sometimes a thoughtless nurse reverses the order.
The mistake is viewed at headquarters with a
tolerant eye; but let any other word in all the
bright lexicon of Webster be impressed on the
budding Tooth intellect, and the erring servitor
is either fried or frozen—l forget which.
Baronet Edmund Hayes and his wife departed
for Hibernian parts last week after giving the fine
flower of Sydney the best thing in good-byes at
the Rose Bay Golf Club. Although the Irish
baronet has possessed his title for 10 years, he has
not used it—at least, not on week days. His
wife was a Sydnev-sider, Miss Alice Wilkinson, a
daughter of an old Judge who died here recently.
The Hayes have a place at Como. Our Como,
you know; not the one in Switzerland. They
have, as well, a castle in Ireland—the real thing,
with a banshee and shamrocks, and tunder and
turf.
Mr. and Mrs. Cyril Ward and Captain Gilliat
left Australia last week. They are going to tour
Japan and then head for England, where Mr.
Ward has a constituency to nurse and Captain
Gilliat a regiment to rejoin.
The social columns of the Indian papers notify
Mrs. T. H. Kelly’s arrival at Lucknow. She was
a guest while there of the Hore-Ruth vens (he, you
will remember, was, till recently, Military Secre-
tary to the G.-G. here); and, in their charge, she
witnessed the Army Cup races and the polo
matches which rage in the siege-city at this time
of year.
Foreign wool-buyers are a notoriously cheerful
class. It is difficult to quench their light-hearted-
ness ; but last week, through the death of Mrs.
Leon Dewynne, who had been for some years a
genial and sympathetic hostess to the little foreign
wool-buying colony in Sydney, the latter were put
into genuine mourning, in spirit if not habiliment.
Mrs. Dewynne was a pretty young woman with
red-gold hair, a Sydneysider who usually frocked
in French fashion-plate form. She and her blond
husband were noticeable figures at race meetings
and first nights, and their devotion to each other
was remarkable in an age of marital drift-in-oppo-
site-directions. Before her marriage she was Miss
Maud Moseley. Mrs. Dewynne entered a private
hospital to undergo a minor operation, and died
24 hours after its performance.
Mrs. Claude McKay and her small boy go away
to see her people by next trip of the Somerset.
In view of her seafaring, her husband’s present to
her on the recent anniversary of her orange-
blossoming was appropriate. It was oddearrings—
a red jewel and a green jewel, signifying the star-
board and port'lights. As odd earrings happen to
be “coming in,” this will be counted unto
the lady for knowledgableness. Mrs. McKay’s
mother, it may be mentioned, is playing in the
provincial tour in “ Priscilla Runs Away,” and
her father (Mr. Thomas Sidney) is with Miss Ger-
trude Kingston at a metropolitan theatre.
More of our well-known people are flying north
about Eastertide. Mr. Owen Cox, managing-
director of the Federal-Houlder-Shire lines, his
daughter (Miss Doris Cox) and his niece (Miss
Gwladys Bensor) depart by the Mongolia in
April.
George Hoskins, eldest son of the ironworks
man who is going to start making rails for Aus-
tralian trains to run over, went to foreign parts a
few months back to see to the shipping of another
hundred thousand pounds worth or so of ma-
chinery. Having a few minutes to spare, and it
being an axiom in the Hoskins’s establishment
that minutes weren’t made to waste, the tremend-
ously-serious young Australian rushed to one of
Britain’s moated granges and got married. Bride
and bridegroom returned in time to buy a few
things for Christmas.
Sydney and the visitors thereto don’t know how
much they’ve missed in not seeing the romantic
comic opera, “A Moorish Maid,” at the Standard
Theatre. It ought to be at Her Majesty’s, and
the plain-thinking and high-kicking of the Gibbs
musical farce should be at the Standard. Music
and libretto of “A Moorish Maid” are by Austral-
asians ; and the first is excellent enough for any
capital city in the world, and the second is fairly
cleverand witty. Mrs. Slapoffski (LaZara) has been
in great form ; and Appleby and Cannam (Can-
nam is the Lauri of fun) and others have been
worthy of all Sydney’s applause.
London hostesses have been driven to the
drastic measures in operation at Australian
Government Houses as a means of checking the
incursion of uninvited unknowns to their parties.
At a big crush, the hostess rarely knows more
than a quarter of her guests by sight. Some she
has sent cards because she or her husband
owes them money; others are bidden in
the hope that they may ultimately become
creditors. Stockbrokers and their families
are asked, with an eye to useful tips.
All these, and hundreds of others who troop into
the ball-room, are accorded a smile of bogus
welcome and passed on to an excellent supper
and much sound champagne. For years past it
has been the custom of hungry and impecunious
young men to patronise any big ball that was
going. That is to say they patronised the food
and drink. Later, growing bolder, they started
taking their young women along for company.
The limit is said to have been reached when half
a dozen young roysterers came on from a Covent
Garden ball, with the ladies they had met
there, to Lady Somebody’s hop, and danced a
vinous measure, to the horror of the guests.
The fact that one of the Covent Garden ladies was
the discharged kennel maid of a friend of the
hostess brought about the downfall of the party.
The male members were in no mood to go quietly,
and a melee occurred that roused the neighbor-
hood. Now each guest at a big London party has
to supply clear proof of identity. It is likely that
a butler who is quick at reading finger-prints will
yet command a high figure in the halls of the
English nobility.
Which reminds me that London Star made a
smashing retort last month to the Daily Tele-
graph’s strictures on Mr. Redmond’s conduct in
returning to England, from America, with £40,000
of Home Rule gold in his pocket. It simply repub-
lished from a back number of the Telegraph a list
of Unionist peers—five dukes, two marquises and
seven earls—who among them had taken forty
million pounds sterling from the U.S. in the form
of Yankee heiresses’ bank accounts. Failing their
wives’ dots, a number of these noblemen would
have been too absorbed dodging judgment debtors,
ca. sa. orders, bankruptcy notices and so forth
to have a minute to spare for fighting against
Home Rule.
Thanks to the lady who writes under the inky
mask of Frank Dan by, we have a new name for
the golden-haired woman—chemical gold or
natural. In her latest book, “ Let the Roof Fall
In,” she has a woman whose strong point is her
pale-fawn hair. It was waved daily by the best
hairdresser in London and washed with white of
egg and champagne. She was Lady CarrieCarthew,
a widow of 35, a modern Becky Sharp, wickeder
perhaps, and more vulgar, and she was known as
the Yellow Peril. She used and misused men in
her yellow-perilous, fluffily-foolish fashion, and
she was as clever as a Yankee financier. No
matter how large was the hat fashion, she never
wore one large enough to cover her star attraction
—the pale-fawn hair. Wise Lady Carrie !
The March, April and June babies of “Eliza-
beth and Her German Garden ” are now father-
less. News from London tells of the death of the
Baron von Arnim of heart failure in Germany.
His interest to Australians lies in the fact that
his wife was a Sydney girl, a Miss Beecham,
daughter of a tea-merchant of years ago.
I’ve heard she spelt her name as Beauchamp is
pronounced. And she is a niece of Lassetter,
senior. In the “Garden” the late Baron
figured as “ the Man of Wrath.” A very lovable
monster he was, evidently. The Sydneysider
named her three daughters by the months of their
birth, and her references to the June, April or
March baby are full of a gentle humor. Whether
she would have loaded an infant with “ De-
cember ” or “ January,” had it arrived on either
of those months, would be another story. Baroness
von Arnim recently dramatised her novel, “Prin-
cess Priscilla,” and it was produced in London as
“ Priscilla Runs Away.”
Our one-time friend and visitor, Kubelik, has
been in Mr. Justice Channell’s court over a fid-
dling engagement that didn’t eventuate in time.
He sued a concert agent for £256 in respect of
certain concerts in 1908. The agent counter-
claimed, because Kubelik was not back in time
from America to take part in the first series of
the concerts. Kubelik recovered £l5O.
Mention of Mr. Justice Ohannell reminds me of
a story told at his expense by Dr. J. W. Smith,
K.C., of Perth (W.A.). Smith was one of the
counsel in an Admiralty Court case. So was
Channell. Now, Ohannell misused his h’s fear-
somelv. The cause of the litigation was a collision
in the” English Channel between a barque named
the Hannah and some other craft. Naturally,
Channell, the h-less called her the “’Anna.” At
last the presiding Judge asked: the
barque really named, Hannah or Anna?” Re-
plied Smith’s leader : “When she left Gravesend
she was the Hannah ; but she dropped an h in the
chops of the Channel.”
Mrs. Harbord’s name appears quite regularly
these days “ amongst those flying.” She was Mrs.
Arthur Blackwood, of Melbourne, until she
married a brother of Lady Carrington. It is a
pretty good record for one born in the autumn of
the nineteenth century; for flying over church
steeples and Eiffel Towers must demand several
ounces more nerve and courage than walking or
driving on the earth.
The most nineteen-hundred-and-elevenish thing
in raiment is a motor-coat with a camel’s fleece
lining. Ladies luxurious, who have such, say it is
the softest and warmest thing in pelts. Most of
us will learn with surprise that the camel has a
fleece. Camels I have known had skins very like
mousey and much-worn oilcloth. In fact, old
followers of the Prophet, owners of camel
teams in W.A., were in the habit (in
the early Coolgardie days) of stitching
oilcloth or felt over the sores on their camels’
backs—stitching on to the original stuff, you
know —and the darn would almost defy detection.
This unique tailoring would be done only when
some dog of a Christian buyer was to be had.
Some genius of the Aboriginal page will be sure
to write saying the foregoing is not true —but I
don’t care.
I have just read somewhere that our recent
visitor, Dr. Bell, the telephone man, is trying to
teach monkeys to talk. It sounds to me about as
futile as teaching flunkeys to stalk; besides,
there are already too many that talk,
and they talk a vast deal too much. To a certain
class of mind, there seems something fascinating
in the idea of upsetting the old order of things ;
thus when it is discovered, after profound
research, that a dog can bark and a man can
drink beer, there is a rush to teach men to bark
and dogs to drink beer. Also, I believe, there
is a great desire to evolve the tree-climbing
horse and the sky-rabbit. If there was any
hope of evolving a polished, eloquent and
grammatical monkey who would write the
History of Monkeydom in ten volumes, and
tell us all about the inner workings of man-
dril society I would be interested, but apparently
it is only intended to teach our arboreal friend to
shout “ pretty cocky,” or something of the kind,
on general principles as the parrot does, and that
bird’s unsupported statements already make my
head ache terribly. And even if the polished and
grammatical ape did arrive he might be a failure.
For instance, he might become Premier, and ex-
pose us to the ridicule of nations by addressing the
House with a book of reference held in his pre-
hensile foot and a mass of documents wrapped up
in his tail.
A lady of Yassberra —where the Federal capi-
tal is going to thirst beside the chain of waterholes
in which the Daily Telegraph’s angler doesn’t
go fishing—has written in impassioned terms to
King O’Malley, urging the claims of the waratah
to be appointed national flower to the Common-
wealth. The lady, who seems to have no use for
the wattle, says that “the back of the waratah is
strong and straight, and it holds its handsome
head erect and looks the whole world in the face.”
Evidently she considers the waratah as a sort of
red-faced vegetable Village Blacksmith who owes
not any man. Considering the size of Australia’s
lOU, about which the Wild Cat complains so
often, I don’t see how the fluffy golden wattle,
with its delicate suggestion of pawnshops, can be
ignored altogether.
The uprush of the waters of charity has nearly
blown the lid off the top of the bore. A friend,
who won’t allow a name to be mentioned, has sent
£5O for the Child Study Association charity. It’s
on a fragrant couch of amaranth and moly that
this donor will lie in the Future Beautiful. That
is, if he doesn’t prefer feathers. The hon. sec. of
the charity, in mentioning the number of cases of
infantile paralysis cured during the year just
ended, would like it known tha,t Dr. Alan Carroll
has of late undertaken the treatment of the
affliction in the adult.
Lewisham Hospital patients are telling their
visitors of the dual festival they had on the 25th
of December. It is six years since the great
Birthday fell on a Sunday ; it will be 1921 before
the biggest festival again falls on the lesser holy-
day. Patients tell that the wards were brilliantly
decorated, that everyone got presents of real
value, that the number and variety of winged
things eaten at every meal must have decimated
the edible feathered kingdom, and that no other
institution could have any chance with Charlotte
Russe, as that adorable thing was wooed to ex-
tinction at the Lewisham. These things are writ
because the patients complain that the glory of
their Christmas celebrations were not adequately
appreciated by the press. They were all on so
royal a scale that they deserved the National
Anthem.
If those who managed the recent Palace Theatre
production in aid of the Lewisham Hospital take
several sizes larger in hats after looking at the
figures on the cheque they handed to the head of
the hospital, no one dare bite a thumb at them,
or at Mrs. Boult, who wrote, rehearsed and
staged “Charley,” if she sees her shadow as long
as Pitt-street. The matinee was little heralded—
it was given on an afternoon of Satanic mugginess,
and no crowned heads were present; yet the
painting on the cheque ran —galloped rather —
close up to three hundred lovely pounds sterling.
Miss Rosina Buckman has been experiencing a
gently chequered career of late. Her engage-
ments, the rescinding of the same, and her re-
engagements must have made her feel she didn’t
know where she were. However, any disap-
pointment over the “ Waltz Dream” job must be
mitigated by the salve of salary, all the same.
Her engagement for the part of the Balkan
Princess in the production of that name is, how-
ever, a very definite thing, and not to be upset or
altered save by special intervention of Provi-
dence.
Another hospital matron has taken a yoke-
mate. Miss McGowen, of the Molong hospital,
was married last week to an officer on one of the
inter-State boats. She carried the usual bridal
outfit, plus an uncommon Brussels lace veil.
Miss Winifred Gullett—a daughter of the well-
known and very financial pressman who has had
the unique distinction of editing both S. M.
Herald and Daily Telegraph— is to be married
early in January. She weds a man from Colombo
—Mr. Arthur Etherington.
Port Moresby has taken toll of the well-known
Joubert family. In November, Jules Ernest, a
son of the Lismore Jouberts, died at the Papuan
port, aged 23. His elder brother, himself a young-
ster, was one of the pioneers thereward.
Mr. J. C. Williamson, who gets back here
about March, tells in a private letter that the
gaiety of capitals, if not of nations, seems to have
swung round to Berlin. The glare of its gaiety
throws Paris into the dusk. He wonders when
the inhabitants sleep. A man may go to an
opera-house or a theatre at seven in the evening,
and at two in the morning he may go to another
good show and be in time for the uprush of the
curtain. If any but one so trustworthy as J.C. W.
were to tell that there were theatres that opened
two hours after midnight, he might not be be-
lieved.
Medlow Hydro is densely inhabited. It owns a
new dining-room carpet, the laying of which was
a ceremony like unto a religious rite. The choice
textile is in soft, glowing, crimson tones. The
lamp-shades, fashioned by a lady from Liberty's
(a dealer in charms and spells, worked off in silks
and gauzes and art papers), are an extra reason
for merely viewing the place. Viewers’ shillings
for last year amounted to £5OO.
A Melbourne friend sends me 10s. for division
between the Hospice and the Child Study Associ-
ation. Thanks, friend.
From the English paper, Votes for Women
A verv unfortunate and ill-judged appointment is that
of the Eight Hon. Lewis Hareourt to the office of Secre-
tary for the Colonies. Keen resentment will be felt by
the women of Australia and Maoriland—who enjoj
liberties of self-government equally with men—that tr
representatives should in future be obliged to deal 1
their relations with the Mother Country with an avowed
opponent of women’s political enfranchisement and a
subscriber to the secret funds of the Anti-Suffrage Lea .
The Government would have been better advised had
they chosen for this office a man less tarred with tne
Anti-Suffrage brush. For the women in Australia and
Maoriland have every right to protest against the affront
that has been offered to them.
The women of M.L. and Australia 95 per cent,
of them certainly—don’t know that Harcourt is
Secretary for the Colonies. The average woman
has a vague idea that she has heard of ‘ Culu
Harcourt, but probably thinks “she ” is a dancer
at the Hippodrome.
Pursuing the subject to its native lair : I see
that our friend, Lord Beauchamp, lately received
a deputation in favor of Woman Suffrage. He
told it that his sympathies were with it
His personal experience in Australia showed him that
it was for the general good of the country as a whole that
its women should be enfranchised ; that women would
never have proper attention paid to the question of their
wages until they received political rights, and that ne
found that the standard of political morality was raised
when women took part. Nevertheless, he declined to
pledge himself to any particular measure, but expressed
the hope that in the course of the next few months the
Movement would get sufficient public support to enable
it to pass a Bill into law.
Meantime the leaders of the Movement (note the
capital M in our ex-Gov.’s quoted remarks) are not
taking any chances—except of gaol—and continue
to smite the enemy on his shiny hat or his window-
panes.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, January 2, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Chirnsides have bagged the Prince, and all
the other wealthy woolgrowers are in despair.
What is a motor visit from a G.-G. going to count
now in homestead chalk-marks after the Chirn-
sides’ royal record in bush hospitality? Prince
Leopold left Federal Government House on Sun-
day for Carrandallac, where Gordon Chirnside,
brother of Pursey, dumps his wool-bale.
The Dudleys have departed to Ercildoune,
Lady Sarah Wilson’s station, where Melba rested
for some months during her last Australian visit.
The Carmichaels are summering at Macedon, in
the lovely Jarrah cottage there. The visiting
Prince went from the G.-G.’s to the Macedon cot
for a day or two before he passed on to the Chirn-
sides’ wattle-and-bark hut. Sassiety in general
has gone to hill and sea, and when it hasn’t gone
it has to pretend that it has. Sweet girls of the
subbubs only motor into town for necessary shop-
ping ; it isn’t form to be seen in the city
except in motor-hats or the Dorothy-Angelica
type of sunbonnet—pink and blue and lilac—
or in yachting rig or seaside clothes. It is all
very instructive and entertaining. Children’s par-
ties are the only “ events.” If the frivols are not for
real children, they are for grown-up irresponsibles.
There was a fine crop of Christmas trees before
the general exodus. The Dudleys discovered
one growing in their ballroom ; and that necessi-
tated a most exclusive pony-party. The creamiest
of the youth of Melbourne motored along, and
were fed liberally with toys and bonbons. The
aides had to implore the help of military pals to
keep order and regulate the number of pink ice-
creams consumed.
The latest bait on the summer boarding-house
line is the announcement that a dark room is
available for photographers. In these days of day-
light developers, few amateurs bother about a
dark room ; but it has its advantages all the same.
Look-outs and cosy-corners in fern gullies are poor
retreats when compared with a dark room, if an
affectionate pair have some films to develop.
Marriages and approaching marriages are help-
ing to make the holiday season joyful. Ethel
Fisken, one of the smart Toorakety team, takes
over Dickie O’Hara for better or worse in
February. Richard, from Bananaland, is the son of
the late C. O’Hara, who lived in the northern
State, but really belonged to County Sligo.
Another Australian whose speech retains a sug-
gestion of Dublin is among the marrying folk.
Dr. “ Chuck ” Dane, who is formally recorded as
Dr. Paul Greig Dane, married the pretty little
brunette Ruth Parker early in December. Mrs.
Dane is the youngest of the pretty Parker girls of
Williamstown. The knot was tied at Holy
Trinity.
For the Bevan-Hurvitch wedding, at which the
pastor himself asked the fateful questions, the
Independent Church was turned into a garden.
The bride drew wealth behind her in the shape of
a train of exquisite Limerick. Mrs. Bevan, the
bride’s mother, has some of the most beautiful
old lace in Melbourne. Sister Doctor Sybil left
her Queensland practice for the occasion, and
came down to act as first bridesmaid. The
Springthorpe chicken Enid acted as second, and
a small niece helped in the mistv-blue ninon sym-
phony. Mrs. Bevan, who always wraps her
beautiful grey hair in priceless lace, wore a small
bonnet of the dainty material and a marvellous
silk Indian shawl.
Rupertswood, Baronet Clarke’s country man-
sion, is not to be opened as a mere batching estab-
lishment, the Russell Clarkes having arranged to
take up their abode there early in 1911. It is a
beautiful, roomy old place that breathes comfort
in spite of continuous disuse. During one neg-
lected period the ballroom was actually used as a
barn, and the smooth dancing floor became dinted
and scarred by hob-nailed boots. Probably the
ill-treated kauri boards will have their furrows
smoothed out now by kind carpenters with planes.
The members of the Sydney Commercial
Travellers’ Cricket Club have been playing on the
South Melbourne Cricket Ground by day, and
theatre-going by night. Last Wednesday even-
ing they invaded Rickards’s, and I noticed that
some bf them had brought pretty wives with
them. I particularly admired the wives’ com-
plexions. Sydney girls, when they are not regu-
lar surfers, have a delicate hothouse look which
distinguishes them from the wind-tanned Mel-
bournians. Complexion-builders are keen on
steam-vapor doses ; so the coastal moist heat of
Sydney must be considered a valuable asset from
a beauty point of view. It so chanced that
Manager Rickards had an interesting programme
for the visitors. The Marbas pair of French
dancers, who do all their talking with their hands
and eyes, were the human stars of the Dill. Bar-
nold’s clever animals were the dog stars. They
raised shrieks of the mincing
hobble-skirter. When a man with a concertina
started to play something from “ Tannhauser,”
the Travellers began to stream out chanting their
war-cry: “We are so dry! We are so dry!”
But the concertina-player rose—or fell—to the
occasion. Dropping Wagner with a thud, he
started to pump out the hilarious chorus. Then
the Travellers smiled again, and stayed to hear
him out.
The Sydney team of commercial traveller
cricketers beat Melbourne out of sight, and there
was a wild finale of dining and smoke-night.
Harry Holden, formerly secretary of the Club in
Sydney, presented the inevitable trophy and a
bouquet of suitable flowers of speech.
There are rows and rows of pretty girls in
“The Gay Gordons”; but they have to merely
hang round while the intricate Scotch jokes are
being sprung on the audience, which wears the
puzzled look of a stoical boxer who can’t exactly
figure out where he’s being,hit. The show would
go better if some of the jests were unravelled a
little and the girls given a better chance. No one
is so dull that he can’t see a pretty girl when she
is served up with plenty of limelight. Manager
Meynell has brought a well-proportioned daughter
with him. Her voice is rather thin ; but as the-
damsel is still in her. very early teens, singing-
teacher Time has plenty of chance to effect im-
provements. She calls herself Miss Mabel Dark
—apparently for no other reason than that she is
divinely fair. The kilt-clad ballets help to re-
lieve the tension produced by the highly-technicali
Scotch jests. There is, besides, a trace of Gil-
bertian humor about the show. An American
girl weeps because she has unwittingly fallen in
love with a title, and an old Scotch dame does
likewise because her adopted child —whom she
spanked cheerfully as plain “Angus”—is proved
to be a belted earl.
The raging bellowdrammer now at the Prin-
cess’s offers one good idea to the woman smoker.
The comedy-man, disguised as a woman, forces
his way into the Princess Ida selectness of a
drapery warehouse where the girls “sleep in.”
Left to himself, he produces a cigarette from the
knob of a monster hatpin, and gets the necessary
match out of the other knob.
Norman Lindsay and Aleck Sass, laden with
luggage and experiences, will totter ashore down
the same liner’s gang plank before this ink is dry,
much to the delight of Australia at large. .At
Fasoli’s, just before holiday time, two other wan-
derers met and compared notes. Charlie Nuttall,
journalist and artist, who has been well appre-
ciated in N’Yark, has returned, to work con-
tentedly in Melbourne. Jimmy Macdonald was
the other home-comer. He is the son of solicitor
Macdonald, and has been in foreign parts for
12 years, half of which he spent in Paris
studying, and the rest in New York teach-
ing millionaires’ daughters the way to put
paint otherwhere than on their cheeks. The spoilt
darling of one Trust king neglected her class
shamefully. “Jimmy” gave several degrees, of
latitude to her, but at last he complained to Mrs.
Dollars. “Why,” exclaimed the millionairess,
“Sadie has been most devoted ! We can’t get
her to think about anything but Art and her
lessons!” A general shake-up all round dis-
covered that several months earlier Miss Sadie
had married on the sly some six-dollar-a-week
Adonis, and the Art school was a grand
screen for the double life. The fat which
was then in the fire blazed so that it
burned a deep hole in Macdonald’s memory.
Peace may have eventually settled on the home
but Jimmy lost a good paying pupil. Jimmy,
by the way, did a bit of marrying himself while
he was away. The girl is a Virginian and a
specialist in child portraits. She is at present
working on a commission from a doting grand-
mother in America, who wants a picture of the
artist’s little son.
Five more sweet girl doctors appeared at the
conferring of degrees last week. Dr. Sweet’s
sister Elizabeth is one. Another is Rachel
Gross, whose father is a Fitzroy councillor.
Margaret McLorinan sets a strong Scotch seal on
the list, which is completed by Gertrude Buzzard
and Mary Glowrey. Some new diseases will have
to be discovered if we are to keep all our young
medicine people supplied with patients and
guineas.
Gladys Chirnside—daughter of the late Robert
and the lady who is now Mrs. Justice Hodges—is
just back from flatting in London with little Miss
Fenner. The lady recently made one of a tiny
and fearfully exclusive dinner party, given by the
Dudleys in honor of Leopold of Battenburg.
The young widow of poor old Bert Armytage is
back in Melbourne fixing up those hurriedly-left
affairs. Later on she and the small daughter will
cross to restful Tasmania for a summering.
J. F. Treadway v one of the men who have gob
very rich by selling Melbourne a vast number of
yards of ribbon and covering with it a vast
number of frocks, died at his home at Kew on
New Year’s Day. Practically he was known in
only two circles—the rag trade, which knew him
very well and summed him up as one of the keen-
est of buyers ; and athletic sport, which knew him
pretty well, and often had the help of his heavy
pocket. Thirty years ago he was quite
sure that the time would come when the rag-trade
that was best worth having would be done in the
near suburbs ; and he laid himself out to get it,
and succeeded so well that he probably dies one
of the wealthiest men in the clothes business.
With his friend Fitzgerald he tripped to Java
after a severe illness last year ; but his constitu-
tion was apparently past rebuilding, and a chill
ended the struggle.
Fred B. Norton, who left for another plane at
the end of the year, set out several times on the
last long journey before he flitted for good. First
it would be reported that he had had a breakdown,
and had gone out of town to recuperate; then
serious reports of his illness would drift down
Collins-street and alarm his numerous friends;
almost immediately afterwards, Norton himself
would be discovered somewhere in town smiling
at Rumor and gaily denying that the Man with
the Scythe had been looking for him seriously.
Box Hill was the spot he usually retired to during
his illnesses ; but the end came at pretty Ivanhoe,
on the way to old Heidelberg. During one of his
Box Hill visits, the local bank was invaded by-
armed masked men and robbed. A friend immedi-
ately wired the sick man : “ Halves, Freddie ! ”
' On Thursday a presentation was made to Mrs.
Malcolm, the late secretary of the Trained Nurses’
Association. The beautiful rose-bowl was handed
over to its new owner by Mrs. Ayres, on behalf of
the people who had come in contact with Mrs.
Malcolm during her term of office. Also a marble
clock was forthcoming for daughter Marion, who
had been her assistant. The pleasant little busi-
ness took place at the Swastika Club, which Mrs.
Malcolm is managing ; and several musical items
bucked up the proceedings.
This is the romance of a post office. A boy who
had just entered the service was shown a letter,
simply addressed to Miss Selina Somebody, Mel-
bourne. “ That’s the sort of thing you will have
to learn, young man,” said the senior. “ Have a
look in the directory for a name like that.”
“That’s my aunt’s name,” said the boy ; “she’s
lived with us for years.” So good old auntie, who
had been the faithful drudge of a houseful of care-
less youngsters, came into her own at last, for the
letter was from an old flame who had desired her
hand and heart many years ago. He had pros-
pered, and had written the letter on the off chance
that it would reach the right Selina by some acci-
dent. And the accident happened.
Mrs. Desmond, who travels with the hubby
whose present task is to fill the awful gap left by
Julius Superbus, is a sister of Nance O’Neill;
and she shares the Nance type of frailness in the
matter of build. She was first favorite on the
out-coming ship, but used to shake the barque to
its garboard streak every time she sat firmly on
deck.
The Melbourne Cricket Club has been firing a
farewell salute with champagne corks in honor of
Major Ben Wardill, now in the 60’s, who is re-
tiring from the secretarial job after 30 years’
valuable service. The big ground by the railway,
A great Peace Promoter is Sport. If all
the nations played cricket and sought wise inspira-
tion from D.C.L. Wisdom, the racial competitive
spirit would find satisfaction in Test matches.
Cricket balls instead of cannon balls, and the
multitude exercising the sportsman’s restraint
instead of battle fury. Sport in the cause of
peace is the greatest ally of D.C.L., the Whisky
of the Wise.*
with its half-circle of towering grandstands, will
not seem the same without him.
Stage dancing is getting further and further
back into the mists of antiquity. Oriental squirm-
ing has had its revival and been discarded in favor
of something still older. Nofru-re, a woman with
a face like Cleopatra, has been appearing in
Baden-Baden in dances alleged to be reprod actions
of the ones performed before Pharaoh, in the temple
of Amon (The Hidden One). Nofru-re has made
a close study of the high reliefs and wall paintings
of Egypt, and has strung the pictured poses into
a complete dance, exactly as a finder of pie-
historic bones reconstructs from them a complete
and awesome monster. She wears her black hair
in a pigtail, with a pudding at the end, and
nothing else worth mentioning except an anklet
on one rosy limb.
The latest fashion is designed for falling avia-
tors. Sometimes, of course, the aviator does not
fall ; but in upper circles it is considered the thing
to dress for it. The descending dress looks re-
markably like grandmother’s crinoline, and an
airman disguised in it bears a strong resemblance
to Charley’s Aunt. The inventor is a young
Berlin “shover.” Put the garment on and it
looks like a very loose ulster ; but take a leap
from your aeroplane, and the thing opens out like
an umbrella, leaving the hands and arms above-
board to manipulate the rubber steering-gear.
Sounds all right so long as you don’t get mixed up
with your wrecked machine.
That old-established culprit, the printer, claims
indulgence for a confusion of “copy,” whereby
Maggie McCann appeared in last week’s Chatter
as the sister of Rita Kiddle, whereas it is really
Mrs. Major Maloney who can claim sisterhood to
Miss Maggie. The tail-end of one paragraph
must have dropped from its moorings and got
hitched to the previous par. Miss Kiddle, as all
squattocracy knows, is the only daughter of the
parental “Billy.”
Said the railway porter :
“What’s that? Glad the holidays are over? Oh, no !
Not at all 1 Half-past 5 a.m. to 11 and 12 p.m. for a
week right off—now would you like that to be over ?
“ It isn’t so much the actual work, but the fool ques-
tions and extra trouble that foolish people give us. It’s
my opinion that only fools travel on holidays ; and they
ali come to us to find out everything. Mostly we send
’em to the Inquiry Olerk. They drive him nearly dotty,
but then he’s paid for it. Lost children are plentiful.
They are sent to the S.M.’s office, and then, if the parents
don’t turn up, the police take them.
“ On Boxing night a small man rushed round asking us
if we had seen his wife. She was lost. We sent him to
the John on the corner. He’s got less to do than we
have. He told us about it after. The little man was
sure she’d got run over or something, and was either in
the hospital or the morgue. She was a shy thing, not
used to battling in a crowd, he said. The John rang up
the watchhouse. She was there—the shy thing. Beer
and battle.
“Yes, women are the worst. They’ll rush into the hall
and ask three or four strangers, one after another, where
they get tickets for Brighton, They don’t want to go to
Brighton at all; Brighton now is the old North Brighton.
They want to go to Beach. But that’s the fault of the
new-fangled changing of names all round. They go and
look at the window, then go and ask the man on the gate
what time the next train goes and which platform it goes
from. The information is stuck up, but that doesn’t
matter. Then they go back and block all other passengers
while thev ask the ticket-clerk. The crowd pushes them
along ; but be'fore they reach the train they have asked
two or three more persons all about it. Yes, like enough
they get there, and home again ; and I suppose they en-
joy themselves.
‘“No, I don’t want a holiday—what I want’s a rest.
No, we don’t get overtime for this, but I’ll get time off to
make up for it later on, if I’m lucky. Then I think I’ll
go to sleep. Anyway, I won’t go anywhere where I’m
liable to be asked what time the 9.40 goes.”
An account of a drawing-room meeting for the
discussion of the Vote, held at the house of another
friend of ours, Lady Brassy, makes amusing
reading because of Lord Brasseey, who was in the
chair, declaring that he “had devoted many
anxious days and sleepless nights to the considera-
tion of this momentous question, and had not yet
finally come to a decision.” Isn’t that a lovely
bit of Brass? Then Mrs. Pankhurst spoke, and
apparently caught and skewered the noble lord’s
views that were dragging their anchors, for when
he replied thanking the lady he as good as
intimated that the country would go to the
demnition bow-wows if it did not give
her the vote. Half a yard of peeresses edged
with peers and other of the slightly-lesser nobility
were at the meeting. That is, half a yard of them
in newspaper print.
The other day I picked up a library copy of
Maurice Drake’s prize novel, “ The Salving of a
Derelict,” and stumbled on the pencil tracks of a
margin scribbler. On page 284, the hero, who
means every word of it, gets this off his chest:
“Man alive, she’s the world to me. I’d lie down
and put her little foot on my throat.” The com-
ment for that impassioned speech is “Bali!”
Again, on page 319, the hard-up heroine, on un-
expectedly getting £l6OO for some old scrip
hitherto considered worthless, uses up a slice of
the small fortune in clothes and amusements:
“She spent nearly £lOO of her new capital in 10
days, and, in satisfying her womanly yearning for
pleasures and pretty things, was able to con-
gratulate herself on havinghadher money’s worth.”
“Cat!” snaps the envious reader. I was doubt-
ful about the scribbler’s sex until I struck that.
The lonely bushwoman bas to meet some big
emergencies single-handed ; and she rarely fails.
Last week an isolated housewife, whose husband
and sons had gone off for the day, was getting
through her work when a flying spark , set the
hessian lining of the house in a blaze. Of course,
there was no time to run for a dish of water, or to
hunt for something to beat it out with. There
was just time to jump at the blazing stuff and tear
it down with bare hands. That is what the plucky
woman tfk?, thus not only saved the house,
but the miles of dry grass outside. Now, in a
bellowdrama, the large adjacent landholder would
gratefully come forward in the fourth act and
insist on paying the wounded heroine’s doctor’s
bill. The occasion seems to demand some sort of
demonstration of the kind, and I hope the adjoin-
ing squatter (if he exists) will perceive that the
fourth act has now arrived.
From “ Rosna,” of Adelaide
Glenelg-on-the Patawaloriga fed the pioneers as usual
on December 28, to celebrate the fact that 74 years ago
Governor Hindmarsh stood under a gum tree and pro-
claimed ns a province. His Ex. was at the 74th feast, as
well as portions of the Ministry, to say nothing of Prime
Minister Fisher, who dropped in from South Africa at the
psychological moment, and added a few sensible words
to the speech list. The plan was adopted for a year or
two of lunching the women pioneers as well as their
brethren ; but we have dropped it, not having had much
encouragement to do anything else.
An interesting visitor at the Commemoration was
Queensland’s Government Botanist, F. M. Bailey. He came
to South Australia in 1839 with his father, who was this
State’s first Government Botanist, and planted our first
baby Botanic Garden. Their original land, down near
Hacknev-road, has been known as Bailey s Garden ever
since. Mr. Bailey’s son, Director of Brisbane’s Botanic
Gardens, is with him in Adelaide.
Amonv our living pioneers about the most remarkable
is T S* Reed, who, at 93, is still secretary to our
Geographical Society, and still a man-about-town and an
active citizen generally. At the moment he is contribu-
ting a series of articles on “ Christmas Contrasts, _ Ihe
Derivation of Words,” and such-like divergent topics to
the daily press.
The two-day?! Christmas meeting of the Port Adelaide
Racing Club was a triumph in the matter of attend-
ance, in spite of the fact that on Monday a fierce hot blast
was throwing’ bricks about the atmosphere. Saturday s
weather was a thing of joy, however, and the pretty
little course was ornamented by a gay and frocky crowd.
Little less than £32,000 went through the totalisator in
the two days. The most exciting circumstance in the
matter of dividends was the £37 which Fastness paid his
faithful. . ..
The hospitable mayoral Cohens leave early in April to
carrv Adelaide's felicitations to the new Crown. Other
voyagers are to be Lisle Johnson and his bride. They are
to be married earl 3' in February, and will leave imme-
diately to make a thorough examination of the world.
Adelaide has been full of bowling enthusiasts all the
holidays, the champions from the other States having
come over to dust the landscape with the local kings and
princes of the ancient game. Various revels have de-
voured the visitors’ spare seconds. One day there was
a reception in the Town Hall, with speeches attached.
On Wednesday the party was dragged to Long Gully, one
of our beauty spots, where views run riot. On this occa-
sion, President S. J. Jacobs handed a gold watch from
the S.A. Association to popular penman Clarence Moody,
who has done more for the game in South Aus. than any
other one. . . _ . ,
South Australia has been entertaining the Lari ana
Countess of Harrowby, and a son and daughter. The
Earl is elder brother to Captain Ryder, who once aided
our Ex., and married the C. H. Angases’ daughter Beryl.
The visitors are being entertained with all due ceremonial
and trumpet-flourishing at the Angas homestead, where
the prize oxen come from.
You can now read the enlightening statement in Ade-
laide Sassiety news that Mrs. Blank has taken a house at
Toorak. The name has been tacked on to a segment of
Rose Park by fearfully original people who wanted to sup-
ply tone to the neighborhood. We now have Norwood,
Kensington, Brighton, Cro3’don, Cheltenham, Hackney,
Henley, Exeter, Goodwood, Brompton, Toorak and count-
less other second-hand names. In fact, Bowden-on-the-
hill is about the only one we can really call our own.
so far as the suburbs are concerned. But our country
villages still sing some native music to you. I never hear
of Willunga, Yankalilla, Myponga, Caltowie, and a hun-
dred others without dreading that someday some stodgy
person will score tbe names off the map and write in
Jonesville, Bosankevtown, or Slushton-on-Slime instead.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.
From the Oldest Inhabitant
A well-known land mark of old Melbourne has just
been demolished —the shop which was dwarfed by
Alston’s sky-scraper on the corner of Oollins and
Elizabeth streets. The little place was built as far
back as 1845, and in the ’so’s and up to 1861 was occupied
by Edward Arnold, a bookseller. This corner half-acre
was purchased at the first Melbourne land sale (June,
1837) by one Ross, a carpenter and builder. He cut it up
into allotments to suit various people who wanted to
build on it. On the corner Michael Cashmore, a Hebrew,
had a drapery store which was known as No. 1, Mel-
bourne. Cashmore was afterwards the first man to
superintend tiie regular slaughter of meat for the Jewish
community of Melbourne. When Arnold gave up busi-
ness he sold the balance of his lease to the Argus people.
They sent a clerk named Read to open there Melbourne’s
first “block ” agency. A son of the original owner, Ross,
is now an inmate of a home in Melbourne.
“Smith, alias Jones,” sees a stranger :
Last week, in a Sydney street, I saw a bald-headed
bare-headed gentleman sitting in a motor-car, in a yellow
nightie, posing for a camera man. I learned that he was
Sasana-Dhaja, Buddhist monk of the Order of the
Yellow Robe—otherwise Mr. Stevenson,,from Yarmouth,
where the bloaters come from. It seems that he has
taken 10 vows, including absolute poverty and absolute
chastity, and has accepted 225 rules to guide his life ;
that he shaves his head ; that he must wear no hat, no
boots, no shirt—in fact, all he possesses are two yellow
robes, one on and the other at the wash. As he doesn’t
come to Australia to make converts, I expect he does it
to try and forget some of the 10 vows and 225 rules.
They must be very trying to live with.
Sasana, who comes from Burmah, explains that there
are 700 monks and 1000 nuns in his Order. He says i hat
some of the women were charming, with creamy skin,
and beautiful hair hanging below their waists. Now they
shave their heads and walk about bareheaded and bare-
footed, and wear the same yellow robe as the men, with
a skintight chemise over the upper part of their bodies ;
and I don’t know how he knows.
A monk may not look at a woman. He must not look
at money. He must not ride behind horses. (Thank
Heaven for motor-cars !) He must not live under the
same roof as a woman. He muse not kill a flea. He
must not beg, but he goes out in the morning carrying a
little wooden bowl, and if he does not approach the
faithful so that they may put food in his bowl, lm has
cast upon them a curse. And yet Sasana-Dhaja tells us
that the Buddhist monks live beautiful lives, full of holi-
ness and joy. He has never heard of wrong-doing or sin
among them. They have neither sorrow nor pain nor
suffering.
I forgot to mention that he has taken a vow to tell
always the bare, bald, naked truth.

PERFECTLY MOULDED FIGURES.
Another Medical Discovert.
To get out of condition is detrimental to beauty
of form as well as to health. There is .a certain
flabbiness which robs a good figure of charm and
grace. Those who are beginning to grow fat
rapidly are never in the pink of condition. But
here medical science has slipped in with a very
wonderful remedy—one which not only restores a
perfectly moulded form, but renews health and
strength. We give the recipe in full for the
benefit of our stoutish friends :—One ounce of
fluid extract of Glycyrrhiza 8.P., one ounce of
pure Glycerine 8.P., one half-ounce of Marmola,
and Peppermint Water to make six ounces in all.
Any chemist will make up this prescription, or
supply you with the ingredients which you can
mix yourself. The dose is two teaspoonfuls after
each meal. The great virtue of this harmless
remedy is that while the excess fat is being ex-
pelled, the person under treatment has a healthy
appetite and does not need to study dietetics or
go in for tiresome exercises. The whole s3 - stem is
“refreshed ” and invigorated, the blood renewed
and muscular development restored.*

WHAT CONSTIPATION MEANS.
Constipation resembles nothing so much as the
stopping of the sewerage system of a city, and
vou know the outbreak of disease that would
result if this should happen. Then try to realise
the danger to yourself if you allow your bowels to
become clogged. The poisonous matter which
should pass off is absorbed into the blood, which
is the cause of numerous diseases. When you are
troubled this way there is just one thing to do-
get a box of Chamberlain’s Tablets. They cleanse
and invigorate the stomach and bowels, and clear
the system of all foul accumulations and restore it
to a normal condition.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
UNSUPPLIED DETAILS.
CUSTOMER : “ Excuse me, Miss, but what age was
this chicken when it met its death ?”
WAITRESS : “ Well, sir, we don't generally supply
a death certificate with the poultry; hut if
you wait a moment the manager might be
able to fix you up.”
The weird Wimmen’s League of Victoria
is beginning' well. One of its lecturers lately
floated into Cnniperdown (Vic.), and gave the
proposed industrial referenda a stupendous lash-
ing. Firstly, she opined that there would be a
great deal of impersonation at the referenda polls,
and that some of their opponents would probably
vote eight or nine times. Then working up into
a condition of frenzy and falling hairpins, she let
drop these thrilling words: “If such a dreadful
thing as the referendum proposals were carried, I
would not be surprised if it resulted in civil war.
Under Socialism the home will be broken up, and
the children taken away from their parents by the
Government.’’ The sweet exploiter of those dear,
dead old fibs is a perfect lady of the name of
Maise. Which only shows what ill-assorted
matches are made in this world, because the lady
most certainly should have married a gentleman
named Chaff.
Sydney Daily Telegraph last week cried
out at the top of its voice for some prompt
Federal interference with the frozen meat
trade. The nodules—or, to give them
their ugliest name, worm cysts—which
are found in Australian beef have
frightened the London health authori-
ties, who have decreed that affected
meat mustn’t go into consumption
until the mysterious lumps have
been out out. As the regulation
is strictly enforced, much of the
fodder is put up for sale in a muti-
lated state, with the result that the
exporters’ bank balances have had
holes chopped in them. Most of
the affected meat comes from
Queensland ; but as J. Bull is too
busy to worry about details, and
knows only “Australian ” meat, the
whole continent has to suffer for
the Bananaland bullock’s sins. So
even the parish-minded press recog-
nises that the urgent matter is too
big for the States to handle, and
therefore calls out to the Federal Go-
vernment to “ save the position.” Take
it on the whole, it is a remarkably re-
freshing shriek for help, for it indicates
that, after all, the Parish Pump knows
that it isn’t the fire engine it pretends
to be.
The gentle, trustworthy Asiatic again !
Fremantle (W. A.) gaol harbors one specimen of
him, a fiend with 53 convictions, lately sentenced
to a further five years. He should have been de-
ported long ago; but the State won’t, and it
would be a gross interference with precious State
frights if the Commonwealth did the job. So the
fiend stays on, and when he isn’t an expense to
the people he is a menace to them.
The breaking up of the big estates is stir-
ring the dry bones in many a N.S.W. country
town. At least three new country daily papers
are breaking into blossom with the new year.
Tam worth, the northern town which by now
would have had a 25,000 population if the Peel
River Co. and other big landlords hadn’t choked
it. will have one; and Wagga will have two. At
Tam worth the two old tri-weeklies have been
bought by a local co. and amalgamated ; but the
Wagga pair are going to fight it out.
Bigjohn Forrest of Westralia, always a
hefty borrower before the Lord, brightened up
the glad new year by a statement of opinion that
naval and military expenditure shouldn’t be met
out of revenue, but should be charged to loans.
Surely the man who would borrow to pay a sailor’s
wages, or to liquidate the bill for an ironclad,
would borrow for anything beneath the sun.
Even a town hall has points about it as a loan
asset compared with a warship. It doesn’t get
shot at as a general thing, and it doesn’t go to sea
and sink in deep water.
Brisbane Tory papers remark mournfully
on the alacrity with which Queensland State civil
servants seize any opportunity of getting trans-
ferred into the Federal service. The latest example
is H. D. Brennan. Public Service Inspector, who
has just been appointed a Deputy Land-Tax Com-
missioner. In spite of Phidston, and Wade, and
Wilson, and the “National” Ass, the State public
servants know that the powers of the Common-
wealth will grow, while those of the States will
shrink, and they naturally seek the bigger oppor-
tunities and the bigger outlook of the Australian
service.
“ T. Tuffchunter” : Possibly Colonel Gos-
low, who lately gave a Sydney harbor picnic to
some hundreds of bis political supporters, has
been reading a pamphlet issued by the Tory
crowd in England : “ How to Win an Election ;
or the Workers’ Guide,” recommended by the
Tory leader Balfour. It says: “The average
working man has two talismans which appeal
most powerfully to his heart. They are beer and
baccy.” It, therefore, recommends giving smok-
ing concerts, with beer, as a good form of
“association work.” But whether the Colonel
has been reading the pamphlet or not. I hope
his guests liked the beer and baccy they con-
sumed, and are properly grateful to the aw—
giver.
One Nathan, of Sydney, writes to the
papers concerning the Commonwealth land tax
under the heading: “How Labor Hits Labor.”
Nathan’s story is that the rich property-owner is
not only going to pass the tax on to the poor
tenant —“ he is going to add a little to it, so as
to derive a profit from it in addition to making
political capital out of it.” And, having- described
the swindle, Nathan heads it: “ How Labor Hits
Labor.” In the same way, if Nathan were robbed,
he would, no doubt, write to the papers blaming
the police for it, and hinting that the force ought
to be abolished.
David Storey’s new Democratic party in
N.S.W. starts with one asset, anyway. It has
secured the active hostility of Sydney Telegraph.
The Telegraph upheld Freetrade. and it fell
dead ; it went bald-headed for Anti-Socialism, and
the cause expired ; it urged the people to stand
bv the Fusion, and the Fusion was routed ; it
preached Dreadnausea, and the scheme blew out
in a gale of derision. At different times the D.T.
has furiously opposed an Australian navy, uni-
versal service, arbitration and conciliation, a
land tax, old age pensions. Labor in the Aus-
tralian Parliament and the N.S.W. Assembly, and
about 178 other successful causes. It has come to
be a Parliamentary axiom that “ the Telegraph’s
enemy is the elector’s friend.” Wherefore David
looks at the press Goliath, and cheerfully en-
courages him to come on and do his worst.
Private Enterprise is still fuming over the
starting of the State coal mine in Victoria. The
other day the chairman of the Outtrim Howitt
Co. wound up the usual complaint of unfairness
and mismanagement by asserting that if Private
Enterprise were allowed to take up leases on the
field, his co. would soon supply the public with
coal at a lower price than the State could do it.
The trouble with virtuous Private Enterprise is
that it won’t give the public this cheap service
when it has the chance. For instance, the Mel-
bourne Tramway Co. made a profit of £172.902
last year; it could have charged the public
£150.000 less, and still have pocketed 7 per cent,
on its outlay. But did it reduce the fares, so. as
to cut out the £150,000 this current year ? Did it?
CONFORMING TO THE CONVENTIONAL.
ARTIST : “ When are you going to sit to me for
a picture!”
SHE: “ Oh, I’m afraid I wouldn’t he a very
good model.”
HE: “ Well — er—but you would have your
chaperon there.”
Sydney Daily Telegraph was patronising
concerning “ the first utterances of the Common-
wealth Prime Minister on his return from South
Africa” ; and it spoke in a large, impressive way
about our “ world-encircling Empire, with a com-
mon ideal and a common destiny.” Where it is
a question of a nebulous, grandiose affair like
Imperial unity, the Telkgiiaph can be as broad as
a smoking-room anecdote. But when it is a
question of Australian unity—a common-sense,
practical proposition, the like of which Fisher
has just seen in actual operation in S’Africa—the
D.T. can't “ widen its range of vision ” an inch.
It froths about “ a world-encircling empire with
a common ideal and a common destiny,’ and
yearns to see the self-governing portions of it
know each other better; yet the mere thought of
a unified Australia, shorn of the idiotic jealousies
and differences created by State Frights, almost
causes the paper to break down and sob.
That most disastrous person, N.S.W.
Attorney-General Holman, is still busy remitting
various fines imposed on union secretaries for
breaches of the Electoral Act. The remissions
themselves, perhaps, don’t amount to very much ;
the worst trouble lies in the remarks that Holman
has made about them. When the amazing blun-
derer described as “well-meaning enthusiasm’'
the action of a supporter who had incited a woman
to commit forgery in order to get certain names
on the roll, he convinced people that he was pre-
pared to look very leniently upon roll-stuffing—
at least when it was done in the interests of his
own crowd—and that political corruption was
going to have an easy, rosy time. Also, he pro-
vided a notable handle for the enemy, and inci-
dentally added a new enrichment to the political
vocabulary. Even Wade, who is something of a
bungler himself, wasn’t so foolish as to describe
baker McKye’s offence as “ well-meaning enthu-
siasm.” It' will be a longtime before the party
fully recovers from that remark.
One Fritch, a Denver (U.S.A.) business
man, who arrived in Australia a few days ago,
thus conducts the case for the prosecution against
the trusts and monopolies that suck his country
dry :
The railway trusts have got us in the hollow of their
hands. My customers blame me for selling nails at a
dollar-fiftv, but they cost me a dollar-forty to get. We
have coal mines, too. We pay the miner a dollar a ton
to get the coal, and the railways charge a dollar-ten to
haul it, and by the time it gets to us we have to pay
three or four dollars for it. It’s the same with cante-
loupes. Some people think canteloups are wild animals,
but thev’re not—they’re just fruit. Ten cents apiece to
the grower—lo cents more to the railways—3o cents to
the consumer. Beet the same, and potatoes. Where does
the grower come in ? But we must have railways what-
ever they charge.
Luckily, Australia isn’t in the grip of the railway
octopus ; but the tentacles of other species of tire
beast are busy getting a hold, and the Denver man
comes opportunely to urge the necessity of voting
for the axe when the monopoly referendum arrives
next April.
Melbourne Argus rebukes Lloyd-George
for an attack on the House of Lords, and runs on:
The hereditary principle, so easy to ridicule by theories
of pure democraev—vet scientifically not quite indefen-
sible—was the subject of the Chancellor’s special assault.
So, hooray ! The Peers are saved ! Legislative
capacity can be handed down from father to son.
True, in this case there’s no proof of legislative
capacity in the fathers; but that is a side issue.
Contrasting the Australian Senate as a democratic
institution with the House of Lords, the Akgus
continues : “If ever a Chamber has disappointed
the expectation of Constitution builders, it is the
Senate of the Commonwealth. . . . No other
second Chamber has ever been such a failure.”
To demonstrate this beyond cavil, the daily Tory
points out that the Senate has passed practically
the whole batch of measures sent up by the House
of Reps. In short, it has obeyed the orders and
realised the expectations of the electors, whereas
it is notoriously the duty of second Chambers to
kick the people’s wishes downstairs.
The anger of the true-blue “Liberals” of
N.S.W. who are cursing David Storey and his'
little “ cave ” is all the more delightful when it is
remembered that those who are most angry are
the people who have always been most horrified
at the “machine” politics of the Labor party.
Now, when David the Hatter says he’s going to
vote as he pleases and not as Wade pleases, the
Daily Telegraph and others simply howl at him,
and want to know what he means by being an
“ intriguing middleman ” and other kinds of dis-
graceful character.
Testimonial to the land tax given by
Rear-Admiral Bridges, just back from Europe :
Then there is this drastic land tax. There is no ques-
tion that it is to a very large extent responsible for the
feeling in England on the part of those with land in-
terests here, that the best thing they can do is to get
out. They are disgusted.
Nothing better could be said of any land law than
that it disgusts the absentee landlord —the curse
of every country. For the employer, the
captain of industry, the man who joins his
brains to his workmen’s muscle and skill,
much may be said. For the absentee land-
lord, who puts no brains into the business
of growing wheat or wool, but merely levies toll
on those who do the labor and supply the brains,
nothing can be said except in the language of
curse and execration. That remark of Bridges
should be worth more than a trifle to the immi-
gration agent in England—and Ireland especially.
Melbourne Argus, on ibe New Era in
Federal legislation, bemoans the fact that—
The old path has been deserted, and the Common-
wealth has set but on a new road, altogether strange and
unknown, without even the experience of other nations
to act as a guide over some of the most doubtful parts
of it.
In the eyes of the Tory nothing can be so deplor-
able, or even damnable, in government as the
exploiting of new ideas. There should be no new
departures in legislation, no venturing from the
old path, no advance beyond the limits of prece-
dent—-and, therefore, no progress. What has
never been should never be, world without
end, amen. Novelties in commercial enter-
prise, inventions in labor-saving mechanics,
and experiments in practical science are
admirable from the bluest-Tory point of view;
but novelties in statesmanship are pardonable
only when they go to strengthen the rule of Capi-
tal and put Labor in its proper place. In short,
Governments were designed by Providence to bp
the slaves of Experience. When they break the
chains of precedent and reach out todosomething
previously unattempted they are rebels, traitors
to the cause of stagnation, and enemies of estab-
lished fact. The Conservative press can’t order
the offenders to the scaffold or burn them at the
stake ; but it shakes its palsied head at their high
treason, and hopes devoutly for the worst.
A LOADED REPLY.
Lord Chelmsford in an address to the teachers in con-
ference advised them to cultivate a hobby.— News Item.
INSPECTOR: “ And, map I ask, what's your
hobby? ”
OUTBACK TEACHER: “Sending down applications
for transfer to the city."
The country manufacturer in N. S. Wales
strikes a snag in the railways, whose freight-
charges are designed to boost Sydney and further
bloat the already over-bloated city. Thus the
freight on brewers’ raw material from Sydney to
Wellington is—malt, £1 3s. sd. per ton; sugar,
15s. per ton ; hops, £3 12s. Bd. per ton ; while the
freight on the same raw materials in the shape of
beer from Sydney to Wellington is £1 Bs. sd. per
ton. In general it costs about £2 per ton to take
ii truckload of manufactured machinery from
Sydney to (say) Temora, while it runs to about £4
per ton to take the raw material the same way.
Against this sort of thing the country manufac-
turer kicks for a while; then he either goes down
and out, or saves the remnants of his business by
shifting to the city, and making the State look
more like a tadpole than ever.
GIVING SATISFACTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Though the Victorian Parliament has not this year
done anything particularly stimulating in legislative
achievement, the session is closing much more auspici-
ously for the Ministerial party than it began.—Argus
(2,1/ 12/’lO ).
We have not toiled like anything
Nor rushed around like scalded ants,
Nor strained, nor heaved, nor tried to bring
The energy of elephants
Upon our daily job. But there !
Why should we work? God will provide,
So long as things are As They Were
And good old Fat is satisfied !
We have not worked like blazes, nor
Moiled as the busy bee doth moil ;
We have not killed one single bore,
Nor boiled the Upper House in oil;
We have not plugged along the track
Which leads to better days for those
Who are not Fat; but. . . forth and back
The House-cat leaps on anxious toes.
Still Fat is fed ; still we remain
His only—body, soul and boots.
From graft we've managed to refrain,
Despite Victoria’s heartfelt hoots ;
Fat loves to see our sort of drone
Work hard at working points, and play
At toil, and thus awhile postpone
The work that must be done—some day.
Still Fat is satisfied with us,
Although we’ve vowed, each day, “I will
Not work ! ” —for best we please him thus ;
The sweated soul, rebellions, still
Gives Fat each day his daily bread :
Still Stout retains his dirty pride. ..
Who wants to try and push ahead
With Stout-and-selfish satisfied ?
\ ICTOIIIA.
