SPORTING
NOTIONS
At the Sydney Stadium gate receipts are
swelling like the head of Clabby, welter-weight boxer
from America, and hippo-weight air-merchant from
VVindville. Which statement is satisfactorily proved by
the proceedings on Dec. 28, when Tim Land, of Australia,
met Johnny Thompson, of Yankeeland, at catch weights.
Considerable breath and ink have been expended, not
to say wasted, in admiration of the scrap. This
paper does not propose to add to the heap,
principally because it reckons the fight for the most part
wasn’t worth a cocoanut, unless the cocoanut was used
to express disapproval. It (the fight, not the cocoanut)
went 19J rounds, and about three-quarters of them were
given over to the American wrestle, which is the chief
asset of the man who relies entirely on abnormal
powers of endurance for victory. Thompson learned
in about one round that his cake was dough
in a boxing count-up, and that a knock-dut
was the single and solitary hope he had. So
he wrestled, and bored in, and poked hard with short
jabs to the body, and leaned on Land with all his might
and main and every other oz. of avoirdupois he had avail-
able. The local man, handicapped by a permanently stiff
leg, was unable to side-step as he should, and wag con-
tinually cornered and loaded up with imported
American. And it was impossible to punch the
American off. The punishment was severe enough
to stop a mule, but Thompson took it.,as the
front door of the suburban residence takes the banging
of the rent collector when the family is pretending to be
out. As the rounds went on these tactics wore Land
down and weakened him till he couldn’t do further
damage. Then Thompson came into his own, and started
to batter out the used-up enemy. In the 19th round
Land went to the floor heavily ; but the gong gave him
another chance. It was clear, however, that only a
miracle, or hanging on to the American in the approved
American manner, would carry him through the next,
and as there didn’t happen to be any miracles about, and
as the American methods haven’t come into fashion in
this sporting country, Land was outed half-way through,
after a brilliant showing against an accumulation of odds.
The most exciting feature of the battle was
Clabby, who was acting as second to his countryman
Thompson. This young man, like the rest of the.
American string, has been allowed so much lati-
tude by a management with a lately-acquired ver-
micelli backbone that he hated to see a referee
handling the light when he (Clabby) could handle
it so much better by himself. When Land was knocked
down in the 19th round the gong clanged before the fatal
“out” was counted. In any case, Land was rising in
time to save himself, but Clabby reckoned, apparently,
that Referee Baker should disregard the rules and award
the fight to Thompson on the ground that it was getting
late, or it might rain or something. His attitude in
Thompson’s corner was such tha/t he should have been
ordered out by Baker, and too m uch abuse cannot be
hurled at the management for permitting its controlling
officer, who had been through an exceptionally heavy trial
all night, to be insulted by a ring-side pug. As a matter
of fact, the position became so intolerable, and so insult-
ing was Clabby immediately after the khock-out, that
Baker was forced to crack him hard on the jaw.
* * * *
The present crowd of visiting boxers are not
likely to improve the already damaged opinion of the
Yankee as a sportsman. Papke may be well within his
rights in taking precautions to prevent his real weight
leaking out; but what was- his object in keeping his
weight secret? Obviously he has something to conceal,
and in this game concealment is prima facie evidence of
humbug at the very least. Papke professes to be wildly
anxious for another match with David Smith. Now is
Smith’s opportunity. Let. Papke put his alleged cham-
pionship on the table and let the match be for that, if
he can make the weight at the ringside. Some of Papke’s
friends say there isn’t enough money in Australia to give
Papke a decent purse for a fight for the championship.
But the real trouble is that there is too much meat about
Mr. Papke to allow him to fight for that blessed cham-
pionship.
* * * *
After a long course of raw bash served up at the
Stadium (Sydney) without trimmings, the batter supplied
on New Year’s Night by Mehegan (Vic.) and Bronson
(America) came as a light lunch to over-taxed digestions.
These two met to contest the Lightweight Championship
of Australia; both weighed under lOst. and were reason-
ably matched otherwise. The ensuing battle was the
most interesting, clean and clever that has been seen
at Rushcutter’s Bay this season. In-fighting and wrestle-
walloping were entirely eliminated, and the pair fought
out a manly, scientific 20-round bout, and were repeatedly
cheered for their fair, even courteous, behavior towards
each other. It was a night of goodwill. Even “ Snowy ”
Baker (referee) and Clabby shook hands. Both Mehegan
and Bronson are skilled boxers, although their styles are
very different : but Mehegan is the superior. He has a
most infernal left, and on Monday night he could
have hired it out as a piston rod to a ferry
steamer. His weakness lay in not moving in
quickly to swat the occasionally demoralised foe.'' A
less exasperatingly deliberate man would have won on a
knock-out half-way through the argument, instead of
winning on points at the end of 20 rounds. Bron Son
hasn’t enough poundage behind his deliveries ; also he is
far too amiable a fighter, refusing tb take advantages
that have been legitimately’ earned. There was no doubt
whatever as to the winner, and Mehegan looks likely' to
be the caretaker of the Australian light-weight champion-
shin for some time to come.
r » * * *
Brisbane had its first view of a big fight on
Boxing Day, when Maorilander Devitt and Clabby’, the
Yankee, met at the Edward»street Stadium to argue the
welterweight championship, and it reckons it saw nothing
to blow about. The affair lasted two minutes. After
being punched four or five times, Devitt fell, and while
the referee was counting off the seconds,'the crowd in-
vited him to get up. He obliged them when the count
had reached nine, and made a wild rush at Clabby ; but
got a short right punch on the chin that outed him.
Then the crowd swore and went to the races^
The Ma State’s cricket victory in Melbourne
didn’t come as a startling surprise. Admittedly the
Cornstalks were a superior lot, while the Vies, were
several degrees below normal. Bardsley played the best
and easiest cricket for his 124. Trumper’s 142 was more
desultory. Barbour wielded a very attractive bat for his
82. The quaintest batsman of the. 22 was McElhone,
whose nervous poking provoked ironic cheers. But he
walloped with greater ardor in his second strike, and
fielded without a fault. The greatest fielding, though,
was that of Matthews, who did two men’s work —and he’s
only a little chap, too. And, on top of that, he smacked
up 39 and 50 not out, besides dismissing three ot the
enemy for 89. After that they simply hid to push some
old fellow out to let him into the Test eleven.
V'* * *
B. Kortlang, who batted so well for his side in
the N.S. VV.-Vic. match, making top score in the first and
third highest in the second innings, threatens to arrive
at last. Though still a comparative youngster, Kortlang
has had a large experience at the game. First, he played
for a church team at Prahran (Vic.), his birthplace ; then
he wandered away and taught the Yankee how to bat,
while playing for clubs in ’Frisco, N’Yark, Philadelphia,
Baltimore, Bawst’n ; also, he was picked as America’s
wicket-keeper against an English team in ’O7. S’Africans
Schwarz and Snooke also had a hand in that game. After
.that Kortlang skipped across and played in Canada;
next he qualified for Surrey ; then he went to China. In
’OS he was back in Australia and played with Balmain
(N.S.YV.); and in’Q9 he was in Vic. once more, keeping
his end -up for Essendon. Yet, barring the American
episode, he was never picked to play for his State or
country till ’O9 ; but since then his big cricket average
reads: 23 innings, nine n. 0., 197 highest score, 1434 runs,
102'4 average.
* * * *
Australia has never lost a Test match in Ade-
laide since the season 1891-2, when Grace’s XI, after
being beaten in Melbourne and Sydney, batted for two
days on a hard wicket, and then, after a wet Sunday, ran
through Australia twice on the Monday. There were
only three Tests to a season in those days.
* * •* *
Tasmania called in at Sydney the other day
and asked the Ma State if it knew anything about
cricket. The Ma. State did, and tossed. But Tasmania
won the spin ; and, looking at the weather, its skipper
sent theMa State to the wickets, where N.S.W. promptly
knocked up 406. It was a -fearsome score —the
sort that leaves a helpless feeling behind it. And
Tassy had the feeling badly, for when its turn at the
wickets came the weather had got worse, the light
was bad, and the outlook was hopeless. The best
the Fiyspeck team could do was pile up 49 for the whole
side, no less than four vanishing under the sign of the
waterfowl. This left the Island State team 357 behind on
the first innings, so the crowd following on put on another
244, which left it still 113 short on the first innings.
N.S.W. thus won by that number of runs and a whole
innings. It was a bad beating.
* * # *
The second Test between Australia and South
Africa commenced in Melbourne last week. Hill, win-
ning the toss, sent Trumper and Bardsley in to score.
And they did, but not so sensationally as they are
wont to do these times. Trumper’s wicket fell
for 34, when the combination had yielded 59. Hill
took the vacant crease, and by lunch they had
lifted the Australian score to 144. After lunch
Llewellyn smashed Hill with a neat thing that was head-
ing for anywhere but the wicket, but which, touching
the ground, curled round the batsman’s legs right on to
the middle stump. It was a Bobbydazzler. Gehrs came
and went, leaving four on the board ; and the score dropped
or soared, as one looks at it, from 1 for 144,t0 four for 164.
Five fell for 188, Armstrong added 75, and after that
the end came quickly, the side vanishing for 348. Africa
went to the,sticks right aw&y, and at close of play had
bashed up 17 without losing any timber. On Monday
Australia learned that Africa is the home of most
new things. The Springboks changed their methods
and played ancient Henry with the Australians
in a way that made Hill weep tears of blood.
Faulkner carried his bat for 188, and the score
stood at five for 352 when the day gave out. On
Tuesday the Australians were to receive a greater shock.
As this issue was going to press Faulkner had lifted his
score to 204 and departed, and tlij? innings was over for
506. As this issue went to press, Australia had lost one
wicket in the second innings for-35. And, worse luck,
that wicket was Bardsley’s.
* * * *
N.S.W., Vic., and South Aus. Cricket Asso-
ciations having agreed, three months’ residence will in
future be necessary to qualify- players for Sheffield Shield
cricket. The innovation is a huge brick aimed at the
practice of pushing in English and other players who may
happen along, and biffing out Australians to make room
for them. The new law is good from all points of view.
**, * *
T. S. Warne, the Carlton (Vic.) professional
cricketer, will have a “ benefit” in Melbourne on March
10, when the Australian XI. v. the Rest of Australia will
strive to rake in a decent purse. Why “testimonial”
matches are introduced into Australia, where players in
“test’’and other matches receive good remuneration,
isn’t apparent; and the various Cricket Associations and
the Board of Control should keep a weather-eye on the
expansion of this nefarious practice.
*- * * *
Queensland’s cricket success against N.S.W. at
Brisbane and Sydney, and against Victoria in Melbourne,
heaves another clod at the door of the Board of Control
in connection with its refusal to let the Northern State
join in the Sheffield Shield struggle. South Aus. basin
recent years been the loud-mouthed objector against
Bananaland’s inclusion on the score of distance ; but the
hammering at the door is making a big npise. However,
as the'other State's have no objection, the Board, by a
majority decision, will possibly decide to admit the
younger brother.
* * * *
Sydney cricketer B. Folkard, who was under-
stood to have been dropped for weariness in the field,
made a fearsome splash at Yass on Monday last. Playing
with a Sydney team he smacked up 100 of a total score
of 392, and then battered down seven Yass wickets for
eight runs. The whole side went for 14. It was a
debacle.
* * * *
These are the days of remarkable cricket per-
formances. At Wellington (N.S.W.), last week, a team
from Orange was dismissed for three runs, one of which
was scored by that old and reliable player, Sundries.
*But at Christchurch team and Sundries
combined to make a solitary run. Which was a record
breaker and a world beater.
* , * * *
If Tasmania cannot play cricket it flatters it-
self it knows something about bowls. The historic and
ancient pastime is the game for ancient Tasmania. So
when the Victorians called in last week they were promptly
seized, taken to the bowling green and had their noses
rubbed in the turf, the margin being five points in three
games. The joy in Tasmania threatens to wake up that
Garden of Sleep.
* * * *
Once again the rumor that the Sydney Thou-
sand will be revived in March next is causing thrills
through the souls of those who follow the wheel. Well,
in spite of all the tricks and swindles that were worked,
we had a great time in Sydney when the Thousand was
booming. What nights they were ! And who can ever
forget the sight when the riders for the Five Miles came
wandering round the track, 30 or 40 of them, running
lightly into place for the start. Yes, most of us would
be glad to see another Sydney Thousand meeting. And,
probably after the long rest the game has had, it would pay
if it were made anything like the spectacular affair of the
davs a-rear. The League of Wheelmen professes to know
nothing of any revival, and the crowd that ran it before
speaks likewise. Every now and again, with tlie wish the
father of the thought, there starts again the rumor that
the world’s greatest bike race will be revived. But the
rumor .never comes to anything, and the dim, mysterious
people who are said to be working it updn the shadow of
anonymity never arrive at the place where the light
shines, and toss over the cash which will make the thing
go. Probably this rumor is the same as those which have
gone before.

“Quill Chauffeur”: Re motoring records at-
tacked (D. 29/12/10) by “Hermes” with a pen dipped

an attempt to discount the praiseworthy modesty
of White (who succeeded) and Camphin (who
didn’t), both of whom reckoned it- was good
enough to talk after, instead of before, the event.

accompanied him on all his record runs, and Whitburn, a

Kellow, whose performance has been many times
eclipsed? Writer reckons that White, an independent
crank, who was his own financier and used his own old
touring car, is the man to whom the alleged glory pro-
perly belongs, seeing that he has won through at the first
try every time. Camphin, a struggling trader, deserves
credit for his pluck and persistence in the face of a bent
front axle and broken spring ; and all the alleged railway
gatekeepers in the Ma State cannot detract from the fact
that he had reached Wallan—26 miles from his goal—with
over an hour to spare, when his petrol tank went wrong*
* * * *
Racing—at Sydney, anyhow—seems to have
degenerated into a mere money-making pastime for
gentlemen of the ring. The Tattersall’s Club meeting at
Randwick was the same disastrous circumstance to
backers as all the other races have been of late. The
horses that carried public money to the front carried it
at such ridiculous rates that all trace of equity dis-
appeared from the bet. But in most cases the horses
that got home started at about eight to one ; and when
the bookmaking fraternity bet anything near eight to one
against winning nags, it is sure evidence that nobody,
save perhaps an owner or tw-o, is on those nags. Sydney
horse-racing courses will be good places to keep away
from till the bookmaker has been abolished in favor of the
decent and automatically-correct totalisator.
BILLY PAPKE,
who mislaid
his world's
champion-

the other

Hurdle Race, Farrell, the second horse, and Pharos, who
fell, went out at two to one and five to two against, re-

against; which is a fair indication that the public vvas
not on Handspike. Poi Dance’s win of the Flying
Welter was a genuine case of a favorite get-
ting home, but the best price going was six
to four against him; which was a mighty liberal
price for the bookmaking fraternity. New Year’s Gift
was one for the bookmakers. Of the 10 starters the
public practically pinned its faith to the horse that ran
third—Leuron, the five-year-old, by True Blue from
Early Bird. The rankest outsider on the list wasNangar.
Nobody Avanted him. Not a soul would have him. So
Patrick Shortodds howled l 2 to one against Nangar
in vain. He proved a skinner. For Tatt.’s Club Cup,
a fairly heavily-gilded affair, with £lOOO added money,
15 faced the starter, and Byplay and Jinnee carried the

won, and thus provided the only instance in which the

with a wild, triumphant screech to inform the world that

i » * * *
S.A. racecourses put £453,000 through the
totalisator in 1910, £42,000 more than the year before,
which was a “ record ”so far. There is no safer proof of
general prosperity in the State. The largest sum at one
meeting was £55,737, for the three daj’s of the Birthday
Cup. The largest sum in one day was the £26,710 put
through at Onkaparinga. The various racing clubs re-
ceived £34,000 as commission, and the State Government
took £11,300, waggishly called “ stamp duty.”
* * * *
Died at Wellington (N.S.W.), Saturday morn-
ing, T. S. Clibborn, full of years and honors. He had
reached the ripe age of 73, and for at least 40 years had
enjoyed a really good time. He played "his part, he
drank his wine, and he took the benefit of his life.
And when he retired from office, the A.J.C., of
which for nearly half a century he had been the
outward and visible sign, granted him an annuity, and
changed the name of one of its races to the Clibborn
Stakes. What more could a racing secretary desire ? His
body was buried in the family sepulchre at South Head
Cemetery. A curious coincidence was that he was the
last of tlie first trustees of Rushcutter’s Bay Park. Re-
cently the Trust was dissolved, and the park taken over
by the City Council, and on the day when the Trust
ceased to exist in law Mr. Clibborn died.
* * * *
After a week of battling on Sydney courts,
N.S. W. Lawn Tennis country championships sorted them-
selves out into a win for Artnidale. The final was be-
tween the winner and Dubbo, and Armidale overwhelmed
Dubbo by seven sets 46 games to one set 21 games. Then
the countrymen became presumptuous enough to try a
racquet with a metropolitan team. The walloping was
bad, the metropolis scoring six sets 38 games to 22 gaines
only. The week did not reveal any players wasting
sweet quality on the country air. In fact, unless the
country player bucks up somewhat, the fixture will have
to be ab andoned.
This lack of new and rising players is the canker
at the root of the game in Australia. In the United
States it Is different. There the new players are rising
so fast it is difficult to hold them down. Beales Wright,
who will be remembered in Australia, and who was atone
time the tennis champion of the whole Federation, re-
cently found himself beaten by a quite young and unknown
player, E. M. McLoughlin. Other great scalps fell to the
Me' man ; but a few days later Wright regained his
superiority, and just managed to beat McLoughlin.
Then, in his second round, a 17-year-old lad made Wright
play four sets to win ; and, in the fourth, Reuben A.
Holden, jun., had him all out. T. W. Hend-
rick was extended for five sets, and had all
his work cut out in five sets to beat a 15-
year-old boy. These are exceptional cases, perhaps ; but
everywhere the same tendency is noticeable. The young
men are coming on faster than the old ones are decaying.
Here in Australia we have Brookes and Wilding, who really
belong to Maoriland. But we have no one coming along
who shows the slightest sign of being able to defend the
Davis Cap when Brookes and Wilding have gone the way
of all champions.
**■*+■
The death of R. F. Doherty is not encouraging
to strenuous lords of the tennis court. R. F. Doherty
and his brother were as pre-eminent in their time—from
1897 to 1907—as the Renshaws had been in the 80’s and
early 90’s of last century. One or both of the Renshaws
died long ago. Tennis is a great game to play—in
moderation. As an all-round test of skill, activity,
alertness, resourcefulness, temper and dogged pluck in
the performer it far surpasses cricket or baseball; but
for the man who practises it as a profession, rather than a
pastime, it is seemingly the most killing of athletic
sports.
* « * *
Evidently the Ma State has no distance ped
capable of keeping time with J. Fitzgerald, the Cana-
dian. The five miles’ professional championship at
Sydney Sports Ground last week was the softest of good
things for the visitor ; he got so far ahead that Hopping
threw in the towel, and the Canadian finished alone in
27min. 37sec. This week he rubbed home the truth of
the foregoing by annexing the mile championship of
Australia in 4min. 25sec. He was 220yds. to the good
from H. McCabe and Hopping.
* * * *
Australian sprinter Jack Donaldson doubtless
made Reg Walker sorry he threw over his amateur
status when the South African was defeated last week
for the World’s Professional Championship at Johannes-
burg. The time is alleged to be 9fsec. —an effort which
left little time for Walker to think on the way.
# * * *
Quite possible that Australia may welcome in
A. Stanbury another candidate for the sculling cham-
pionship. He is a younger brother of the once mighty
“ Jim,” with plenty of beef and strength. Although at
the recent N.S.W. championship regatta he didn’t show
to much advantage in the lumpy sea, his supporters are
very sanguine about his rowing ability. J. R. towns
won the title from A. Priddle, with A. Stanbury and ;A.
Green in the rear ; and a small piece of record i that all
competitors claim relationship with ex-champion scullers.
Detailed accounts of the Webb-Pearce sculling
match show that Webb put up a much better struggle
than the earlier cables suggested. We were given to
understand that Pearce led from start to jump for the
finishing line, whereas at one time Webh was actually ni
front and going like a rocket ; but Pearce passed him,
and after that held him with comparative ease. Webb
owned up after the race that he is not w'hat he used to
be. It is the doom of man that his champion-
ship days are few; and Webb’s are gone for ever.
He must now repose in the reflected light of
his past glory. Meantime an agitation for a meeting
between Pearce and Arnst has broken out, and in the
course of the barracking some tall things are being said
in the endeavor to prove that the question of whether
Arnst or Pearce is the better man is surrounded by an
atmosphere of grave doubt —doubt that harrows the
public heart and sears its brain centres, keeping it awake
o’ nights—doubt that can be removed only by a struggle
on the Parramatta. The struggle will apparently even-
tuate, as Arnst is willing to meet Pearce, and even row
him.
* * * *
The gods who control the Ma State’s swimming
legions might do worse than look round for someone to
help Cecil Healy uphold the Ma end of the log at the big
carnival at the Municipal Baths in Woolloomooloo Bay
next month. Healy is swimming better than ever before,
but there are fears that by the time he has to meet Beau-
repaire he will have gone stale. Some private trials in
the harbor baths of late have revealed that young Frank
Fittes, who some years ago threatened to do things, is
now at the height of his powers. Although not in proper
training, he is swimming excellently, and showing phe-
nomenal speed, and, as he has been residentin N.S.W. for
nearly two years, the Ma State’s swimming crowd might
find it worth while to take an interest in him.
There is a story around Tamworth that Bill
Squires has recovered his punch. In a disturbance there
recently six coves, blokes and cobbers attacked him, aqd
were smashed up in short order but with great thorough-
ness. Only one escaped by instantaneous flight, and
after finishing off the others Squires is said to have
started in pursuit in a way that proves he is anything
but decadent physically. Whether the fugitive was over-
taken has not transpired ; but as Bill is still at large it
means probably that the fugitive got clear. William was
fined a bob each— with apologies from the Bench, whose
sympathies were plainly with thejiuneher.
The bounding idiot who trespasses on the track
has been at it again. This time at Gilgai, five miles from
Inverell (N.S.W.), Mr. W. W. Pomroy wasdoing a prelimi-
nary round the course before starting in a race, and the
usual pedestrian—this time two of him—crossed the
track. To avoid running them down Mr. Pomroy ran off
the track, collided with a stump, and leaves a widow and
two young children. Pomroy practically threw his own
life away to avoid hurting these two people. And in this
he was wrong. Blitherers who get on the track should
be ridden down ; if that cannot be conveniently done,
they should be shot.

Things are looking up in the Pacific. The
British Lion—presumably#; after deep considera-
tion—has come to the conclusion that he can spare
just two joints of his long naval tail for the China
station. So the elderly battleships Triumph and
Svviftsure are shortly to amble out and grin tooth-
lessly across the narrow .sells at Nippon’s modern
floating forts. It is rather like putting two
wizened little grandfathers on outpost duty; but
it accords well enough with the present warlike
spirit of Bull’s Empire. The National Conference '
on Sea Training, convened by the Navy League,
which sat—-or reclined—recently in London with
the avowed object of preventing the gradual dis-
appearance of British seamen from British ships
adopted a motion urging that boys attending ele-
mentary schools be trained in seamanship ai d
navigation. The blessed conference said nothing
about kicking Ram Chunder out of the fo’castfe
or, in lieu thereof, adding a course in marine
slave-driving to tl|e curriculum of the green young
sea pups. It just passed the job and the responsi-
bility on to the unfortunate kid in a large, loose
fashion, arid gave three cheerp for Nelson.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“C.K.Y.” : Have just had my second and final
div. as a creditor of the gone-bung Farmers and Settlers’
Co-operative co. As a shareholder I rake in nix. I was
induced to take shares on the financial repute (see S.M.H.
and D.T.) of William Mcßillion, chairman. lam satisfied
now about “ publicity ” being a sufficient safeguard. This
thing was reported solvent; three months later it ex-
ploded with a horrible perfume. Wrote to a director—
the M.L.A. for Waverley—to know what had been done
with my dollars. Reply : The matter is in the official
assignee’s hands. Waited to read the report of the
evidence of the Heaven-born financiers; but, for some
reason I should like explained, it was heard with closed
doors. And we are still waiting to hear where our money
went. Also we have strong doubts about Heaven-born
financiers.
* * * *
The S. Aus. Supreme Court, after thinking hard
for over two years, has decided that niallee is not timber.
Chief Justice Higinbotham, of Victoria, said that a.l
trees, whether alive or dead, are timber; but S. Aus.
decided to accept the English definition: “Wood usid
for building for human habitation, not as a temporary
makeshift, but as suitable material in the place where ii
grew.”
it -k * *
“ Klifton ” : Messrs. Hooke, Spunge and Robbe
are rival drapers, carrying on business in three small
shops which adjoin. The relations existing among
these three commercial magnates are not happy, and
each loses no opportunity to outwit the other. The
other day, to his disgust, Spunge found Rooke’s windows
plastered with—“ Colossal Salvage Sale! Wonderful
Bargains !! ’’ And on Robbe’s windows—" Realisation of
Bankrupt Stock ! Must Be Disposed of. No Reasonable
Offer. Refused !!! ” Then Spunge had an inspiration,
and soon the tide of customers which had been flowing
into his rivals’shops turned into his own establishment.
For his place is in the middle, and over the top of his
door Spunge had erected a huge placard, which read:
“ Main Entrance."
* * * *
“Rodd Rowan”: The members of the Mel-
bourne brick combine are supposed to be making money.
Quite a mistake ! The manager of one of the cos., who
is also a large shareholder, says so himself. “ There is
very little profit in brick-making,” he told one of his
foremen. “ No,” said the other, “ there can’t be much in
it at present. Four or five years ago, when we were in
the poor ol'ay on the surface, you were making a profit.
There was 12J per cent, of broken bricks then. Now
there is only 3 per cent, of losses that way. We are
turning out the same quantity of bricks now with 16
less men at work—l 6 men’s wages saved. And there has
been an increase of nearly 15 per cent, in the price (38s.
instead of 30s. per thousand). So, as you say, there
can’t be much profit.” , The manager thought hard,
grinned, and walked off. 1 There’s another thing besides.
Lots of builders accept the figures on the load-tickets or
cartnotes. One recent week the contractors decided to
check the numbers. One alleged 500 load was 180 bricks
short. None of the others was so bad, but the total
shortage for the'day was 500 bricks. If the contractors
hadn’t noticed they would have paid 19s. for bricks they
didn’t get. And if ISO bricks can evaporate off one dray-
load, think of the frightful loss there must be that way
in a whole brick-yard. I hope, after this, you won’t say
anything unkind about brick cos.
* * * *
“Jape : There are no fine points about a
sausage. It is neither fish, fowl nor good red herring.
Swine, and the husks that the swine did eat, about fill
the bill, though Chicago specimens may also contain
Prodigal Son. And yet, on Saturday nights, for the un-
sung, unhonored sausage strong women have fought.
Bah! have we, I ask, no steak in the country? The
meanest rich womata I know buys sausages; she buys
half a pound from me, and half a pound from the Com-
monwealth Meat Emporium, and as seven problems go to
the pound, she makes one problem on the deal, for pro-
fessional etiquette forbids dissection. 1 mention this to
show that it isn’t all love of sausage that spurs the
sausage-buyer. It is sometimes love of getting a bargain
—one sausage to the good. The good? Ha, ha! I
make the sausages. And I hate that woman.
* * * *
“Jewel-Block”: I guess the 50,000-tons-capa-
city credited by cable to the projected Cunard leviathan
stands for load-displacement. But even that would mean
about 40,000 tons gross, as against the Mauretania’s 32,000
tons. The new colossus, accommodating nearly 4000
passengers, will be just over one-sixth of a mile long, by 31
yards in beam—a positively appalling mass to come hurt-
ling along at nearly half a mile a minute. By the way,
there’s a flutter left yet in the despised wind-jammer.
The recently-ScottiSh four-masted barque Strathgryfe
—now German in name and flag—has just footed it from
the Land’s End to Melbourne in 73 days, doing the 5600
miles from the Cape in the remarkable time of three
weeks. I suppose the heart has gone out of the “ Main-
sail haul ” business nowadays ; but, though the 62-day
run of the beautiful little Thermopylae has never been
equalled, trips of 70 odd days used to be not uncommon.
In the old Theophane we made 74 days of it, one voyage ;
and the skipper swore a mighty swear that, next trip,
he’d down that or die. Well, he did die, and his next
voyage was. presumably, on the Jasper Sea.
‘ * * * *
“ Melb ” : Don’t suppose that the main support
of the sumptuous Pekin Caf6, and more sumptuous Can*
ton Caf6, is Chinky. To be sure there is Shark Fin, at a
half-guinea, for the Chink millionaire ; but these places
would starve on Chinamen. The real backing is White
Trash, as with the laundries. A restaurateur, near the
Pekin Cafe, writes up “No Asiatics Served.” The Chinky
will say, “We don’t care about ’em either.” The Canton
Caf6 is a long way off Melb.’s Chinatown. It goes in to
undercut the Spaniards and Greeks. White cookery is
the staple. Collins-street and Flinders-lane roll up.
Apparently this is the only Chinaman in Melbourne with
a liquor license. L. L. Tack by name. We lose one L.L.
and gain another. When White men work for Chinese
gardeners or banana merchants they are declasse. Does
anything of the kind apply to swells who go to Chinks
for their tucker ?
THE WORLD AND THE FLESH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. J. T. Huston, addressing the Food Reform
League, a vegetarian body, at Melbourne the other night,
said he had grave doubts about the story of Adam and
Eve and the apple. “X am convinced it was not an apple
Eve ate, but a mutton chop,” said the rev. gent.
Brother Huston recommends oatmeal and raw food as
conducive to saintliness.
Had Eve eaten only apples,
As we read in Bible tales,
All our taverns would be chapels,
And we’d have no need of gaols.
But she ate a chop, and quickly
Came the fall of man to pass.
Sin and trouble followed thickly.
It is writ: “ All flesh is crass.”
On a vegetable diet,
So the cabbage-eaters say,
E’en the Devil would be quiet,
And old Nero bend and pray ;
For the wicked inspiration
That our fellow sinner cops
Is not due to vegetation,
But to devilled bones and chops.
In your wheat there may be weevils,
Blight in ’taters, old and new ;
But the soul-destroying evils
That disport in Irish stew
By the mutton are created.
Sinful thoughts and actions rash
Are with meat associated —
Vide “ sausages and mash.”
So repent, and let them put yer
On an oatmeal diet now ;
For the Devil is a butcher,
And his leading fiend is Cow.
Brother H. no worldly wag is,
And he preaches with a will,
If it wasn’t for the haggis
Scotland would be Eden still!
VICTORIA.
SILAS SMELL.


BEALE HIGH-GRADE PIANOS
Some reasons why Fourteen Thousand
are in use in Australian Homes:
Not mere cheapness—there is nothing' cheap about them, and it has
not been our aim.
Every part, every detail, is of the highest quality, and the sKilled
labor is the highest paid in the world.
The wrest-pins are all turned and nicKeled, which is found in no
other piano.
The bass strings are hexagonal, costing about double the price o/
ordinary wire, also a unique characteristic of Beale Pianos.
Tbe Iron Frames are mathematically accurate, and the scale is
calculated to a greater degree of musical perfection than any other,
because the tuning-system allows of a higher strain than does any other.
No climate has any effect upon the tonal quality.
The choicest timbers are available to us, not procurable in £urope.
We use Californian Redwood, the ideal for resonance. From our own
Queensland forests we have superb pines up to 7 feet in diameter, free
from Knot or flaw.

OFFICES & AGENTS AT ALL PORTS


Captain C. S. Cartwright, who pilots the
Commonwealth’s Endeavor in its efforts to put
salt on the tail of Australia’s missing fish supply,
has had his thrills. He was wrecked years back
on Tristan D’Acunha, the speck on the face of the
Atlantic which was once garrisoned by the
British to keep an eye on the back door when
Napoleon was on St. Helena. Just how the eye
was going to be kept on at the distance was never
explained. Anyhow, Nap. made no attempt to
escape by swimming in that direction. When
the garrison was withdrawn, a small community
decided to remain, and was remaining vigorously
when Cartwright scrambled ashore. He was so
enamored of the simple life that he stayed five
years, and married there. Then lack of “school-
ing” for a growing family drovp him out. He
also tells a yarn of a cargo of whisky that was
cast ashore on the island. But as the whisky has
all gone, that isn’t a story for thirsty weather.
Missing: Friends.
THOMAS (TOMMY) WINTER, 24 years
of age, about 5 feet 8 inches high, strong
build, fair complexion. Last heard of August,
1908, at Woongarra, Bundaberg, Queensland.
Anyone who knows anything of him please
write to his parents.
ROBERT WINTER,
Bowning P. 0., N.S.W.

IN BANANA LAND
“ Bromide ” on Queensland pubs :
What is “Stan” talking about? Can’t he tell the
difference between a Chow and a white man? He tells
us “ there are at least seven ‘white’ hotels in Cairns”
(B 22/12/TO). It’s two months since I was in Cairns, and
there were only two then. If there are seven now, some-
one must have been whispering in the ear of Mr. Beer-
pump. He tells us, too, that “Mackay is nearly all
‘white,’” and “Townsville is rapidly falling into line.”
I’ve only just left Mackay; Townsville six days ago.
What I saw in both places forces me to say that “ Stan "
is a liar. I took a run around the Townsville pubs, and
found one “white” house in Flinders-street, and three
on Ross Island. No, I wasn’t drunk—not drunk enough
to mistake a Chow cook for a good white man, anyhow.
I know Townsville beer will turn plain atmosphere into
blue snakes and green devils ; but even it won’t make a
Chow white. As to Mackay, my inquiries showed that it
owns 24 pubs, of which 14 employ the colored curse ; and
I reckon it’s up to the white workers of the district to be
ashamed of it, for, so far as most of the pubs are con-
cerned, the matter is entirely in their hands. •
Barrister Ryan, of the Bananaland Par-
liament, gave the case away the other evening
Mr. Ryan : I desire to support your ruling, Mr. Deputy
Speaker. I think the arguments which have been
adduced bv the other side of the House, particularly by
the Treasurer, also support your contention. That is my
opinion.
The Premier : That is your professional opinion.
Mr. Ryan : No, it is not my professional opinion ; it is
my honest opinion.
Whereat the House guffawed. Yet, under the
party system, the politician should be about the
last person in the world to throw mud at Costs,
Esq., for having one opinion for private consump-
tion and another for business purposes. There
isn’t one party man who believes in everything
his party says and does; yet it is his job to
barrpek apd vote for the lot. If he approves of
99 per cent, of the party’s policy and votes “No
on the 1 per cent., then he is in danger of throw-
ing out the party and bringing in one with which
he doesn’t agree once a month. The system
and the absence of elective Ministries simply force
men to have two opinions, and to frequently use
the professional one that isn’t honest.
“ The Crocodile ” : Kidston ought to feel
proud of the wages his prosperous State pays
railway men in the Far North. In such places as
Townsville, Cairns, etc., where the cost of lijing
is very high, married men are working for 7s. a
day. Some of these men are supporting numer-
ous children, and the wives who do the house-
keeping deserve the Victoria Cross. The Phidston
party will no doubt look confidently to these rail-
way men for support at the next general elections.
And the rotten end of the business is that the
crowd will get a good deal of it too.
Railways Minister Denham, in advocating
the building of the Western Transcontinental,
dwelt on the splendid revenue-producing capabili-
ties of Queensland’s railways. Yet Denham him-
self, only four years ago, pointed out that there
was then an accumulated deficit of millions on
these lines ; and only last year the State’s railways
failed to pay their interest bill by £130,000, which
sum was made good out of consolidated revenue.
If this happens in a good year, what will be the
size of the deficit that the taxpayer will have to
carry through a drought when the system is handi-
capped by an additional 1200 miles of qraCk
running through one of the driest belts in
Australia?
PRACTICAL PETER.
HIMPERIAL HOFFICER (fiercely): “ That’s no way to hold a rifle. Do you mean to say that
you don't know the proper way?"
PETE FROM PARATOO: “ That's all right, old cock. I’ve shot more blanky rabbits than ever
you saw."
“Boggo”: This is how the Phidston
Ministry fulfils its old-time promise to cut down
the expenses of goverhment. Throughout the
Publie Service practically all the higher-paid
officials have obtained increases this year, while
the Leader of the Opposition has been paid at the
rate of £524 per annum, and the Deputy-Leader
who officiated in his absence has received the
same Salary. Also the Queensland taxpayer has
enjoyed the privilege of paying the Speaker’s
wage twice over—Armstrong, who took Bell’s
place, being paid at the same rate as the Honor-
able Joey. Of course, this is all very nice and
kind and sympathetic— but where does the tax-
payer come in ? Well might Arthur Morgan
remark from the dais of the Council: lhe
demon of extravagance is abroad in the land ! ”
Lennon, Mulcahy, Nevitt, Crawford and
Foley, of the Opposition, jettisoned their anti-
borrowing principles when Phidston s borrow,
boom and burst policy was under consideration.
Their explanation was that they had favored the
proposed railway and couldn’t consistently kick
the proposals to finance it. Once upon a time
Lennon was a bsfnk director; and if somebody
had promised him to reduce an overdraft, he
presumably would not have objected if the cus-
tomer had dynamited the bank’s safe to get the
money. Or if Mr. Foley thinks that, at next
election, his opponent should be defeated, he will
presumably see no objection to somebody putting
a bullet through the gentleman to make dead
sure of it. In short, if Messrs. Lennon, Mul-
cahy and co. agree that something should be
done, they .want Queensland to believe that they
can’t consistently object to any suggested method
of doing it, no matter how rotten they may have
previously declared that method to be.
The Church “Upon the Bust.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Archbishop of Brisbane, speaking: at the presenta-
tion of a testimonial of £lOO to one of his clergy, said .
“ However, when a gentleman went ‘ on the bust, even
if he were a clergyman, £lOO did not last for ever ; and
he had therefore much pleasure in presenting alsoia silver
box for use in the sacred offices of his ministrations.

OH ! Some are gay on guinea five, and some
are glad on ten,
But they be mostly humble folk and
common toiling men ;
And others need a twenty when they seek the
jolly bowl,
And fifty scarce suffices to put others on the ff 'oll;
And there be those whose eighty pound would
melt in just a week,
But all of these be journeymen carousers, so to
speak ;
But if you want to see a spree and view the jolly
dust,
Just keep your eye on parson men who venture
“on the bust.”
Yes, the Bishop you may trust
Be the raising of the dust,
For a hundred pound’s a trifle when the Parson’s
on the Bust!
You talk about your Foxes and your Sheridans
and such—
I own in their potations they could beat the very
Dutch;
The bullocky who boozes is a spectacle to view,
The sailor man’s a caution with his beery Liz and
Lou ;
But the Church that drops its halo for a moment
in the spree
Is a quainter exhibition to the likes of you and me>
For when the parson peeleth off his coat of cleric
rust
And getteth off his cassock in careering on the
bust,
Oh 1 The Bishop you may trust
That there’ll be a jolly dust
When the parson drops a hundred on a sacerdotal
bust.

and true;
They tear his reputation, like a rotten rag, in

And do their lurid best to bring him level with

And that is how it happens that the Parson when
astray

he Must

the Bust.”
Oh ! the Bishop you may trust
When the Tempter a gust,
Oh ! a Huiidred Pound’s a trifle to the Parson on
the Bust!
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, December 31, 1910.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The question whether Premier Kidston is to
parade at that little matter in Westminster in
June, when George V. will put on his metal head-
gear, is waxing hot. Up till now Wullie has ap-
proached the question most warily. As a matter
of fact, he would like to go; but it has to be
remembered that the House is, after all,
mostly Philp, and that Macartney and others
have been getting unmanageable of late. So
With Wullie so long absent, things might
happen. In a word, Philp might happen.
As matters stand, it looks as if Kidston s deci-
sion will be a fair indication of the approaching
political weather. If he thinks that, bolstered up
by his loan railways, he can win the 1912 election,
then he will sit tight and take no risk ; but if the
idea gets into his head that the game is up, he
will very likely go to the United Gohendom, take
a title, and then flop gently into the Agent-
General’s shoes. These will he made vacant by the
stepping out of Robinson, who is waiting with
one ear turned to the door-bell and one eye glued
on the steamer time-tables.
Did I tell you that Dr. Eleanor Bourne, in order
to take charge of the collective school-kid of this
State, has given up holding the ether sponge
while her brother medicos do the carving ? Hence-
forth sore eyes, deaf ears, delicate chests, incipi-
ent curvatures, classroom ventilation, seating
accommodation, and the inculcation of the rudi-
ments of personal hygiene and the art of first-aid
will be her special care and particular job.
Popular opinion commends the Government for a
very wise choice in its first school doctor. Eleanor
Bourne carried off the first exhibition granted to a
Queensland girl at the Sydney Uni., and followed
it up by a brilliant student career. Since screw-
ing up her plate upon the brass-bound Terrace of
Brisbane, she has been much sought after as a
child doctor, having “a'way wid her” among
small folk. >
Two engagements are calling for congratula-
tions. One instalment goes to Miss Eleanor
Hardie, eldest daughter of one of Brisbane’s most
popular medicos, and Donald Cameron, son of the
canny Scot who for so long’ represented Brisbane
North in the State Parliament. The other lot
goes to Miss N. Cory, of Toowoomba, and Mr.
Archie Hogarth, an engineer who hails from
Cloncurrv, where the copper comes from. The
latter pair announced the mutual understanding
at the local races on Monday last, and immedi-
ately the air was strewn with blessings.
Two deaths recorded lately are those of Mr. Por-
ter, a solicitor of some 30 years’ practice in these
parts, and of Arthur Martin, for many years a
well-known auctioneer, who was “ passed in ’’ on
Friday. Both men were at one time as well
known as the town clock.
The baby immigrant that recently arrived in
Brisbane as a stowaway on the steamer Levuka
was the centre of an interesting gathering at St.
Colomb’s Church, Clayfield, on Sunday last, when
the names of Jessie Philippa were given her.
Miss Philippa has, since landing without ticket or
attendant, become the charge of Mr. and Mrs.
Cruise, of Clayfield, who will bring her up in the
clan. Her first name is that of her adopted ma,
and the second an adaptation of that of the good
stewardess on the Levuka who “ did for her ” on
the voyage north.
There’s some talk among the tea-cups of a wed-
din’ that went astray in the Far North. A youth-
ful bride with a trunk full of pretties blew into
this port last winter, and after thoroughly enjoy-
ing herself for a day or so, duly set sail for the
North, where the impatient groom was awaiting
her. Then the clock stopped, or the wireless got
out of gear, or something, for no one heard an-
other word of the parties until the lady was spied
on a southern-bound steamer. Now comes the
rumor of a bridegroom who, after an enthusiastic
send-off at the mining camp and a pre-nuptial
presentation, was forced to return brideless,
though not altogether profitless. He obtained a
clearer insight into the changeable ways of
women.
By the time this reaches you Toowoomba will
be recovering from its annual breakout on New
Year’s Eve. Picnic races, with a series of balls
thrown in, were arranged for the death-bed
revelries of 1910, and although last year the guests
had to be revived with ices and fans after each
dance, the present weather in the hills is not
nearly so Sheolistic. Half Brisbane departed to
assist in the tumult.
Max Maxwell is out recruiting, and has been
roping in quite a number of locals. Ronnie
Byram, a young man well possessed in the matter
of features, is giving up banking to take on grease
paint; while Miss Nellie Nicholson, Brisbane's
most Gibson-like girl, is also gaining a little
experience ere she goes south to besiege the stage-
door which carries the sign, “Comic opera.”
In addition to a plentiful supply of youth, a col-
lection of really beautiful top notes and a good
appearance, Miss Nicholson has a sizable bump of
intelligence which should secure her a thinking-
part in any show.
The flittings to the South still continue. Mrs.
William Collins and her bairns travel by the
Wyreema to Sydney en route for the Apple
Island ; and the Jas. Forsyths are off to the same
small, State in a week or so.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
The Bowman testimonial fund, which was
opened some months ago, closed on the last day
of 1910. A total of £970 was in the bag when a
count was made, and straggling subscriptions to
come in will probably enlarge the token to £lOOO,
which will give the sick Labor leader the long sea
voyage that his condition demands.
The Sapphiretown gem miners are feeling
good towards the Fisher Ministry. Some time
ago the local Progress Association wrote to Cus-
toms Tudor, asking for a duty on foreign-cut
sapphires, and upon doublets and bogus gems.
The Tariff Amending Act lifts doublets and Brum-
magem out of the free list, and puts them where
they will pay from 3G to 40 per cent., and the
question of a duty on cut Sapphires and zircons is to
be considered when the tariff is under general re-
vision.
Bananaland Assembly was overrun with
deputies last session. The illness of Speaker Bell
gave Chairman of Committees Armstrong the job
of Deputy-Speaker ; and Dave Bowman’s break-
down gave Lennon a busy time as Deputy-Leader
of the Labor party ; and Armstrong’s temporary
promotion to the Speakership led to Kidston’s
colleague (Grant) being made Deputy-Chairman.
Also, there was a Deputy-Premier. At any rate,
the policy which Kidston advocated was mainly
the policy which Kidston had denounced; whereas
it was the policy which Philp, of the gorgeous
deficits, has all along barracked for.
Three little years ago Premier Kidston
preached from a score of Queensland platforms
that Bananaland should go slow with the borrow-
ing business on account of the approach of the year
1914, when the State has to renew some 12 millions
of loans. To-day Premier Kidston is preaching
that the best way to prepare for the crisis is to
rush another £7,000,000 of borrowings on to the
market. Philp says that is just what he would do
if he were Premier, and nobody has any difficulty
in believing him. It is exactly the sort of finance
Philp was fed upon in Queensland’s drunken days,
when the fact that a man had borrowed £lOO,OOO
from the Q.N. Bank was reckoned the best of
reasons for lending him another £50,000. But if
Philp consulted the Q.N. Bank he might get a
hint that the scheme wasn’t a complete success so
far as either the borrowers or the lenders were
concerned. In fact, there are some people who
would go so far as to call it a rotten failure.

QONGO ROOFING is made for all
weathers. An unusually severe storm
won t hurt it. An extraordinary hot spell
won t hurt it. An extra long cold snap
won t hurt it. It will stand any kind of
exposure. The weather finds no way in
which to attack Congo Roofing.
can’t damage it, because there is
nothing in it that is affected by water
in any way. If Congo Roofing were put
at the bottom of a pond, the water would
never disintegrate it.
A IR can t damage it because there is
nothing in it that is volatile —nothing
that will evaporate. If Congo Roofing
were hung out like a flag for years, so that
air could get at both sides of it, it would
stay just as pliable and elastic as when new.
(P LI MATE can’t change it because there
is nothing in it that is affected by either
heat or cold. The heat on roofs is often
terrific, and in cold weather the snow thaws
and freezes there for weeks, but Congo has
never shown any effects of such exposure.
[T can t rot because there is nothing in it
that can decompose. It is because we
know the nature of the materials we use that
we venture to say that Congo Roofing is
practically indestructible.
pEOPLE who buy Congo Roofing for a
building once don’t buy any more in
after years for that building —because it is
there to stay , although the roof costs but
little. 1 hey do buy more for their other
roofs. Everyone who tries Congo is soon
a convert, and he becomes more enthusi-
astic the longer he waits for the roof to
wear out.
Jh VERY foot of Congo Roofing is care-
fully inspected as it comes off the
machine. No imperfect rolls are allowed
to leave the factory. Every pound of the
material that enters into the composition is
carefully tested before being used, and
strictly held to standard. Should it fail in
the slightest way, it is immediately discarded.
Congo Roofing is handsome in appearance,
being of a uniform slate-grey color.
ROOFING is put up in rolls
36 inches wide. Each roll contains
216 square feet, sufficient to cover 200
square feet and allow for a 2-inch lap. The
rolls are finished with an attractive wrapper
and label, and are capped with a special
metal cap to protect the ends of the roofing.
A steel rod runs through the centre to hold
the caps and cement securely in place. .
gUFFICIENT Congo cement, nails
and tin caps for applying are neatly
packed in the centre of each roll, so that
you pay nothing for extras.
CONGO ROOFING is easy to apply.
Skilled labor is not necessary. You
don t have to hire an expert to help. If
you can drive a nail you can do all the
work that is necessary.
CONGO DAMP COURSE
Congo Damp Course is an effective armor against underground dampness
and vapors. It should be used under 1 basement and cellar floors and
cemented tight with Congo Cement. The lime in mortar will not injure
it, the earth gases will not hurt it, water is powerless against it, white ants
find that it contains certain antiseptics which they cannot attack, vermin
and rodents likewise leave it strictly alone. Congo Damp Course costs so
little and is so easily put in place that you cannot afford to build without it.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no .US. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

THE CONDEMNED ROOKEKY.
’Twas a dilapidated rookery,
There was cobwebs on the wall,
The cracked ceiling indicated
’Twould collapse suddenly and fall ;
When condemned by the authorities
The old pensioner shed tears
For the crib had been his castle V
For over forty years ;
It had withstood the heat of summer’s
And the force of winter’s blast
And to the lonely veteran held
The memories of the past; y
In the grave yard lie his faithful wife
Who had been his guiding star,
They lived happily in the rookery
Till the poor soul crossed the bar.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.
TomF.: Haven’t got'time or space to make remarks
about every drawing submitted. Those that are ac-
cepted are paid for promptly. Sometimes work isn’t
good enough to accept, but is promising enough to en-
courage. If you haven’t received the encouraging word,
you’ll probably now suspect why A.M.S. : Drawing is
miles below acceptance level; and the joke, though better
than the drawing, isn’t hilarious Griswald : Better
keep to your grocery... .Stacky : Joke run dry, too.
“ Goanna ” : Before long there is going to
be a blithering and instructive contrast out Gren-
fell (N.S.W.) way, where that great philanthro-
pist (ride morning press), landra Greene, M.L.C.,
has so marvellously developed wheat-growing at
landra. Greenethorpe, as the railway station is
now called, is a choice specimen of a one-man-
owned wheat town, and the rows of tin houses
shimmering in the heat of the summer sun are
a torpid spectacle. They are all painted the
same ferocious color —brick red, all neatly built,
and all on the one monotonous design, the
same deadly plan. The spectacle doesn’t yell
very loudly about the profit of wheat-
growing from one standpoint; but this will
be happily' balanced by a palace Greene
is building for himself. Rumor puts its cost at
anything from £30,000 to £OO,OOO, so it will run to
more than a whole lot of the tin tenant-houses.
The house is being built of concrete, and is a
massive advertisement for share-farming—as far
as concerns Greene, the one man of the crowd who
doesn’t have to farm.
“Pick”: If the Murrumbidgee started
with a foundation of beer and trimmed up on rum,
it couldn’t follow a more staggering course.
Rising in the Kiandra (N.S.W.) country, its
waters first flow south-east and then south. By'
i Qoonia they are worming through the ranges
towards the east, until cannoned off northward
by the inflow of the Eumeralla and Bredbo.
Thence they sheer off hastily from the arid regions
of Yass-Ganberra, towards the north-west, until
the Y-shaped gap between Barren Jack and
Black Andrew pour them out into the west.
After that their general trend is south-west and
south, but at Jugiong they take a fantastic twist,
which enables the owner of the Redbank sheep-
walk to loop in 1600 acres with 220 panels of fenc-
ing, the river enclosing the rest. Nearly every
whaler met along the “ ’Bidgee ” has a kink m his
garret. This is brought on by constantly turning
around the bends in the river.
“Spring”: A still-lively N.S.W. Judge
on a country circuit once unexpectedly claimed a
night’s hospitality from an old pioneer who had
often begged him to drop in. The house was in
darkness when he drove up ; but a pipe gleam in
the verandah welcomed him in. Inside, the host
yelled for “ Jacky ” to bring in the lamp. An un-
canny head dived through a door and said there
was “No kerasin, boss.” “ Well, get the caudle,
yer black imp!” “No plurry candle, boss.
“Well get some blankv grease in a jam-.tin and
stick a piece of rag in it !” yelled the host and
was proceeding to describe to the visitor the thick-
ness of the average nigger’s skull when from the
darkness earcie the sepulchral announcement that
there was “no plurry grease.” “Then—damn
yer eyes—go down the paddock and make a
stringvbark torch, and let his Honor have his
supuer.” As the nig stole away to the place
where the candles ought to be, and selected a
stringy torch from a number of others, the ancient
pioneer was telling his Honor that the wicked
nigger must have eaten all the candles, and the
Judge professed to believe his statements.
Sydney, fair city of the South,
Set in Pacific Seas,
The ships glide through thy fine mouth,
Aided by a welcoming breeze,
Where Dunbar’s wreck lies bleak and cold
Around past the Sow and Pigs,
They slowly move with gentle ease
To where the Warships bale
In dirty gray, with various rigs,
Into the Farm Oove they squeeze,
While Manly’s lovely steamers rush
Up to the Circular Quay, *
And Mosman’s and North Sydney blush
For power and fuller speed to keep with these,
The City proud from here does brush
For miles around its leas,
While coves and bays marked out as such
Set up a roaring wheeze.

“The Old Soldier”: The two British
officers who were caught in the island of Borkum
trying to find out what the Germans were doing
in that quarter have got the trouble they seem to
have been looking for, and have been sentenced to
four years’ confinement in a fortress. This is a
punishment unknown to British law; so the
British daily paper lets its fancy go :
The prisoners are cheerful and are satisfied with the
result. They will probably be sent to the fortress of
Gatz [Glatz] in Silesia. They will be allowed all com-
forts, and the society of the Governor of the fortress and
the officers.
frlatz is an old place which has never seen a
esieger since the Seven Years War. It has
plenty of vaulted stone casements in its detached
bastions to accommodate prisoners, who pro-
bably include some officers of the German army
found guilty of serious breaches of discipline.
The prisoners will have sufficient food and clothes
and some books, and will be let out into the ditch
for exercise under the eye of a sentry quite often
enough to keep them in health. But they will
hardly get very much of the society of the officers
of the garrison. They will certainly see the field-
officer of the day regularly, and he will listen to
any complaints they have to make. They may
or may not be allowed to write letters; under
Ordinary circumstances, “detention in a for-
tress” in German is really solitary confinement,
the prisoner being shut off from the world. The
wonderful thing about the whole business is that
two really clever men could take so much trouble
with such a very small result. They could have gone
into many a shop in Hamburg or Bremen and
bought excellent German maps and charts that
would have told them all they wanted to know.
SEMPER EADEM.
PARSON: “ Well, Mr. Giles. How's your cold!" PARSON: “ And how is your wife? ”
GILES: “Oh, very obstinate, sir.” GILES: “Oh, about the same.”
They only made Tait of the Vic. Railways a
K. 8., after all. He should have been at least a
K.C.M.G. —a Knight Commander of the Mangled
and the Gored. But perhaps K.B. in his case
stood for Kristian Burial.
Sydney’s rockchopper—the man who belts
the holes for the sewer pipes through the sand-
stone that underlays the city—has once more
leaned on hiS job and demanded another shilling
a day. And probably he will get it. The work
is so health-breaking that, so far, every appeal for
concessions has been granted. The last award
dealing with the industry fixed the rate of wages
at 11s. 6d. a day of six hours. Now a demand for
12s. 6d. a day has been made to a contractor who
has a big job ip hand for the Water and Sewerage
Board, and the, choppers simply d—n the
award. The contractor is kicking like Henry,
sen.; but there are very few rockcboppers, and
they are as unanimous as if there were only one
rockchopper. So, looking at the position casually,
it would appear that if the extra shilling isn’t
paid, the extra sewer won’t be made. Which is
simplicity in its most simple form.
A Press Union has been formed in Mel-
bourne on a good basis. Editors Cunningham
(Argus) and Schuler (Age) have given it their
blessing. Cunningham, in fact, will address the
crowd."though he cannot join. Good Argus and
Age men are in it. The best feature of all is that
the few who have nothing to complain about are
throwing in to help the many who have. The
movement is to be Australian, with a special , eye
to the country. Another laudable thing is that
there is to be co-operation with compositors.
Fearful nonsense has been talked in the past about
a dividing line between writer and comp. The
most junior cadet at 20s. was supposed to be a cut
above the lino-operator, making his £5 or £6.
This was the English idea. Now the craft is
going to be Australian. So members of the union
in Melbourne go so far as to talk about printers
refusing to set non-union copy—in which case, of
course the journalist will have to stick to the
comp.’ The meanest Gradgrind going is a certain
type of country newspaper proprietor, and-he will
have to be attended to—in the interest-of the
decent ones as well as of the press workers. And,
after them, the union must have a slap at parsons
and pensioners, who come in with their stuff on
the cheap, and take bread out of the mouths of
poor journalists. The most offensive of all is the
Reverend Wowser. The job of keeping souls out
of Hell isn’t enough for him. He would run a'
hay and corn store only that Mrs. Grundy would
squeal. So he dabs away in a sneaking fashion at
what he calls journalism.
Sydney Morning Herald collects some
weird information at times. The latest (3/l/’ll)
was inclosed in a heavy-dumpling article dealing
with the list of New Year ‘‘honors Says
Gran’ma of the possibility of Prime Minister
Fisher being offered a title at Coronation time:—
The ultra-democrat can seldom resist a title even if it
is only a title made for export and placed m that
dilemma he is apt to play fast and loose with his political
fntp There are, of course, men whose hold on their
nartv is so great that they can dare to be a Knight
Commander. Sir Richard Seddon was a conspicuous
case in point. If anything, Sir Richard was even more
popular than plain “Dick.”
As a matter of fact, Richard Seddon steadfastly
refused to listen to any suggestion that he should
accept a title, although to that end oil was poured
over him repeatedly and pressure put upon him
continually. If the old democrat was ever Sir
Richard Seddon, it must have been since he passed
to the Golden Shore; and if the Herald has a
special representative in that part it has never
previously made as much use of the fact as it
might have done.
“N.” on the martinet methods of boss
Booth, of the “ Salvation ” Army :
Here’s the stupidest thing I have seen this week in
Melbourne, and that is saving' a great deal. Herbert
Booth, son of the Gen’ral, commanded in Australia for
five .years, and was about as good as any of the other
Panjandrums—Barker, Howard, Coombs, McKie and
Hay. Herbert built and opened two important estab-
lishments, the Training Home and Printing Office. I
heard him address the Harmy as “ Precious Comrades,”
and the Harmy Hallelooyered. His name was graven on
the memorial stones. Just now, under an imperative
ukase from headquarters—which I suppose means General
Booth —Herbert’s name has been chipped off those stones.
What a malignancy of Christian spirit! Still, it is re-
ciprocated by General Booth’s other son, Ballington, who
took legal steps in N’York to have the name of William
Booth removed from his little son. It recalls the Mel-
bourne parson who quarrelled with his patron after
whom he had named a little son who died. That parson
chopped the name off the tombstone.
After doing five years’ useful work, J. M.
Bell, the Canadian whom Seddon imported as
director of M.L. geological survey, hands in his
papers and hits the trail for his native snowfields.
On the journey, he calls in at Papua to examine
an alleged oil-bearing area for a Melbourne syndi-
cate. Bell is still only 33, but he has already
stacked up a tall reputation. He was only 22
when he led the Canadian Government’s expedi-
tion to Great Bear Lake; at 23 he was leader of
an important expedition for the Lake Superior
Power Co.; he was a Harvard Ph.D. at 26, and at
27 he secured the big appointment in the raeketty
islands, where the geologist has to drive his pick
in gently lest he should annoy a caged geyser or a
corked volcano. He takes with him his mining
geologist, Colin Fraser, a local product of the
Auckland University, who has been assisting
“ the boss” with considerable distinction.
A brother of Captain Creswell, director of
Australia’s baby navy, threatens' to be leader
of the very little Labor party in S’Africa’s new
Union Parliament.
The matter of erecting a tombstone over
the grave of the late “Johnny’’Sheridan in Waver-
ley Cemetery (N.S.W.) was discussed at a meet-
ing of the Melbourne Green Room Club last week.
Sheridan, thus far, has been a very neglected
identity since his death, it seems, and his grave is
a poverty-stricken allotment without a stone to
mark the fact that a famous theatrical character
was laid there. For the “Widow O’Brien” was
famous, and will be remembered in America,
Britain and Australia for a long time to come.
No other female impersonator was ever so con-
vincing, droll, instructive, or thoroughly clean in
his characterisation as the little Irish-Jewish
humorist who created his own joke in “Fun on
the Bristol.” The proposal to. spend a few pounds
on a tombstone is too modest. If the subscription
were extended to New York and London, a more
considerable sum might easily be raised.
Ernest Whitington, of the Adelaide
Register, tells this yarn : —He was with the S.
Aus. legislators on their recent Murray trip, and,
with only two bathrooms among 50 passengers on
board the steamer, was induced by Jackson,
Chairman of Committees, to hang on behind the
dingey that was being towed," and take his wet-
ting that way. The exercise, at 10 miles an hour,
was refreshing, but the pace took his pyjamas off.
They were recovered and thrown into the boat,
and "just, then the procession reached Renmark,
where the youth and beauty of the place were
standing on the wharf. ' Whitington dared not
climb out; in fact, he dared not do anything bub
drift. So, preferring death to dishonor, he drifted
down on to a steamer, got inside its paddle-box,
and took'a sporting risk of its revolving, while he
resumed the one garment that civilisation de-
mands as indispensable.
From “ Boswell ” :
“I move that the voice of the people be no longer
heard.” That appears to me the sum and substance of
the “arguments” adduced by Wade and co., in their
opposition to the McGowen Government’s Electoral Bill.
They clawed at the Bill here, and they scratched at it
there, and they moaned long and piteously because cer-
tain “Liberal” seats would be endangered were the
franchise granted to all the free people living therein. It
seems to them shocking that electorates hitherto re-
garded as pocket boroughs should be exposed to the fire
of the enemy. The previously unheard of provision
whereby nomadic workers may enter their protest
against Toryism once in three years doesn’t appeal to
Wade, Wood, or Waddell either. And yet, as all three have
reDeatedly announced that they are the only true friends
the working-man has. their fear of his vote is remark-
able. The roaring Wade and the yapping Wood them-
selves have such a burning desire to be heard, and are so
frantic when gagged, that their sinister attempts to gag
others had better be set down to a hallucination that
they constitute the whole State, and that nobody else
matters.
The Tory press fusses considerably over
the fact that Rear-Admiral Bridges has spoken
bitterly of the Federal progressive land tax, and
has declared that it will “warn off the would-be
settler.” This Bridges is a Tory, aged 67. He left
the navy and became a big landlord in Victoria
23 years ago, and proceeded to warn off the
would-be settler ; and he has made much money
here, and has sat long hours in the Melbourne
Club casting a disparaging eye on the country
which has treated him a great deal too well.
Bridges, in fact, is not a bluff British tar these
days, nor is he an outsider taking an unbiassed
view of Australian legislation. He is just an
infuriated old local landlord, who hates the idea
of having to make a little bit of room for other
members of the human race, _ and if he didn t
object to the Federal land tax it would be a very
poor sort of tax.
“ 0.K.” supplements a send-off :
A par. in a recent Bulletin informed me that W. H.
Williams, oldest of Melbourne printers and about the
voungest-looking man for his age that ever talked
familiarly about the Roaring ’so’s in Victoria, was lately
numbered with the fallen. Rest his soul ! I knew him
well of yore, and edited one of his weekly publications
what time it was in the hands of trustees, W.H.W. being
a printer who knew his business, but preferred to be
unbusinesslike in his methods. At that time and ever
afterwards I wasted breath in exhorting him to write up
his reminiscences—commercial, musical, literary and
theatrical. He never could be persuaded to undertake
the task, or to believe that he had—what he really
did have—a light and ready journalistic hand
for it. Williams, in his young days, was a colleague
of G. V. Brooke— i.e., he warbled between the acts of
Brooke's show at the theatre in Queen-street, Melbourne.
He had raised his tenor voice in public ere the veteran
R. S. Sinythe came to Melbourne as a youthful journalist,
and could talk more interestingly of old musical identities
and their art, in all its bearings, than any other man I ever
met. But what I chiefly remember him by was his scorn-
ful criticism of newspaper vaporings re the dangers
arising from the burial of the dead in the vicinity of the
living. W. H. Williamscame of very tough stock, and he
told me that his aged parents had lived for about half a
century in a house at Chester (Eng.), located at the foot
of a hill crowned with an ancient cemetery. The house
had a large cellar, and into this cellar the gravy
from the crowded cemetery had been draining for
all those 50 years that the old folks lived, hale and
hearty, in the premises above.
“H.H.”: For a pointlessness which
amounts to the incurable ward in the lunatic
asylum, and a heaviness compared with which an
elephant ranks as a balloon, I recommend the
following anecdote, dug out of a Sydney snips
weekly:—
The following interesting incident took place one day
towards the end of last month on the public road at Bal-
moral.
While the King, accompanied by one or two members
of the royal household, was returning to the castle on
foot from a deer-stalking expedition, the party met two
small boys, between five and six years of age, with their
schoolbags on their backs returning home from school.
His Majesty' stopped the lads, who respectfully raised
their baps, and in his well-known kindly manner in-
quired whether they were on their way home from school,
to which they replied in the affirmative.
The King next asked if they could read, and being
eagerly assured that they could do so, they were asked in
turn to read a small portion, which they did, selecting a
passage which they had learned thoroughly at school, and
knew almost by heart. His Majesty highly commended
the lads on their accomplishments, remarking that he
doubted if his own boys could have given as good a read-
ing at their age.
The King then asked the hour they had to be at school
in the morning, and received the reply, “ Nine o’clock,
sir.” The next question was, “ When do you get home f ”
to which one of the boys replied, with amazement written
on his face, “We’re gaun hameth’noo.” His Majesty
patted both lads on the head, and departed, smiling at
the lad’s reply.
Observe the blighted imbecility attributed to the
monarch. Any ordinary person meeting two
small schoolboys just after school hours would
assume that they were going home from school.
His Majesty made dead sure by inquiring. And
then he asked what time they came home from
school instead of recklessly assuming that a small
boy who is found going home from school at, say,
4 p.mi, probably goes home from school at 4 p.m.
Observe, too, the royal sense of humor ; the fact
that the small boys were going home filled him
with mirth. . And consider his conscientiousness ;
he patted them both on the head. It was lucky
there weren’t 19 of them, else the job would have
looked wooden and mechanical. Even as it was,
the spectacle of one boy being patted while the
other one waited to be patted, and then the one
who had been patted standing at attention while
his mate was put through the process couldn’t
have been inspiriting.
A small curse to the extent of £2,107,000
came home to roost in Victoria on New Year’s
Day, and the accommodation provided for the
fowl was just what might have been expected.
The Weary Government cordially invited the old
creditors to accept another IOU; but the total
amount thus renewed was only £538,352, or little
more than one-fourth of the lot. The balance has
to be provided for somehow. As a stoppage
Weary has got £500,000 from the Savings
Bank, and a London bank will give thg State a
temporary overdraft for the rest ; but Ihis only
means pushing the trouble a few weeks into the
future. Weary and Watt have therefore two
struggles ahead of them —one to extract from a
new Cohen enough to pay the bill that the old
Cohen wouldn’t renew, and the other to extract
the £2,000,000 which the Government says it
is going to play up in railways, etc. For the
former, the Government proposes to offer a
foreign Cohen its IOU; for the latter, it pro-
poses that the State shall offer itself its own
lOU.

BOONHRA
Acres.
Queensland’s Greatest Agricultural Proposition,
OITUATED in the famous Kingaroy District, at Goomerri Railway Station, right on the Kilkivan Railway Line,
eight and a half hours rail from Brisbane.
Described as a plateau or steppe, on the coastal side of the Main Coast Range, about 60 miles from the sea,
807 feet above sea level, with a delightful climate, well sheltered in winter, yet cool and breezy in summer.
Excellently watered by numerous ever running creeks and large lagoons. Level roads throughout. Free from
pests. Proved drought proof. Ample rainfall of 35*14 inches. The home of the rich Queensland blue grass. Nearly
43% of first-class agricultural land and less than 1% of inferior land, certified by a Government expert. The best
potato, onion, and lucerne soil in Queensland.
Large new hotel on the estate. Butter factory in course of erection. Reserves for school, church, and show-
grounds. Motor cars meet every train for inspection. Fares paid from Brisbane to the Estate when inspecting and
when attending the sale, if you purchase.
Subdivided into 172 Farms from 32 to 423 acres .
EXPERT OPINIONS
MR. W. O. LAMB, who was one o! the Agricultural Pioneers of the
Darling Downs, where he has been farming for 48 years, says:—“The average
quality is exceptionally good. . . . The soil is specially adapted for producing
large crops of lucerne and potatoes. . . • I can conscientiously recommend this
country, &c.”
MR. DAVID McCULLOCH of “ Roseneath,” Parkes,
N.S. W a prominent agriculturist, who will harvest 4,000 acres of wheat this
season, says“ Kidges and flats alike will compare favourably with the richest lands
of Victoria and N.S.W., and the potentialities for dairying and lamb raising are
practically unlimited. 1 ’
MR. PHIL. McGRATH, Snr„ who has been 44 years lucerne farming
in Laidley, says :— '* I consider the Boonara Flats are exactly similar lucerne soil
, , The ridges are exceptionally good soil.”
MR. H. RAFF , Surveyor, with 30 years experience of
Queensland, says:—“l have subdivided many large estates in Southern, Western,
and Northern Queensland . Boonara leads them all. Its physical features
combine to make it the finest farming and dairying proposition I have ever seen.”
MR. J. W. WALDRON, who has had considerable farming experience
on the Tweed liiver and elsewhere, says :—“ So adaptable for farms . . . plenty
of permanent water , . . rich alluvial deep soil flats for lucerne, potatoes, etc.”
