The Perfect Sphere.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f sea corrodes the continents, and all
I Their beetling cliffs that front it as a wall,
Till, undermined, the stern rock-masses
fall.
The serried hills, by dint of wind and rains,
Send down eternal tribute to the plains
Until the earth rotundity regains.
Some huge upheaval lifts the rugged range,
Piling the rocks in forms new, grand and strange;
And straightway start the elements of change.
For though the stubborn soul of grandeur clings
To fight the equilibrium of things,
Force coped with forqe a general balance brings.
Down to the caverns o{ the utmost sea
The mountains trend, with each new drift set
free,
To fill the gap and close the cavity.
The Andes, Alps and Himalayas soar
Into the clouds ; less lofty than of yore
They slowly sink, and shall uprise no more.
For, in the misty years beyond our ken,
When we have long been cleared away, we men,
The earth shall take her perfect shape again !
VICTORIA.
C.G.A. COLLES.
Her Lost Love: A Romance of
Waddy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rnHERESA MEED was the embodiment
of Long Suffering. Waddy accepted her
as such. She was all the township com-
prehended of romance, too. And Theresa “ kep’
store” on Old Road, which was the acknowledged
chief thoroughfare of Waddy till the new pine
school and the tin post-office added so much to
the pretensions of Hoskins’s Way. Later, as the
result of a phenomenal burst of public enterprise,
and in the *hope of booming real estate and add-
ing to the attractiveness of Waddy as a health
resort for “ asthmatics,” a kerosene lamp was put
up opposite the P. 0., and Hoskins’s Way became
Regent-street.
After that Old Road slunk into the background.
The stock of kerosene gave out in a month, and
the public lamp never flickered again, but the
boys continued to gather about the post, and the
men to hold their nightly conversazione along
the lower rail of Flude’s fence, where the light of
other nights had fallen. Even the goats that
had once scratched themselves on projections and
angles of Old Road, followed the popular trend,
and now scraped their spare ribs against the
palings and door-posts of Regent-street.
This did not affect Theresa Meed. Nothing
affected Miss Meed. She was a thin, dark, hand-
some woman of 45, with an ivory-white face that
showed neither line nor stain, a vivid arch of
black brows, and two cold, ebon eyes. She wore
nothing but black, and that was neat and flawless.
Even the dust respected Miss Meed. Her wealth
of dark hair, parted to the level of her eyes and
carried into a heavy roll behind her ears, added
to her impressive appearance. She was easily the
most imposing thing in Waddy.
It was generally agreed that Theresa Meed’s
wrongs made her sacred, and her great wrong was
common property. There was not a child over
six in the township and district who could not tell
Miss Meed’s lamentable story. Every newcomer
had to hear that affecting narrative.
„ Twenty-three years ago Don Lovell came to
Waddy. He was only a stoker at the Long Shot
mine ; but he was young and good-loOking, and
when off duty he wore a better suit than Tracker,
the manager. Don was too partial to billiards, he
smoked cigars, and his whisky bias was quite pro-
nounced ; so Waddy credited him with a good old
English family and better days. He soon dis-
covered Theresa Meed, then a girl of 22, whose
curious attractions were rather over the heads of
philandering natives.
Even at that early age Miss Meed “ kep’ store,”
as her mother had done up to within six months
previously. Now, Theresa was an orphan, and
alone in the world. Like her mother, she made
no friends. She was very reserved, but Don
Lovell didn’t seem to notice it. He went often
to the store, and he chatted gaily and banteringly
at Theresa, precisely as if she had been an ordi-
nary, light-hearted human animal.
Eventually he beat down her restraint and con-
quered her reticence. They were seen sauntering
together on Cemetery Hill in the evenings, and
on Sundays they walked to the Springs and ate
fruit in Capel’s garden.
Lovell worked a wonderful change in Theresa
Meed. She became suffused with a hectic happi-
ness. It was like a fever that burnt red in her
cheeks, and glowed in her splendid eyes. Don
alone seemed regardless of this great transforma-
tion. He was careless and easy always; all
things rested lightly on his shoulders.
Waddy quite made up its mind that they would
marry, and one evening, when Lovell had been
about seven months in the township, Cameron
asked him, airily : “When’s it to be, Don ?”
“When’s which to be what?”
“When do you and the grocery consolidate?”
Lovell arose, yawning. “We don’t,” he said.
“I’m not of a marrying stock.”
“Why, man, she’s half daft about you. I
thought you’d just swoop at the chance.”
“I brought nothing to Waddy. I intend
taking nothing away.”
Cameron was amazed. “ Don’t vou count on
having easy diggin’ with that girl,” he said
“She’ll make a runnin’ fight of it.”
“I’m not such a fool as to stay and debate the
matter at length, Cameron. On a nice night for
walking I’ll pick up my heels and get over the
hill.
Lovell did not give anyone a chance to spoil his
programme. On the following night he went
quietly “ over the hill.” There were reasons for
an unostentatious departure quite apart from
Iheresa. Don had taken “the bankrupt’s track.”
He owed everybody money, more especially
Hamm the publican, and Franki of the Waddv
Temperance Boarding House. These two made
an effort to locate their missing friend but the
search was fruitless. Don Lovell had left not
even a footprint, and after two days it was
accepted its definite that lie would not materialise
again in the vicinity of Waddy.
On the evening of the second day, Theresa
Meed appeared at Tracker’s gate while the mana-
ger was working in his garden. She was even
paler than usual, and spoke with marked effort.
“Mr. Tracker, I want to know about Mr.
Lovell,” she said. “ Has anything happened ? ”
“He’s gone off, my girl,” replied Tracker,
bluntly.
Theresa’s two white hands gripped the pal-
ings. “ But only for a little while ? ” she said.
The manager shook his head. “ The fellow’s a
bit of a scamp, I’m thinkin’, Miss. He’ll not
show his nose in Waddy again.”
For fully a minute Theresa Meed stood there,
voiceless, clinging to Tracker’s gate. When she
spoke again, her tone was quiet and assured.
“You are mistaken, Mr. Tracker,” she said.
“ He will be seen here again.”
That was always Theresa’s answer if anyone
spoke to her of Lovell. Her confidence struck
the people as pitiful, and Theresa became a
heroine of romance.
There was a little garden before the side en-
trance of Miss Meed’s, home. The gate of this
Theresa opened wide, fastening it back to the
fence with a chain and padlock. She also opened
the side door, and secured it so with a cleat nailed
along the floor. For over 20 years neither gate
nor door was ever closed.
This action was quite in consonance with public
sentiment. Waddy gave it a romantic interpre-
tation. The open door was a pathetic appeal to
the recreant lover, a touching assurance that,
come what might, in her heart there was for him
an imperishable welcome.
Theresa went quietly about her duties, a strik-
ing figure, gentle but tragical. Her faith never
faltered and her words never changed. A sort of
pitiful reverence enveloped her. She was the un-
complaining victim, the Faithful Soul, a living Re-
proach, a marble monument reminding all of the
perfidy of man. Every Sunday night, wet or
fine, still or stormy, she walked to the summit of
Cemetery Hill, and stood by the hump-backed
gum that sprawled its gnarled limbs along the
ground, where she had sat with Don in those
brief hours of happiness.
The people of Waddy said that Theresa Meed
always looked past them--beyond them. Her
eyes were always seeking her Lost Love. Their
interest was not wholly artistic. It was paternal
and benevolent. Waddy stood about the Forsaken
One, an unwinking guard.
When Spicer set up his Universal Supply Store
in Regent-street he sold one pound of anti-friction
and three candles in three weeks. Then he struck
his flag and put up the shutters. When Tanner
got into the habit of hanging about Theresa’s
store, three citizens of W addy took him aside and
expostulated with him. He was advised to desist.
He ignored the admonition, and was nearly suffo-
cated in the slurry from the Long Shot puddlers.
Theresa had another suitor, “Curly” Oates.
For a year or so “Curly ” was content with mute
adoration. Then one day he spoke to her follow-
ing her in the street.
“Say, Treser, won’t yeh marry us?” pleaded
Oates.
Theresa Meed was like a pale statue, cold, deaf,
blind.
“ Oh, go on, yeh might’s well. I’ll make yeh a
good ’usban’, ’selp me I will,” persisted Curly.
“ I bin sober a year.”
Theresa walked on, her eyes fixed straight
ahead, her face rigid.
“I’m that fond ov yeh, Treser, I can’t eat,”
pleaded the tremulous suitor. “I got a stiddy
job. I’ll do anythin’ if ye’ll on’y have us.”
He followed her to her own door, appealing
piteously ; but she never turned, she gave no sign
of hearing, no hint that she was aware of her
suitor’s existence.
That action did “Curly” a lot of harm. It
was as if he had tried to break an idol or besmirch
some cherished tradition. He was severely repri-
manded. He met expostulations at every turn.
Eventually, he fled the township like a badgered
malefactor.
Later, a little girl told a strange story about
creeping unheard into Theresa’s house, and watch-
ing the heroine before a mirror, making her face
white with mysterious chemicals, reddening her
lips with rouge, and darkening and pencilling the
curious arch of black brows. No one believed her
tale. It only earned-the little girl a whipping,
and. was regarded as a quaint outbreak of original
sin.
Then came the irresistible railroad, harshly
materialistic, blundering over sacred things.
Theresa’s store must go. The hill on which it
stood was to be cut through. Miss Meed resisted
stonily, but pathos was vain against the machinery
of progress. The store was lifted on a j inker,
and shifted into Regent-street. Theresa was
compensated, and iron utilitarianism prepared
to sweep away the little garden, and the small
emblems beloved of a stricken soul.
Miss Meed could not content herself after the
change. She neglected her business, and drifted
uneasily about the township. She was often seen
standing in her dismantled garden on the Old
Road. Waddy understood, and was full of sym-
pathy. It even cried out against the railroad that
was to bring Prosperity along by main strength.
Never had Waddy been so stirred as on the morn-
ing of the discovery of Theresa Meed’s disappear-
ance. She had gone in the night. There was
little stock left in the store, her rooms were as
usual, but Theresa was nowhere to be seen.
Public opinion was strongly in favor of suicide.
The old shafts were searched ; the deep holes
along the creek were dragged ; and Tracker—an
old man now—went carefully over the Long
Shot dam with a garden rake. Returns were nib
The efforts of the police to trace Theresa by
description were not more successful. No pallid,
black-haired, statuesque woman had been seen
any where. Waddy abandoned the idea of suicide,
and said that Theresa had gone out into the wide
world seeking her lost love.
Five months later the cutting through Bald
Hill was nearly completed, and had encroached on
Theresa’s garden. It was on a Monday, at noon.
The navvies had put a big blast into the reef be-
fore going to crib, and suddenly and mysteriously
Waddy became conscious that something was
wrong. The people poured from the houses.
Instinctively they ran to the cutting.
The blast had torn out tons of yellow reef,
leaving a clean face above, and about two feet
below the subsoil a niche was disclosed in which
reposed a human skeleton. The bones were
intact. Standing out from the ribs at the back
was the buckhorn handle of a large knife, the
long blade of which, now a mere wisp of rust,
passed through to the breast-bone.
Plainly, Don Lovell had called upon Theresa
and made known his intentions, for the remains
of a small parcel containing identifiable articles
lay with his bones.
The coroner’s theory was that Theresa Meed
had known all along the whereabouts of her lost
love. He lay in the grave she had dug for him
under her bed, with the long bacon knife she
wielded so cleverly fast in his heart. The jury
returned a verdict in accordance, with that idea.
EDWARD DYSON.

Matchless
On the West Coast of
Tasmania.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SAND and strips of made road, and, in
the infrequent forest belts, a blustrous wind
all day amid the tall trees. The Stanley
Road Board will kill a few yet.
White sand, yellow clay, black peat, and water
like strong cold tea. Pink and red heaths and a
soil too poor for blackberries, though the pest
throws its spiky wires over the roadsides where
the land is fat.' Then the button-grass, and
heaths and the bells of the Blandfordia growing
sheltered under dwarfed gums; squdgy, boggy
plains for a dozen miles; then crooked eucalypti
and the three-petalled iris starring the ground,
showing the true fleur-de-lis of Florence, and
giving these sunny glades the semblance of a
Tuscan lawn. Flowering desert; black pools in
the sand; more spewy button-grass, gushing to
the hoof-beat —a vegetable spring mattress, on
pressure squirting water through every hole in the
wires, and the liquid covered by a beauty of pink
and white and lilac—every inch a flower.
At last, the sea; high ground and sudden little
valleys, yellow sea sand drifted in tongues over
the white sand of the land. The sea sand, very
fine of grain, wind-combed into fantastic shapes
as mother combs little Willie’s front curls. Out-
crops of limestone, succeeded by barren quartz-
ites, lichen-spotted.and pockmarked with fungus;
small patches of English grass ; little invasions
of clover; and then the house at the Arthur River
where lives the ferryman—beg pardon, the cap-
tain of the punt.
At the ferry the river is very deep, and above
it is navigable for 20 miles. It is 150yds. wide,
and its entrance is incurably rockbound, sand-
bound and timber-bound. The flowers and ti-tree
and stunted gums—as of Sandringham—grow
here to the sea ; and there is added to them the
buzzy—a vegetable porcupine which sticks to the
traveller so that he may carry it further on and
distribute the seed where it isn’t wanted.
Once over the river, and there is the tine gallop-
ing ground of the beach, level as a billiard-
table, resilient as rubber. The redbills and sea
snipe and gulls and mollyhawks start from the
horse’s feet. Great fantastic sea-growths and
drowned kelp, torn from the sea depths and cast
ashore, stripe the sand. The bull-kelp smells
pleasantly, when it is new; but when old,
it is cast high on the beach and fly-blown, and
sea birds feast on the rottenness and the filthy
insect life and the taint of iodine. Above the
fantastic sand-drifts is the short sweet grass sought
by the coast cattle ; flowers grow under the bushes
of the little white currant which is the food of the
bronzewing pigeon ; the horses revel in the joy of
movement —the green seas steeplechasing over the
brown serrated rocks which wrecked the barque
Rebecca and her immigrants 50 years ago; the
white quietness of the drifted sand ; and beyond
the breakers the depths, moaning to a lashing wind,
that fresh gale which seems to blow for ever from
Cape Horn. Whalebaek rocks taking the steeple-
chasing seas in sullenness; sierras cutting the
leaping water with a scream, and in these rocks
the nests of the Cape pigeon. There are quick-
sands on these open sea-beaches at the mouth of
every creek and river, but safety for horse and
man where the fresh water meets the salt. In
the freak of a ribbon of kelp lying in a beach pool
and blown into sinuous movement by the gale,
you may see a possible origin of the probable
myth of the sea serpent. Echelons of the sea
besiege the fresh water of the river ; all is war
here, sand, wind, sea, bird, fly, maggot, redbill,
gull and sea pigeon. Ducks from Australia come
here by the'thousand in midsummer to feed on
the corruption of the beach and the rotting bull-
kelp ; arriving to the day, because it is a fashion-
able crush for ducks. We might read, if we
could, in the Daily Quack some such Society note
as this:
Lord and Lady Mallard, the Hon. Miss Duck and
Captain Teal have gone to the West Coast for the maggot
season.
The red-billed oyster-catcher dresses like a waiter
—white shirt and tail coat, all complete—and
walks flat-footed, as if calling for the last plate of
meat at midnight. The waiter’s wife meantime
tries to attract us from her ndst, flying over the
little sandlark, who totters at great speed on his
apparently unreliable legs.
As" we galloped, a dog with a temperament
which never learns by experience, and who had,
therefore, to trail his neckchain as a drag, gal-
loped ahead ; but the journey was not enough,
for he had a mind. To employ that mind on the
15-mile run he carried heavy sticks and roots in
his mouth, planting his luggage as he tired of it
under a bush, that he might play with it again on
the return journey. And the sun by some miracle
came out, and the gallop was good, and the
gale blew enough to make one enjoy the struggle
with it.
But there are times, on these wide beaches, when
travelling is no pleasure. At high tides, with a
bad sea and heavy west weather, the great logs
that strew the beaches go afloat, and when a sea
breaks, horse and rider are hidden in foam—safe if
the horse stands still, but often the frightened
animal plunges, gets in amongst the floating logs
and is carried to sea. Many cattle were so lost on
this stock route to Zeehan. The noise bewilders
them ; they become dizzy from long looking at
the retreating wave and try to follow it. The
rider, if lie be nervous, has his eyes on a fixed
object—tree or rock or some other immutable
thing—and keeps his head that way. At river
entrances, like that of the Pieman, there are very
big quicksands to avoid, and the drover sends his
cattle into the salt water; and then comes the
danger of the sea. Suds from the tortured foain
in the guts of the rocks pile up 15ft. or 16ft. high,
and smother the living thing that goes into it. At
some big rivers a lane of floating pine stakes, held
by wires, keeps the cattle from ringing; and the
passage is assisted by drovers in boats, and by the
dogs that jump on the backs of the swimming
beasts, and nip them to mad effort. And after
the rivers and the beach there is the button-grass
peat for the summer road, sand for the winter;
and an energetic man driving half a dozen wild
“ fats ” through the mud and the stinging sleet and
the black water to Balfour, to sell then at sd. or
6d. a pound. , . , ,
Whale Head is a little toy of a harbor, a boy-
size haven, but a very welcome side-track in this
mad coast. The sun, after being invisible for
nearly all dav, came out to set like a Masonic
lodge certificate. There were broad sun rays, like
those used by German lithographers, and broken
by a black cloud with a white circle and a red
speck looking out from the rays like an eye; and
to complete the chromo, a gull flying to the sun
set as understudy for Noah’s dove, but going to
slav fish and crying raucously as he flew.
The gale got its second wind at nightfall and
blew all our whiskers off; and we sat close to a
big fire on that bitter summer night, and shivered
in the genial zephyr whenever we moved. This
polar-bear weather is valueless at all times and
ridiculous in December. The ideal weather for
the world is an early spring morning in Hell.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Book.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BEFORE me gleams a Volume, rare,
And radiant to behold ;
With picture-poems painted, fair,
In lines of green and gold.
That Great Raphael, who moulds the flow’r
And stains the sunset skies
Its author is. No critic dour
His workmanship decries.
Homeric is his theme, and this
No halting rhythm mars—
What bard-song ever soared like His
Whose harp-strings reach the stars?
With Art sublime the Great Book glows ;
And magic minstrelsies;
And Music from its pages flows,
In chords that never cease.
On each fresh page this endless tome
Some new desire doth hold ;
Its readers may for ever roam
By wonder-ways untold.
Aye, he who cares, may turn at will
Rare treasure-leaves, to learn
Of cool cloud shadows o’er the hill
Or sunlight on the fern.
Slow may he follow —all his soul
A-thrill with long delight—
The changing Seasons, as they roll
The path of Day and Night.
His eye may wander in the Spring
O’er dewy lands, a-sheen ;
Where fairy martins sweeping wing
Across the paddocks green ;
Or scarlet lories, in their flight
Among the wattles, fold
Their turquoise wings ; to drop like bright,
Red rubies cast on gold.
Oh ! he may gloat the hillsides clear,
That em’rald with the vine ;
The waving wlieatfields just in ear,
The silken-coated kine.
When from her green the briar breaks,
A sweet rose-sister shy,
Or proud, the lordly gymea shakes
His crimson banners high.
A serial of the seasons, he
Who loves the Book may read—
The tale of summer, joyous see
Imprinted on the mead ;
In yellow heads of ripened wheat,
In purple clusters, hung ;
In orchards, breathing forth the sweet
Of ripened fruit downflung.
Beneath the ti-tree down the creeks
Is writ a chapter cool,
Wherein the tongue of Nature speaks
From shaded reach and pool.
Brown Autumn, like a dairy lass
With rain-wet cheeks of health,
A rosy gleaner, too, will pass,
Her apron full of wealth.
Their sloughed, curled bark, the silver trees,
From shining trunks unswathe,
Like nymphs by singing, summer seas
Disrobing ere they bathe.
Now lines the dasyure lean his lair ;
Now moults the wild black swan ;
The shifting snipe are otherwhere;
The covey quail are gone.
New wine and must, and yellow gourd ;
Fat sacks of spilling grain,
In rick and loft and cellar stored,
Bespeak the garnered gain.
So Winter steals, with soft warm rains,
To soak the canefields all;
And Southern snow, that deep remains
Along the ranges tall.
So Winter, in her fur-lined gown
And hood and muff of grey,
Goes tripping o’er the farmlands brown,
Frost-jewelled, on her way.
So, from this Book the Bards-to-Be,
The Painters yet unborn,
Their songs will glean in ecstasy :
Their pictures clothe with Morn.
The Shades of those, in Austral rhyme,
Who wrote, as pioneers,
Will surely hail that full, sublime,
Rich culture of the years.
When I have turned the Puzzle Key,
That opes the Low Black Door ;
And from this human entity
Go forth, to sing no more,
On some Australian hill, that greens
With bourgeonage of grass;
Where down the Morning’s cool demesnes
The glowing day-winds pass;
Low lay me down. Nor wet in grief
The Old Earth-Book with tears;
Remembering that line and leaf
Were mine through many years.
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.

IHE Australian thinks in Continents, because
he is the only man who has a Continent
to think in.” THE LONE HAND is the only
Magazine that caters for a Continent.
MEN WILL FEEL YOUNG
for just as long as their digestive organs continue strong and
active. When the stomach grows weak, the liver sluggish,
the bowels constipated and the kidneys insufficiently active,
age creeps on apace. There is truth in the saying that a man
i§ as old as he feels, and the woman is as old as she looks.
Some men at sixty are almost boyish in the exuberance of
their vitality. Others at forty show all the signs of being
“played out”—appear, in fact, prematurely old. Men of all
